THE RED PAGE.
Primitive Arts and Crafts.
AWAY up in the north of Queensland, be-
yond where the sugar-cane and the
Kanaka goes, Dr. Roth, the Northern
Protector of Aborigines, carries on his
investigations into the habits and customs of the
Australian natives—a work which will be more
welcomed and better thought of by posterity than
by us. What he is doing now he has begun to
snow us in the issue of bulletins fpom his depart-
ment, issued under instructions from Home Secre-
tary Foxton.
Dr. Roth found much to interest him in the
manufacture by the blacks of string and other
forms of strand, and their concomitants, basketry,
woven bag, and net-work. Away back “in the
dim red dawn of man ” some ingenious paleolithic
individual discovered a use for a little piece of
string, or its equivalent. He found perhaps that
a wedge of stone tied on to a 2ft. handle was far
more efficacious in argument than a sharpened
flint in the hand, and maybe his wife wished to
ensure the presence in the cave of a plump young
ichthyosaurus when the time came to cook dinner.
When string was discovered it became indispens-
able, and soon fancy-goods’ manufacturers of
those days began to make a profit on decorated
dilly bags and other feminine fripperies. Up
on the Bloomfield and Batavia rivers—anywhere
north of Cooktown, in fact—Australian
aborigines are still engaged in primitive
string-making, and some of their methods, as
described by Dr. Roth, are instructive. The
blacks have various sources of supply. They draw
on animal products in the shape of human,
’possum and kangaroo hair, and kangaroo-tail,
emu-leg and snake-neck tendons. In weaving hair
into twine the womeir-folk are particularly adept,
and, according to Dr. Roth, spin out strand after
strand of it with no other help than a hooked
stick and their own perseverance. But the chief
source of raw material is the vegetable world.
Fibre twine is prepared by chewing, soaking and
drying, and the required lengths are split off
with a mussel-shell or a finger-nail. These fibre-
shreds are manufactured into twine by first of all
rollingthenifirmandflatintoaspiralform, and then,
after doubling, this spiral is again rolled, under
considerable pressure, between the palm and the
thigh of the operator. All fibre twines are thus
made of two plies, and some of them are of great
strength. The twine is used for all sorts of pur-
poses, such as plait-work (which is the amqsemenj
or the employment of the boys and tlie girls),
chain-work (with which the men and women put
themselves into mourning by wearing it on their
shoulders and arms), fringing-out waist T belts, and
so on. Dr. Roth has, in his examination of the
various netting-knots, found close analogy between
these efforts of primitive man and the stitches of
modern lace-work. A dilly-bag made by the un-
prepossessing gin developes a striking likeness in
its stitch to a specimen of Point-de-Bruxelles lace
or to Point-de-tulle. The black changes the
fashions of his nets and bags, and makes them
with simple loops, loops and twists, hour glass or
double loop, chain twist, and other intricate
devices, according to his necessities or his artistic
taste.
Tire Koko-Yirnidir language is the subject of
the second of Dr. Roth’s bulletins. With the
assistance of two German Lutheran missionaries,
Messrs. Schwartz and Poland, of the" Lake Bed-
ford station, Dr. Roth has elaborated a language, '
which to most, of us is a series of grunts and gut-
terals, into a highly inflected and grammatical
affair. This Koko-Yimidir language is, curiously
enough, the very one of which Captain Cook made
a vocabulary in 1770, and that fact makes the
present-day study all the more attractive. To
find that the aborigine is not content with simple
sentences, present tenses, and nominative cases,
but instead indulges in all sorts and conditions
of compound words and phrases, in verbs that
possess all the peculiarities of imperfect, irre-
gular, active, passive, transitive, and in-
transitive forms; of nouns that have several
derivatives attached to them, that boast
of gender, number and case ; of pronouns
which are personal and relative, and have all
three cases ; of adjectives, adverbs, conjunctions,
and the rest of the parts of speech—all this is suffi-
ciently startling. When one remembers that
these men are, in point of physical and intellectual
development, but little removed from those who
gambolled with the various 'strange beasts of the
first eras, and that through all the ages their lan-
guage has been retained purely orally, there is
much to give us pause. And the ingeniousness of
it all! Set aside the mere grammar, the different
forms of sentences necessary to express different
notions, the multiple inflections each denoting a
different idea, all the innumerable turns and twists
given to what was perforce a limited vocabulary
in order to obtain as much variety as possible— set
these aside as food for the philologist merely, and
much remains for the popular fancy to pick at.
Dr. Roth tramples out the theories about kanga-
roos by the assertion that the “natives whom
Cook interrogated on the subject still allude to
that animal as ‘ gangaru ’ ” Compare some of
the. Yimidirian substantives used to denote attri-
butes with our own. We call a man close-fisted ;
they stigmatise him as mangal-muru (short-fisted);
a generous man is open-handed with us, with
them he is mangal-daimbur (loose-handed).
Mangal-gulrrigul (heavy) signifies one clumsy, and
a deft man is called mangal-dallel (light). Those
who have seen a pelican waddle and flop will
easily understand why the ungainly bird was
termed belu-warra (hip-crooked). Again, the
currish dingo is told that he is ngamu-ngartchar
(that he had Fright for his mother). But perhaps
the most interesting of all is the association of
ideas evident in the naming of introduced articles.
It must have been a very ingenious gentle-
man who first thought out the connection between
a child carried by the gin and a horse and so
named it ngakw-ganggal (a shoulder child) or
between a letter and the national game and called
writing kakau (cat’s cradle). Tea is bor (dried'
grass); sugar, mwrla (honey); and tobacco, ngalkal
(smoke).
Magdalen Mwlttu is one of the girls at the Cape
Bedford mission station who, without any assist-
or help, writes Lamb-like little essays on various
things in her native language just as the fancy
takes her. She is a correspondent of the Doctor’s,
and has written him several most interesting letters.
Indeed, one hopes that her passion for the new
kind of “ cat’s cradle ” may long continue, for she
is likely to contribute to Australia some invaluable
accounts of native manners and customs. Here is
an instructive bit of her copy on marriage and
burial customs (the translation is a free one, but
close enough for any ordinary comparison):—
(I) will tell you about men taking a wife. When men
want a wife (the parents) do not give her to them willingly
or with kindly feelings. Both mother and father strike
the young man’s head first; when he is covered with
blood they give her to him. Once given to him the wife
builds the hut and lights the fire. But the girl does not
readily go to meet her husband ; her mother and father
have to send her before she will go ; then she meets him.
Furthermore, during the daj'time she will not sit along-
side him in the shade. She is always with her mother ■
So much for the bride. When once the wife is engaged
to her husband for uood and all, the mother does
not see her son-in-Taw nor his mother’s mother
nor his father’s younger sister; they might die (if she
did). Should her husband die, she does a cry, and wears
white clay on her head and puts many strings round her
throat, and (these) she winds round her armpits. And
alongside the corpse there they sit, both wife and mother,
that is, the young man’s mother. (There) they light a
fire ; with the smoke they will hunt the flies away, they
might make the body foul (if they didn’t). On the death
of her husband the wife has only one night’s rest before
she offers her head to be struck with a wommera by all
the old men roundabout. As soon as much blood is seen
they are no longer angry with her.
What a treasure that girl will be when she gets
older—always supposing she remains at the mis-
sion—and is better able to co-ordinate her ideas
and give expression to her thoughts ! And we
may confidently look to Dr. Roth to pump her
until her stock of folk lore is exhausted, by which
we shall be much benefitted —even if we merely
get at first hand what we knew generally before at
second.
T.D.
Renter.
WHEN George Warrington wished to fire
young Pen dennis’ imagination he took
him to visit Printing House Square, to
see the home of the Times, with its
ambassadors pricing potatoes in Covent Garden
and whispering to statesmen in the courts of
kings. But since the “ Thunderer ” allowed its
ordinary journalistic caution to be warped by
party leanings (tlie Parnell business, the South
Africa business, etc.) it has lost much of its pictur-
esque reputation for infallibility and omniscience.
There is much more interest, nowadays, surround-
in x “ Reuter.”
To-day there isn’t a newspaper printed in the
English language that doesn’t contain some news
gathered by Reuter—the great news machine of
Old Jewry that daily gathers in facts from all the
world, and distributes them all over the world as
“Reuter’s cables.” And it all arose through
Reuter pere’s fondness for keeping pigeons. In
the early part of last century, a young man named
Reuter was a bank clerk and kept pigeons. This
was somewhere on the continent. He went in for
“homing.” One day the local greengrocer asked
Reuter, who was going on a trip, to let him know
on his return what was the price of potatoes in
the next town. Reuter sent the ruling rates for
potatoes per pigeon. From the price of potatoes
to prices of stocks and shares between the share-
brokers of two continental cities was one step
forward, and handing the messages to the local
newspaper was another. A little more evolution
and there arose the organisation of to-day, which
told the British people of the relief of Mafeking
48 hours before the British Government got the
news ; which proclaimed to the world the mutiny
of the West Indian troops in Ashaiitee, before it
was known in Lagos ; and which called forth the
censure of the- Times for giving heW§, from South
Africa which was unknown to Kitchener, but
afterwards proved to be correct, and thus earned
a long leader in apology from the London Granny.
* * * *
Although now a limited liability company, one
man still guides Reuter—Baron Herbert de
Reuter. He sits in a many-windowed, low-
ceilinged room in a tumbled-down rabbit-warren
sort of a place in Old Jewry, hugging tb himself
the pleasing reflection that nothing happens that
is worth happening that he doesn’t hear about
straightaway. To ensure all this, much “news”
has to be provided for months before it happens.
For instance, eight months before Kruger said he
would stagger humanity, and did, the Baron knew
that there was going to be a considerably large
war, and, like Labouchere, was laughed at when
he told a big politician privately that it would
cost £300,000,000. But the Baron knew, and he
selected Barraclough (his Brisbane manager) as a
strong man, and whisked him off to the Cape to
make the necessary preparations for running 50
correspondents, including Jfl special war-corres-
pondents in the field. Barraclough broke down the
other day, but has recovered now. Six months
before “the stagger T humanity ” announcement
the Baron applied to the War Office for maps, but
the Intelligence Department hadn’t any, with the
result that throughout the war the department
was much indebted to the Baron for the use of his
maps.
* * * *
The Baron works very hard. He has three
secretaries, and they all have to speak five
languages. It’s necessary, because so does the
Baron, and he writes in all of them. Baron Her-
bert, like all German University men, is a perfect
store-house of information, and I wish I could
write about a lot of things he knows more of
than any man living. If I could only write a
tenth of what he told me of the secret history
of South Africa it would elucidate much, but how
I would like to have beard all he didn’t tell me
about the Jamieson Raid, and which he will never
tell to anyone. The greatest pleasure I. have ever
enjoyed was when, on one or two precious occa-
sions, I dined with him at the office. The dinner
consisted —it never varies—of a pot of tea brought
in by one of his messenger-boys (there are 95
kept on the premises all dressed like express
messengers with “Reuter” on their caps) from
an adjoining cafe, slices of currant cake, and
innumerable Egyptian cigarettes. The Baron
“ dines ” thus at 7.30, and if you can tell him any-
thing about any country, he may give you a treat
by talking until 9.30 on men and politics of many
countries.
• * * *
The Australian papers have no direct influence
on Reuter’s work. Lachlan Mackinnon, when
he formed the present cable syndicate, tried to
ignore Reuter totally, but half the cables we get
are Reuter’s, obtained by the combination.
Reuter, however, is only paid for the right
to use his service, and not to make a
special service for Australian readers. The gene-
ral system now with big London papers is that
they have their own special correspondent* in
various quarters, and supplement their matter
with Reuter’s cable. Reuter matter is sent out
in flimsy to the subscribing newspapers, who pay
only for what they use. At present, the most
direct Australian connection with this great
news-gathering agency is that Reuter has the
right of access to all the Australian Press syndi-
cate messages on their receipt at Melbourne,
and supplies some of the country press with a
summary therefrom. But, if a new daily paper
were to be started, with an independent cable ser-
vice, Reuter would be probably one of the com-
petitors for the supply of the news.
PAUL PET.
OUR BURNT VERSES.
I wonder if the world, like me,
Has realised the aching void
That bards of old have eaused to be
Through verses they themselves destroyed !
Starting from this, the heart discerns
Vast works that might have been —but weren’t!
First : there would be in Robbie Burns
A lot of things that Robbie Burnt!
Then, if the fearsome truth were known,
I dare say Southey and Lord Byron
Have used a few things of their own—
Poetic fire, in fact! —for firin’!
If fires, like statesmen’s mouths, could gape
And show those castaways revealed, ,
We’d have one great, long “ fire-escape”
Would reach from Sapho down to—Neild !
THE BAROLD[?].
“The Viking Strain,” by A. G. Hales (our
Australian friend “ Smiler ” Hales), up to its
middle page, is well enough—a vivid story of
the horse-racing and prize-fighting which Smiler
Hales knows so well and describes so pic-
turesquely : then its star of sequence sets
and disappears in a ruddy welter of wild
incidents, like those of a Drury Lane melo-
drama or a Marie Corelli romance. The
hero is made the victim of an adventuress,
and marries her, kills a man for the sake of her
good name, goes through hair-raising adventures
with police and other foes on the European Con-
tinent, escapes to South Africa and becomes a
Boer hunter of lions, is joined there finally by
the one true English' lass who has always
loved him, and—the curtain. There is a littlfe of
everything in the book—even some spicy details
of matrimonial intimacies. Perhaps that will
make it more popular than if it had kept coherently
to its central idea, and been a sporting romance
of a type somewhat superior to Nat Gould’s: a
book often sells on its faults. At the least the
work has the mjerit of reflecting the splendid
energy of Mr. Hales ; and England is such a
very tired place nowadays that energy, even when
it runs a bit wild, commands attention.
&
How closely our Australians are watched ! To
hand this, week two letters, one from the far North
of Queensland, another from N.S.W., pointing
out that in a story in Pearson’s mag. Louise
Mack makes the rain patter on a cast-iron roof in
Australia. As an Australian she is expected to
know better. A third letter from a Sydney
suburb brings a complaint as to the lady’s
“ lamentable ignorance of hydraulics ” in a Lon-
don published story :
The point of the yarn hinges on the bursting of a water
pipe which floods the house. The occurrence is very
vaguely described, but the idea seems to be that rain
having caused the tanks on the roof to become full, the
whole weight of water had thereby been concentrated on
the small pipe loading to the bathroom and had burst it.
Now, the pressure required to burst a (sa.v) one inch iron
waterpipe, with material one-eighth of an inch thick- is
about 2Qoolbs. per square inch, and this would represent
a head of water of about 500 feet. She carefully picks
out the strongest point in the whole water system, and
says the water burst the pipe because it was the smallest,
whereas the smallest is the strongest in proportion to ite
size, etc., etc.
The noble bourgeois, the bourgeois nobility, the
educated t>f the favored middles class understand
Republicanism quite well—it is a lesson for which
not much previous knowledge is required, and
they would publicly acknowledge it were it not
that they would thus come into conflict with
money. Every dollar is a sturdy opponent of
Republicanism, and every ducat an Achilles. A
republican has good reason to hate money. If he
catch this enemy, alas ! the victory is worse than
a defeat. The republican who has got money has
ceased to be a republican. He is then like that
Austrian soldier who called out, ‘ ‘ Corporal, I
have taken a prisoner; ” but when the Corporal
told him to bring the man along, the answer was,
“I can’t, for he won’tlet me go.” I observed to-day
how the sympathy which Republicanism arouses
is constantly kept down through money interests.
Conversing with a very distinguished banker, he
said to me, excitedly, “ Who disputes the advan-
tages of the republican constitution ? I myself
am often quite a republican. But observe : I put
my hand into the right trouser pocket, where my
money is, and the contact with the cold metal
makes me tremble. I am afraid for my podket,
and I feel myself monarchically inclined.” — Heine.

has gained the rogue of fashion be subject to the swift
change of fashion ? Is this bloom a sign of health ?

i A Doctor’s Present.
A Thrilling Story of Suffering.
I
A Silver Lining* to the Darkest Cloud.
The Case of Mrs. A. JOSEPHS.
(BY A SYDNEY REPORTER.)
There is no suburb that has changed its
status as completely during the past twenty
years as that of Woolloomooloo. Facing
the bay bearing that name, with its
picturesque walks along the foreshores, and
with Hyde Park, the Botanical Gardens,
and the extensive Domain recreation
grounds in close proximity, Woolloomooloo
is one of the most attractive localities
around Sydney, and in it, at No. 18 Wood’s
Lane, lives Mrs. Annie Josephs, whose in-
terview with our reporter is reproduced
below.
“ I have lived at this address,” said Mrs.
Josephs, “for the past eight years, and
when I had been here about half that time
I thought I was going to die. It was after
a confinement that I suffered from blood
poisoning, besides which I lost a great
quantity of blood, and became so weak
that I could not put a foot to the ground
for nearly twelve months. For almost a
year I was in bed, being nothing but a
helpless and hopeless invalid, for I must
tell you that all the treatment I had
received did not lead me to believe
that I would ever get better. There
seemed nothing for it but to lie and
wait patiently for death to relieve me of
my sufferings, as I kept on wasting and
wasting away, and I was almost paralysed
with agony.”
“ Where were the pains principally
located ? ” enquired the pressman.
“ They were in every part of my body,
and my head swelled till it was about twice
its natural size, besides aching so intensely
that I scarcely knew what was going on
around me. Sometimes I became quite
stupefied with pain, and as I never could
sleep at night it is not to be wondered at
that I got so worn out that I used to lie
like one in a trance. All my limbs were
swollen and terrible pains afflicted them as
well, and when the severity of my aches
became so intolerable that a change in my
position was necessary, I had to have a
hoop bound round with wadding put under
my legs, because I was too weak to keep
them bent for a second without having
something underneath to support them.
There was no doubt that the longer I lived
the worse I got, for my nervous system was
frightfully strained, as I could not keep my-
self from shaking, and my mind was always
in a state of fear and anxiety. By some
means that 1 cannot explain, because I was
not enlightened on the subject myself, my
vision became very dim, and I nearly lost
the sight of my right eye. As an operation
was indispensable, I went under it, and
although my sight was saved, yet the strain
on my feeble constitution was almost too
great for me to bear. I got more nervous
than ever, and could not be left alone in the
house. The shaking of a window or the
creaking of a door sent a thrill of terror
right through me, and the next sensation
that I usually experienced was giddiness,
as though things were moving about in the
room. And you would hardly credit how I
felt if I ate any food, although I should say
‘ drank ’ instead of ‘ ate,’ because I was
only allowed the lightest of diet, and it
was invariably liquid. All the same, my
stomach could not digest it, and the way I
used to swell out with wind was something
awful, my chest also being troublesome
with a feeling as if a weight was inside me.
My skin was as yellow as a guinea, owing
to my liver being diseased, the doctors said,
and my face was so thin and disfigured that
many of my friends did not know me.
Having previously been very stout, they
could not realise it was me when they saw
nothing but a shadow before them, and the
ominous shakes of their heads made me feel
very downhearted. It was evident they
thought I was dying, and some of them
have since told me they felt sure I could not
live.”
“ You mentioned something about doctors
just now. What did they say about your
prospects ? ”
“ For a long time they tried to cheer me
by uttering kind and encouraging words,
but they did not do me any practical good
with their medicines, so then I submitted
myself for treatment at hospitals.
The physic pn scribed at those institutions
took no effect, > o I sank down to the lowest
depths of despair, because I thought my
fate was sealed. Later on I thought I
would make a final effort, and I sent for one
of the doctors who had been attending me
before, but he said that he could not do
any more for me than what he had
previously done, excepting to advise me to
take a course of Clements Tonic. He said
it was an excellent medicine, and soon after-
wards he good-naturedly made me a present
of two bottles.”
“ How did you feel then, Mrs. Josephs ?”
“I felt as if my whole life depended
upon Clements Tonic, and in doing so I am
sure I did not exaggerate the seriousness
of the situation, as every other treatment
had failed. When those two bottles of
Clements Tonic were finished there was
more life in me than there had been for
twelve months. I could then take a little
food and enjoy it. Before taking Clements
Tonic everything I tasted was as if I had
put a penny in my mouth, which left a
coppery flavor behind ; but that unpleasant-
ness was soon rein died, and, besides, the
coating over my tongue was cleared away.
Constipation had troubled me, but Clements
Tonic soon remedied that, and all the time
I was getting more light-hearted than ever,
because my pains were decreasing. These
imt rovements gave me a renewal >.f hope,
and, as was prudent, I clung tenaciously to
Clements Tonic. The next thing to cheer
my drooping spirits was to observe the
swellings in my head and limbs were being
reduced, and the pains were much less
severe than they had been. It was a very
unusual thing for me to wake up in the
night, but if I did I soon went to sleep
again, and rested peacefully till morning;
and another great benefit that I derived
from the use of Clements Tonic was that
my nerves were toned to their normal con-
dition. Those of my friends who had daily
expected to hear of my cU'vlt could haidly
realise the fact that I was being carried
further away from the grave every day by
Clements Tonic, and although four years
have passed since then, yet we often have a
chat about the narrow escape i had, and
Clements Tonic is the household remedy of
us all. •! was trying to tell you how 1
progressed. Well, in a week or two more I
was able to eat solid food without the least
compunction, as my digestive organs were
as healthy as they could be, for there was
no flatulence or chest pains to inconvenience
me, and there was not an ache in any part
of my system. When my strength and
health were completely restored I devoutly
thanked God for my happy release, and as
there is no doubt that Clements Tonic saved
my life you are at liberty to publish my
story in any way that the public may hear
of it.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, Annib Josephs, of 18 Wood’s-lane, Woolloo-
mooloo, in the State of New South Wales, Com-
monwealth of Australia, do solemnly and sin-
cerely declare that 1 have carefully read
the annexed document, consisting of four folios,
and consecutively numbered from one to four,
and that it contains and is a true and faithful
account of my illness and cure by Clements Tonic :
and also cont> ins my full permission to publish in
any way my statements—which I give voluntarily,
without receiving any payment; and 1 uia k e
this solemn declaration conscientiously believing the
same to be true, and by virtue of the provisions of the
Oaths Act, 1900.
Subscribed and declared at East Sydney, this
nineteenth day of February, one thousand nine
hundred and two, before me,
EDWARD W. BUTLER, J.P.

Winter Colds
Doctor advised Scott’s Emulsion
rather than any other form of the
oil
We are living now in the trying days of
the year when a winter oold is almost every-
body’s portion. We do not treat winter
colds with sufficient care. If we thought
of them seriously we would get the remedy
for them, as this doctor advised.
“ 67, Cleveland St., Upperthorpe,
“ Sheffield, England,
“ February 15th, 1901.
“ Dear Sirs, —For some years past I have
been very susceptible to colds and bronchitis,
and this winter my doctor advised me to
take cod-liver oil, and suggested Scott’s
Emulsion in preference to any other form
of the oil. I took your preparation and
found great relief from its use in a very
short time. My cough quickly responded
to the treatment, my chest was greatly
strengthened and my general health im-
proved considerably. I continued the treat-
ment for a time and am gratified to say as a
result my health is now all that I could

HERBERT TURNER.

wish, for and I have not had a cold since

Mr. Turner emphasises two points for

Winter colds yield promptly to Scott’s

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Autumn Flashes.
Fair afternoon, tho’ short her stay,
A-flame, a-throb, a-flower,
Crowds Spring’s whole glory of a day
In this chance Autumn hour.
Peaks, through the azured nearer view,
To half-greens ghostly wane,
As if they’d burst their vials of blue
And spilled them o’er the plain.
In spite of sun, whose stagey glare
Would eyes’ applause usurp,
I tender furtive tribute where
His chorus cloud-birds chirp:
(As while some witch-soprano sings
The veil from Isis’ face,
We, trifling, notice in the wings
A flirting girl’s grimace.)
While fickle breezes browse or skip
Their shepherd, she-oaks croon—
E’en Sorrow seeks Joy’s beaker lip
This lovely afternoon.
Though dying, glows the berried hedge,
The wounded ground’s a-green,
And where was bush-fires’ sacrilege,
Faith’s pious shoots are seen.
No blot, save where, ’neath sky-wall’s base,
Pride’s hoarded acres spread
By landless Plebs’ insensate grace—
Till Gracchuses are bred:
Or where with glowing callousness
Young men and maids career
In pride of limb and steed and dress—
To kill a quivering deer.
So dreaming, rushing hoofs I hear
And —Autumn’s grandeurs fade
To heotios of a dying year
In the glory of a maid.
The passing glory of a maid,
A-bud, a-flash with love 1
How mean the browsing breeze, burnt glade,
Peak, verdure, clouds above !
The blots, too, seem, I fear, less black
As dims the April’s flame,
For from the cruel hunting paok
And squatter’s home she came.
MELB.
ALOHERINGA.
The Bushmen.
God, the day He fashioned them,
Toiled to make them true,
Hand and head and heart (d them,
Blood and bone and thmv ;
Set them in the foremost rank
Giants’ work to do.
God, the day He fashioned them,
Bred them to their siren,
Supple as their bullock--whipe,
Tough as twisted wires ;
Filled the strudy brains of them
With His battle-fires.
Then, when He had fashioned them,
Tried them for His pride ;
Sent His droughts and ohastened them,
Sent His flames to ohide 5
Sent His floods to harry them
Wasting far and wide.
But the strength He set in them
Braoed His fighting brood ;
Bravely, face to face with Him,
Shoulder-firm they stood—
Then the God who fashioned them
Knew His work was good l
So, when He has gathered them,
God will not forget;
High at the right hand of Him
He shall have them set;
Paying thus for pride of them
Seven-fold his debt!
WILL H. OGILVIE.
Winter.
Her breath is in the breeze at dawn,
These Autumn days fast vanishing;
And anxious men, who’ve braved and born,
Are wond’ring “ What will this one bring ? ”
Set faces just a shade relax
When up the West her gloom is cast;
And hopeful men who swing the axe
Say, “ ’Twill be better than the last.”
Teams on the hillsides bare appear
When mists are lifting, and the crows
Shout in the furrows at the rear:
The settlers’ “ hope God only knows.
The seed-machines hum round the plains
(Like hornets in the face of Fate) —
Though Heaven is sparing with the rains,
And feckless men won’t irrigate.
The skipping lambs at frosty dawn,
The flitting songsters in the pine,
Sweet flush of day, when night’s withdrawn,
And brilliant tiers of leaves a-shine—
These move the sluggard heart to fire
These Autumn days, fast vanishing:
But flood and deluge men desire
Who wonder “ What this one will bring.”
v.
WILL JARRATT.

The SPA-TREATMENT
FIFTY POUNDS.
IT is highly probable that a trip to Europe for a course of waters at any of the famous Spas
would cost over Fifty Pounds, but many who could afford £5O cannot afford the necessary
time. It will therefore be welcome news to learn that all the benefits of the fifty-pound
a tn o P are contamed in a 1)04616 of KUTNOW’S POWDER. John Strange Winter (Mrs.
Arthur btannard), the popular authoress, after trying it, wrote, “ That it ought to be known all
over the world.” The late Sir Morell Mackenzie, M.D., wrote : “ I have tried Kutnow’s
Powder, and find it answers particularly well.” Professor Lawson Tait, M.D., wrote :
Lawson Tail heartily recommends Kutnow’s Powder.”
HEALTH
In thoughtless ease too little priz’d
This richer gift than wealth;
Of it bereft, too late we feel
Life is but life with health!
Procrastination in applying a safe and efficient remedy may mean incom-
prehensible mischief to those who suffer from derangement of the digestive or
excretory organs. Success in business or the professions, or in any walk in life,
depends very largely upon the state of health. Men who work with the brain
value health, and men who value health take KUTISTOW’S POWDER. Any
one of the ailments mentioned above may develop into serious disease unless
checked in time. A Sluggish Liver, for instance, produces mental depression,
clogs up the bile-ducts, brings on a bilious condition, and creates excessive uric
acid. Excessive uric acid contaminates the blood, and breeds Gout, Rheu-
matism, Sciatica, and congests the kidneys. Congested kidneys bring on Stone
in the Bladder, Diabetes, Bright’s Disease, and vesical irritations generally.
KUTNOW’S POWDER is simply invaluable in the treatment of these
derangements, and will restore the bloom of vigorous health to the most debili-
tated patient. It is an exact reproduction of the famous medicinal merits of
the waters of the European Spas, minus any of their drastic or lowering
properties.
Beware of Fraudulent Imitations.
KUTNOW’S POWDER may be obtained in capsuled bottles, of all Chemists and Medi-
cine Vendors. See that the Registered Trade Mark, “ Hirschensprung ” (or Deer Leap), and
the autograph facsimile signature, “S. KUTNOW and Co. (LD.),”areon the label and carton.
ONLY THESE ENSURE GENUINENESS. INSIST ON “KUTNOW’S,” AND
FIRMLY REFUSE ANY SUBSTITUTES; THEY ARE WORTHLESS AND OFTEN
INJURIOUS.
Proprietors: S. KUTNOW & CO., LTD., 41 FARRINGDON ROAD, LONDON, E.C.
Proprietors in U.S.A.: KUTNOW BROS., LTD., 853 BROADWAY, NEW YORK CITY, U.S.A.
A Striking Success
Unquestionably the necessity of vigorous health
is seriously personal to everyone, and fortunately
| science has triumphantly provided the means
whereby the debilitated may regain and retain a
re-vitalised energy. The curative and healing
virtues of the waters of the famous European
Spas are now embodied in Kutnow’s Powder,
which is presented in the form of a beautifully
white, dry effervescent. It is improved by the
deletion of the drastic and nauseous elements of
the medicinal springs. By removing the drastic
constituent and introducing a palatable ingre-
dient Kutnow’s Powder is decidedly preferable
to the original waters.
Its advantages are:—
Firstly—
The power to expel the excess of bile and
acids; also, the poisonous and putrefying
deposits in the bowels and kidneys.
Secondly—
The power to stimulate a sluggish liver and
to eliminate all the excessive Urio Acid.
Thirdly—
The virtue of not only cleansing the bowels
and diluting the urine, but at the same time
exercising a soothing influence on the system
generally.
Fourthly—
The freedom it induces in the flow of the
gastric juices, thereby removing all the terrors
of indigestion.
Fifthly—
The utility of combining curative properties
with strengthening and invigorating virtues:
it is cooling to the blood, refreshing to the
brain, and revitalising to the whole system.


Dr. Cherry, Victoria’s new dairy expert, j
is perfecting a system of tests whereby the much-
worried factory manager can tell whether milk
has been adulterated by the addition of water or
skim milk, or has had the cream removed. The
adding of skim milk has hitherto been very preva-
lent. There is a hard time ahead for the unfortu-
nate dairyman, who will soon be reduced to the
calamitous necessity of delivering his milk in the
state in which it comes from the cow—or with
merely some dirt and hairs added.
Robert Brothers writes :
On 3rd June, Narandera (N.S.W.) Land Board con-
sidered a case of living interest to selectors. Tubbo
Estate 00. sued six neighboring settlers for the half cost
of a rabbit-proof fence, and the board decided in favor
the station people. Tubbo Estate 00. is a squatting syn-
dicate of wealthy capitalists, mostly absentees. A year
or more ago a certain tract of country, leased by this
syndicate, was thrown open for homestead settlement,
and was taken up by seven men, who put all their savings
into the land, with the object of making a home.
Between the boundaries of these selections and the
station boundary runs a road. The selectors proceeded
to put up fences on their boundaries, and the station
people did likewise on the opposite side of the road, the
only difference being that the station boundary fence had
less wires run through, but was protected against bunny
by wire netting. The syndicate’s fence being completed,
the selectors were billed for half the cost of the. erection
of the fence. The selectors resisted, as the station fence
is no earthly use to them, since they have their own
fences, and, anyhow, the station fence is on the wrong
side of the road. Moreover, they are all more or less
crippled in finances owing to building their own fences
and otherwise improving the land, and the payment of
the half cost of the station fence will practically ruin
them. The cost of the fence is put down at £7O odd per
mile, a heavy expense for an • impoverished selector to
meet within the first year of occupancy. As a matter of
fact, the decision of the Land Board pushes them off the
land. According to this decision of Narandera Land
Board, what selector will be safe from paying half the
cost of erecting station fences and half the cost of repair-
ing them ? And what is to prevent all N.S.W. becoming
a sheepwalk ?

butions submitted, whether in the form of Political,
Social, or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales or Sketches

inch artistic contributions will be received are those

SATURDAY, JUNE 21, 1902.
An Irresponsible Federal Parliament.
It is now practically certain that every State
of the Commonwealth barring Westralia will
close the year with a ponderous deficit, and
Westralia is only saved by the fact that it
is still allowed to collect inter-State duties—
a privilege which no other State enjoys.
But for its inter-State duties, which are
only temporary, it would also have a deficit.
Meanwhile the Freetrade party in the Senate
has a bare majority, partly by reason of the
folly of Victoria in electing, at the request of
the Age, one or two personal friends of
David Syme whose fiscal politics were more
than doubtful; and it is gaily passing resolu-
tions for the further destruction of tariff
revenue. The present revenue isn’t sufficient
to keep the States really solvent, but by the
curious arrangement of things the Federal
Parliament, which raises the tariff revenue,
doesn’t require to concern itself as to
whether the States are solvent or not. And
the States themselves don’t care sufficiently
fur their own solvency to Inake anything like
the row they ought to raise over the Federal
proceedings ; a long course of deficits, loans,
and shufflings have made solvency a thing
little valued in State politics. Also the
Federal politicians are built very much on
the same lines as the State politicians ; they
are mostly either men like Symon, who know
nothing of finance, or men like M'Millan,
Joe Cook, and the two Smiths (B. and S.),
of W., who gained their knowledge in a
Government whose highest aim was to cook
the accounts, and misappropriate the loan
money, and tell astounding lies about the
shortage.
• • * *
So long as the principal source of revenue,
the customs and excise, belongs to the Com-
monwealth, and the principal cause of expen-
diture, the public debt, is left with the
States, there will be confusion, and the con-
fusion at present is very bad indeed. Just
now the situation stands something like
this :
(1) The Commonwealth is authorised under the
±SRADDON clause, either to reserve for its own use during
the next 10 years one-fourth of the customs and excise
revenue, returning the other three-fourths to the States,
can take over the State debts and use the othe^
m to pay the interest with.
<?) While the deb.ts are not taken over by the Common-
wealth the Commonwealth Parliament doesn’t require to
care a straw whether the State Parliaments get enough
to keep them solvent or not.
(3) If the Commonwealth paid all its expenditure out
l r 9 e -™'“ e WO ! l]d re( t u > re . probably, somewhere about
use. That means that, unless
pa nnn ?° dl r ect , taxation, it would require to raise
JifiVfin’nm oufc of whic h it must return
chnm-e o r o h o f H ta , teS ’ 0I ? whlch basis there would be a
stote of ddapidation nUght not dr ° P int ° a ver * v bad
f ederal Government, however, is already
vear tw a° rroW ’ ” 14 borrows, say, £750,000 in one
year, then it may only need to raise £1,500,000 for its
own use by taxation. Therefore a £6,000,000 tariff will
° f tbatlaw only requires that £4,500,000
probably burst States ’ ln which case the States will
Federa ! Parliament has the principal power of
™' a Z T ne> '’ wlthoufc being under any responsibility to
aS t m, fl,ld enou ftb money to meet’the
cheerfmiv ni - • T , heref ? re . can reduce taxation
fiiw • J 18 thereby principally reducing the other
fellows income, and Svmon and co. can throw around the
blessings of commercial freedom ” with a light heart
• • * «
If it were not for the Braddon Blot
clause, matters, of course, would be im-
measurably worse. The Blot is the only
thing which ensures to the States such tariff-
revenue as they have ; without it, the
Federal Parliament might return nothing at
aIL In the days when the Constitution was
being constructed, a strong party, mostly
from N.S.W., contrived to prevent the
debts being federalised on the ground that
it was a dark and baleful dodge on
the part of persons who were still at
large by which N.S.W.’s credit was to
be used to prop up “ the bankrupt States,”
and it postponed indefinitely the fede-
ration of the railway systems on the ground
that there was a murky conspiracy against
“our” assets. The union was left incomplete,
and the Braddon clause, as a kind of treaty
between the party which had the power
to raise money it didn’t want, and the party
which hadn't the power to raise money it did
want, was the natural outcome. But even
it is an insufficient remedy. The majority
in the Federal Parliament throws away cus-
toms revenue with a light heart, because the
States are responsible for the deficit that!
will follow, and the State Parliaments seem
inclined to let the deficit slide with a light-
heart on the ground that they aren't respon-
sible for the throwing away of revenue. And
the people of Australia have to stand the
loss either way.
* * * .
If the Federal Government were to take
over the public debts of the States the posi-
tion would be vastly improved. There
would then be little or no surplus to be
returned. The States would be left to find
the money for their own responsibilities
while the Commonwealth would do likewise.
If the Federal Parliament, in its joyous
pursuit of the blessings of commercial free-
dom, which is another name for destroying
local industries in order to increase the
demand for M'Millan’s imported calico,
chose to throw away revenue, it would at
least be its own revenue it was throwing
away and its own deficit it was creating.
Nominally, of course, if the Federal Parlia-
ment cut down its own revenue so low, by
the sacrifice of customs and excise duties
that it wouldn’t cover the interest on State
debts taken over, it could call on the
States to make good the difference.
But when there was no surplus in the
hands of the Federal Government out
of which it could stop the amount
due, the prospect of the States re-
sponding to the call is too small to be
worth alluding to. The Federal Govern-
ment would have to pay its own way out of
its own taxes, and the State Government
would do likewise, and the present compli-
cation would come to an end.
* • •
To do this effectively, however, it is
necessary that the Commonwealth should
take over all the State debts as they existed
on Ist January, 1901—until the Constitution
is amended it has no power to take over any
later liabilities. And these were a variable
lot, the respective liabilities at the date of
the beginning of the Commonwealth beino-
about as follows
Debt per Interest per
Inhabitant. inhabitant
, per annum.
Queensland .. £73 19 2 .. £2 16 10
South Australia .. 73 2 6 2 17 4
WestraUa .. .. 67 14 6 " 214 0
N-S.W 49 7 3 .. 118 4
Tasmania .. .. 47 17 8 1 16 0
Victoria .. .. 46 5 1 .. 112 8
Th e amounts have varied a good deal since
these figures were drawn up—that is to say,
the amount of debt which the Commonwealth
can take over .remains stationary at the
figure of Ist January, 1901, and that amount
now only represents about £59 per head on
Westralia’s increased population, while
smaller variations have happened in other
States. But still the discrepancies are very
large, and no State wants to strike an average
by which it will carry a copper of any
other State s burden. Yet if the Common-
wealth took over the Victorian debt of
£46 per head, and a corresponding propor-
tion of the debts of other States, it would be
a . very incomplete and half-hearted opera-
tion. A large proportion of the debts would
still be left in the hands of the States. There
would be no general consolidation, and there
would still be the necessity to return some
Federal revenue to Queensland and S.A.,
and the Federal Treasurer would still be
some kind of agent or bailee for the State
Treasurer, which is an uncomfortable posi-
tion for all parties, and puts all parties in
a bad temper.
* * * *
There are one or two apparently possible
ways out of the difficulty. The three States
with the largest debts are also the ones with
the largest area of unsold lands, and if the
Commonwealth took over the debts bodily
they might compensate it for relieving them
of an undue share of liabilities by handing
over sufficient of their public lands to
equalise things. That would make the Federal
Government a large landholder in a patchy
k ; nd of way; still there is no reason why
the experiment should not be successful.
Again, the Federal Government might take
over the whole heap of debt as it stands and
make it the common debt of Australia, the
States which are relieved of the largest share
of debt compensating those which are re-
lieved of the smallest share by giving them
their own State securities for an amount
sufficient to equalise things. A simpler way
would be for the Federal Government to take
over the whole mass of liabilities, and agree
to pay for a number of years a fixed subsidy,
not the present fleeting and unreliable “sur-
plus,” to the States whose burdens are unduly
increased by the arrangement. It could be
calculated exactly how much Australia in
general owed to the States which handed
over the smallest debt, and this amount,
principal and interest, could be paid off by
instalments. The simplest way of all would
be for the States, in a fine, large spirit of
brotherhood, to agree to lump the debts and
not haggle about details. The States with
the largest debts are also the ones with the
most room for increased population, and
their growth might, in a few years, make
the arrangement less uneven than it seems.
The Government which proposed this
arrangement would at least receive the
cordial support of Queensland and S.A.—
but neither Sydney nor Melbourne would
be a good city to be in when the pro-
posal was mooted. At all events, the
necessity for making the Federal Parlia-
ment responsible for something is very
evident Its Symons and M'Millans are
dropping into a habit of knocking off revenue,
and leaving the State Treasurers to wrestle
with the consequences, which promises
disaster.
A Groper Democrat.
Nanson, who was Lands Minister in the very
sudden Morgans Cabinet in Westralia, and who
now leads the old Forrest party, vice Forrest,
Throsskl and Morgans, all politically deceased,
has come out with a programme calculated to make
the hist hair of old Groperdom stand on end with
amazement. Many strange things have happened
to old Groperdom since it was first awakened
from its slumbers by the feet of the gold-seekers
trampling over its chest, but few have been more
sudden than Nanson’s programme. The Wes-
tralian Opposition leader favors the immediate
granting of Old Ago Pensions, redistribution of
constituencies according to population, a Factories
Act somewhat on the Victorian model, a State
Liquor Monopoly, and the reduction of the State
Governor’s salary by one-half, which will bring it
down to £2ooo—the amount paid by the Canadian
province of Ontario, which has 10 times Westralia’s
population. And he is half inclined to commit
himself to the abolition of the useless and not at
all ornamental Upper House, though just here he
is much less definite than he might be. The fol-
lowing is how Nanson hovers regarding this
subject:—
There was one reform to which they would have to give
earnest and close consideration, and' that was the Aboli-
tion of the Upper House. He mentioned that because
they could nob too soon take it into consideration.
(Cheers.) He was aware that there were many constitu-
tional authorities averse to only or.e Chamber. He knew
also that the doing away of the Upper House was sur-
rounded by dangers. (Hear, hear.) He looked at the
theory of constitutional authority, and also the fact that
the experiment had proved a success in Canada. He was
not absolutely pledged to support the abolition of the
Upper House, but if it could be shown that it had proved
a great success in Canada, that only the best results had
been attained, then the question would have to be taken
up and seriously considered in Australia. It might seem
a daring radical proposition. (A Voice : “Not at all, it
is very wise.”) He believed that it was his friend, Mr.
Wilson, who made that interjection. If so, he was glad to
note that Mr. Wilson’s views were broadening on the eve
of his re-entry into political life. So conservative a
paper as the London Times had recently alluded to
Canada’s experiment as an admirable one. If he had to
choose between two weak Chambers, reduced so that all
the interests of the country would not be properly looked
after, and one strong Chamber, he would certainly be in
favor of the one. It would give that Chamber a stronger
sense of its responsibility. He knew, with even his brief
experience of political life, that in the House, when a
difficult question was presented, members would say,
“ Oh, pass it on to the Council ; they’ll chuck it out.” It
was not a prominent item on his platform, the abolition
of the Upper House, because it was not one requiring
immediate attention.
» • • •
The Westralian Opposition Leader certainly
doesn’t commit himself violently as regards the
abolition of the Upper Gropers. Still he is
further advanced than any other Opposition
leader in Australia except Browne, of Queens-
land. He is further advanced than any Premier
in Australia, and he is miles ahead of the great
bulk of Groper politicians. Nanson is a
new man, and almost unknown, but a
politician with such a programme deserves
to have a trial. The Bulletin, at all
events, begins to grow weary in some respects of
the well-meaning and clean-shaven Premier
Leake, who gets very little “forrarder,” and so
long as Nanson sticks to the above policy, and
shows any reasonable sign of being qualified to
carry it out, this paper’s vote goes with him. No
other man who seems at all likely to secure an
Australian Premiership at an early date has even
begun to assimilate the fact that government by
one House of Parliament is a strictly British idea,
and is not by any means a rash experiment, but a
device which has been in full working order for
many years. Six out of the eight divisions of
Canada manage to live and prosper with only one
House (Ontario, with over 2,000,000 people, has a
single House of 94 members), and with a local
man, appointed by the Governor-General, as
State Governor; and that local man struggles
through with a wage of from £l4OO to £2OOO a
year. It is gratifying to find one prospective head
of an Australian Government who knows that
this bright example exists, and who can so far
.struggle out of the purely Australian rut as to
propose following it, even though his proposition
is less forcible than it should be.
• • * *
Nanson’s weak point is his pathetic faith in the
old Bigjohn borrowing policy. He has a cheery
belief that the boom will last for ever. His
simple logic is that because Westralia, while
on the top of a gold boom that can’t last because
gold is only temporary, and on the summit of a
loan boom of such dimensions that it has added
£4O per head to the debt in ten years, is able to
pay its way easily, therefore it can always pay its
way easily. Victoria thought the same in 1889,
when it was whooping on the topmost wave of its
loan boom—the year of the £750,000 surplus ; but
Nanson, like every other boomster, reckons that
Ins bubble is the exception—the one permanent,
non-burstable bubble of the earth. Still, the rest
of his programme entitles Nanson to respect.
And, after all, it is pretty evident that one of the
Australias will have to go insolvent as a warning
w ie F est before anv good will happen, and
Westralia may as well be the dreadful example as
any other State. For the sake of his State Liquor
Monopoly idea, and his Factories Act and Old
Age Pension schemes, and his half-idea—may it
shortly become a whole one—in favor of the
abolition of the Upper House, The Bulletin
inclines to vote that Nanson is a fit and proper
person to be Premier of Groper Land. If he gets
the Premiership and then falls away from grace in
the democratic sense it will only be what most other
Premiers have done before him. The quantity of
prace that has been fallen away from in Australia
is enormous, and most of the time this country
finds itself choosing between the politician who
will probably fall from grace and the one who
never had any to fall from.
Politics Which are Too Practical.
The debate in the Australian Senate on the tariff
proposals of the Barton Government has given
further proof that there is little educated national
sentiment as yet in our politics. There have been
many mildly fierce wrangles about twopence-half-
penny issues involved in the details of the
tariff, eloquent debates as to whether the
country would be a few odd pence to the good
or to the bad if a particular duty were
increased or decreased, wonderful statistical
calculations as to the relative advantages of
importing certain articles or manufacturing them
in the country, but no member has taken up, con-
sistently, a national attitude and dropped in on
the war of figures with the remark that if, as the
Freetraders said, the country was bound to lose a
little on the Protectionist deal, and the issue was
to keep a shop and gain twopence-halfpenny, or
be a self-contained nation and lose that amount,
he was for being a nation all the time. Yet that
is a phase of the issue which is worthy of
serious attention, quite apart from all the
economic controversy as regards Freetrade and
Protection. Australia, as a country which
exports raw material enormously and im-
ports manufactured goods enormously, practi-
cally stakes her very existence on the con-
tinuance of world-peace. Whilst the highways of
the sea are open, she can sell her raw materials
and secure those products which are necessary to
her comfort, if not to her actual existence. Soaps,
clothes, shoes, paper, machinery, war materials
are easily obtainable now on payment of the price.
But in case of a great war blocking the sea-ways,
and putting a stop to both exports and imports
for a while, what then ?
* * • *
Well, now, even in a partially-Protected Aus-
tralia, the blocking of the sea would involve this
continent in the gravest trouble. The people
would be able to feed themselves in a rough
fashion, but they would have but poor clothing if
the war raged for long, their journals would have
to stop publication for lack of paper, their
harvests would in time have to go unreaped, and
their mines undeveloped for lack of machinery,
and, in case of invasion, their soldiers would have
to go to battle with bill-hooks and scythes for
lack of ammunition. If the Freetrade idea were
carried out in full, and most of the existing indus-
tries wiped out by exposure to the cheap-labor
competition of the world, the lot of Australia in
war-time would be absolutely hopeless.
Yet it should be understood, as a first principle
of politics, that, quite apart from any issues of
Freetrade or Protection, Australia, being in an
insular position and liable at any time to be cut
off from communication with the outside world
by a naval war, should be able to feed herself,
clothe herself, and arm herself out of her own
resources, from her own factories. Even if the
pursuit of this end causes an economic loss (which,
of course, it would not), yet that should cause no
hesitation._ The home-made rifle may cost
£3 as against the imported rifle’s £2, yet even
apart from the fact that it is cheaper for a man to
pay himself £2 than to pay another man £3, it is
clear that Australia, as the only means to make
sure that she can get the rifle when she most
needs it, should pay the £3 without a murmur.
The same remark applies to the rifle’s
ammunition, and to agricultural and mining
machinery, and to railway engines and rails, and
to steel ships, and to a thousand other things.
Even if, by building a big reservoir, a citv
makes its water-supply more costly than
by relying on roof-tanks and wells, the
reservoir is generally built to give surety of
supply. And a nation, to ensure a continuance of
its existence as a nation, must make certain of its
supply of the first necessaries of civilised life.
That’s the National view of the fiscal issue.
* • • *
But extremely little was heard of it in eithei
House of the Federal Parliament. There was
much animated talk on tare and tret lines, much
fractious discussion as to whether the odd half-
penny leaned in the balance towards Freetrade or
Protection. But hardly a word to indicate
that, as Australian legislators, their first duty
was to see that Australia should be a self-
contained, self-supporting nation, able to live if
necessity arose, without any outside assistance
dependent on nobody’s tolerance, trusting to no
blind chance of Fortune for her national existence
A Freetrade Australia, besieged and beset with
enemies, would have to surrender at the first call
because there would be no powder for her guns’
and no hope for her people to live without foreign
imports. An Australia made self-reliant by Pro-
tection, supplying her population with all the
necessaries of life from within her own borders
could maintain her national existence as long as
the courage of her men remained, as did France
after the Revolution when she faced and beat back
a continent in arms. If France at that time had
had to import her arms from England and her
food from Germany the Republic would have been
smothered at its birth.
* * • •
In the recent “ White Australia ’’ debates there
was more of the national spirit shown bv the
Australian legislature. But even therein no
politican brought to the issue the true glow of n
passionate patriotism. There was certainly


some discussion of the racial degradation which
would follow the presence of a large Asiatio
population side by side with the white com-
munity, but no member flung at the flaccid
Tories, who droned of the thousands of pounds
sunk in this and that industry, alleged to depend
for success on black labor, the facts as to one ot
the revolting race-outrages so common in America
as the result of the friction of a black and white
population, with the challenge—“Tomy mind,
better to sacrifice millions of money than to stain
our nation’s hands with that! ” Yet how electric,
how unanswerable, such a defiant argument.
Who of the Bruce Smiths, wearing the livery ot
Mammon, would have dared to join issue on that
point, to plead for bringing a better sugar-dividend
with so many burnings-at-the stake, so many sex-
outrages, so many Kanakas* lives sacrificed .
• * • *
There is nothing more disgusting in politics
than “blather”—the frothy “iloquence of the
man who Orates rather than speaks, who calls
upon the stars in their courses and all the Pagan
myths in a cheap classical dictionary to furnish
tawdry ornamentation for his bombastic periods
and insincere arguments. _ Better anything than
that—better the most sordid and calculating half-
penny spirit. But there is room for a little more
idealism, a little more “ imagination ” of the right
sort in our Australian politics. Public men might,
with advantage, try to come to look upon their
country more as a fatherland and less as an em-
porium—a branch emporium of the great nr in of
John Bull and Co. —as a fatherland to be guarded
sacredly from wrongdoing as well as from disaster.
At present our politics are greatly lacking in
inspiration.
Notes on the Rise and Progress of Pearl-
Shelling.
Whites a man from the North:
In the beginning of the 70*8 seafaring men heard more
and more of the deposits at Cape York of the big bivalve
shells, from the nacre of which the substance known to
jewellers and fancy goods makers as “ mother o’ pearl ” is
taken. In those prehistoric days the brothers Jardine
and their father, the P.M. of Somerset, were, or had been,
kings. Somerset, be it known, is the country at the
extreme end of the York Peninsula. Seafaring men
found that the wild aborigines of the northern cape of
Australia and the islands between it and New Guinea were
able to plunge into those seas and by swimming down
reach certain shells at the bottom of eighteen and twenty
feet of water. These shells, as big as soup-plates, were
not hard to see at that depth in the clear water. Thus
began the pearl-shelling by the use of naked aborigines
plunging in and swimming down to the bottom, and thus
began the name of swimming divers. Many a native died
by diving too deep and getting all the wind squeezed
from his lungs by the pressure of water at those depths.
To assist the swimming divers, Malay crews were
imported. Soon the enterprising Sydney diver, with his
scientific diving dress and accompanying pipes and pumps,
heard of the fortunes falling into the hands of the lucky
captain adventurers who could get their swimming divers
on a “ patch of shell.” So arrived the white diver and
his apparatus. Then gradually there grew small settle-
ments upon different stands in the vicinity of the
‘ ‘ patches of shell.” Let it be said shell is just like land-
minerals : it exists in aggregations. These are called
patches. Other minerals exist in aggregations
and are called lodes, or seams, or reefs ; and shell, like
them, exists in aggregations called by a different name.
The oyster makes the shell by extracting the minerals
of which it is composed, from the water. Consequently
this diving industry has the advantage over other
mineral industries in that, while clearing away the lode
of other minerals means its permanent removal, clearing
away of a patch of shell means only its temporary
removal. This fact is well known to the syndicate of
shellers, hence their anxiety to remain “ kings of the
shell beds.” Well, then, now had arrived the master
European diver who was to revolutionise old methods. For
many years shelling operations were carried on from vari-
ous islands lying between New Guinea and the Australian
mainland, and it is hard to contemplate a pleasanter life
in happier or more beautiful surroundings. Here Nature
is superb, and her colorings are finer than those of the
Mediterranean. Man is avaricious—he is the same good
son of Satan that we find elsewhere. The first old sea-
dogs and Francis Oadell, discoverer of the River Murray,
came in small schooners of 100 or 120 tons. There was
no settlement of a permanent nature, and these schooners
carried stores and grog to supply the crews of their
diving boats. As the settlements on the islands grew
more civilised the schooners disappeared. The white
diver now owned his boat and worked for himself. He
built houses on the beach of some stand which he chose,
and married and reared children of the European race,
and packed his shells in sheds built on land. Thus the
industry appeared likely to become rooted to Australian
soil, and the wealth produced likely to give rise to per-
manent towns, just as the wealth of gold or copper, coal,
or silver has produced permanent and lasting towns on
Australian soil. Then came the syndicator. He viewed
with distaste the idea of the pearl-sheller wasting time by
living at home ashore. “ His place,” said he, “is at sea ;
he should never lose a moment; what is the good of him
living in this isolated place, owning his own boat?
* * * »
He proceeded with schooners and little paoks of ten or
twelve boats, manned by colored divers and colored crews,
to squeeze the white diver out. He packed his shell
upon his schooner. It was so easy to kill the white diver.
There were but few white divers rearing European
families; they had no powerful friends; why dared
they work the shelling beds at all ! Every European on a
patch of shell soon found himself surrounded by a dozen
Japs or Manilamen working at the order of the syndicate
and systematically eating up his patch of shell in order to
s arve him out. Soon the European found that his haul
2 less and less and his hitherto prosperous business was
b. -n up by men to whom the advancement of Australia
Wdo tno moment. Indeed oppositely ; the advancement
of the i dands by settlement upon them must be defeated
in every way possible. Hence came the Act to stop the
Japanese or Manilaman or other colored diver from work-
ing for himself. The profits of the fisheries the syndicate
could send to London and spend them there or in any other
place the syndicator chose, but the industry gave no per-
manent improvement to the district in which it was
carried on. Readers can judge of the consternation of the
syndicators at the appearance of a Commonwealth upon
the horizon. Then they said : “ We must bluff those Com-
monwealthites,” and they did. The men who have biffed
the European divers off the ground deserve scant sympathy
from Australian working men. The miners on the fields
of Australia should think where they would be if the
British syndicates could introduce Chinese or Jap miners
to work those claims, or goldfields, which are scattered
all over the Australian continent. The biffing process,
heartlessly enough applied to European divers, may with
equal heartlessness be applied to the syndicators whose
motto is not “ Advance Australia,” but “ Advance
Avarice and Down with the White Men.”
• * * •
Let us compare pearl-shelling with agriculture, since we
have compared. Do not the agriculturists of Australia
want to work on their own farms? Have not the big
estates on the Darling Downs, which were given to syndi-
cates, been bought back and sold to small farmers? But
the pearl-shell syndicates didn’t compensate the colored
divers who owned their own boats when they abolished
their licenses, and took their business from them. The
syndicates didn’t pay for their monopoly. They stole it-- 1
and, as they gave no compensation, they deserve none.
The'pearling beds worked by white divers for themselves
compare with the small farms worked, by farmers for
themselves. The big pearling syndicate is brother to the
big land syndicate, only more unscrupulous and more
avaricious. Finally, let it be said to men who talk of
turning Dutchmen: “Very good, gentlemen! Turn
Dutchmen, but remember, no use of our hospital for your
seamen, no landing on any of our islands (and we own
them all) for wood and water. Get your wood and water
where you get your nationality. You have declared war
upon us. You are the aggressors. Don’t complain if we
give you hard knocks. Don’t blame us if we kick you out
of our Commonwealth, bag and baggage. Don’t blame
us if we rid the Commonwealth of a set of men whose
avarice has been so excessive as to cause them to begrudge
their men going ashore for half a day lest they might ask
for some of their pay, and spend it in Thursday Island,
and help to maintain a place which, according to their
benevolent views, has no right to be in existence.”
PLAIN ENGLISH.
An Inaccurate Pastor.
A careful perusal of the vast mass of sermons
preached on account of the declaration of Peace
isn’t calculated to increase the public’s previously
low opinion of the parsonical profession. The
parson is one speaking with authority. He claims
to be some kind of Chosen Vessel — an individual
specially selected to carry spiritual tidings, and
one more or less inspired. When he is found
making shocking mistakes re small ordinary
matters that any decently-educated person should
know something about, it naturally arouses a
suspicion that he may be a bogus Vessel, and that
if he was any kind of genuine messenger, the
authority which sent him would take more care
to prevent its representative making a fool of
himself and bringing the whole business into
discredit. For instance, that plump and well-
Eaid apostle, Pastor Bkvan, well-known in Sydney,
lathered in this fashion to a Brisbane audience ;
and lie was a fair sample of many dozen others :
No war in the history of the world has ever been so
severe, yet none had had so many mitigations. No war
had ever been closed where the conquered had been given
such fair and just terms. . . . There was no other nation
whose flag gathered with it the freedom and the privi-
leges which honored the British flag. Germany was a
great and civilised nation, but when Germans wished to
colonise they chose British colonies to dwell in. He was
thankful that the British flag was supreme in South
Africa, because he believed that under no other rule
would the uncivilised races of the country be treated with
such justice and humanity. He was aware that the Briton
had a good deal of complacent contempt for everybody
who was not a Briton, but in his dealings with the lower
races he was just, and on the whole he intended to lift
them up. There were some lessons for Australia to learn
in this. Why should the sons of Britain, speaking the
English speech and drawing their inspiration from
English law, deny the liberties they enjoyed to men of
different colour ? There were some men called statesmen
who had hardly the right to be called politicians, who
were placed in spheres where they might exercise mighty
power, but who did not seem to draw their inspiration
from the great founts of British story. Surely the young
men of Australia would not fall into the traps that were
set for them l>y r sixth or even less rate politicians, jour-
nalists, and publicists, but would see that this land of
Australia offered equal liberty and justice to all races.
The poor old Pastorhasevidently never heard of the
Thirty Years War, and a few trifles like that, and
to his narrow intelligence the Boer scramble just
ended is the greatest war of history. He evidently
doesn’t realise that Britain denies all political
liberty and political rights to about 350,000,000
colored men—and he wants Australia to give
“equal liberty and justice to all races,” and
talks of this as following a British precedent.
And it is evidently news to him that the German
immigrant doesn’t make for Greater Britain—that
in the main he avoids it as he would a pestilence.
The figures are available up to 1900 and in that
year, out of 22,309 Teutonic immigrants, about
400 went to British colonies ! Brazil alone re-
ceives about as many Germans as all Greater
together. The British colonies con-
stitute fifth of the globe, but they attract just
about one-fiftieth of German immigration nowa-
days. They attract rather more than the German
settlements—not much more. Moreover, even the
Britisher shows a marked aversion to his. own
colonies; out of 172,000 British and Irish immi-
grants in 1901 only about 60,000, or a little over
one-third, went to British colonies. When Pastor
Bevan, who is just about as inaccurate as the rest
of his tribe, is so miserably incorrect in small
things that can be easily verified, how does he
expect his word to be taken about large things
that can’t be verified —Gehenna, for instance ?
»
Pastor Bevan Again I
Sydney’s old friend, Preacher Bevan, has decided
that the Kanaka is good for Australia, though if
Dr. Bevan seemed to know a little more about
very ordinary current matters his opinion might
be more valuable than it is. In. a recent Jingo
deliverance at Brisbane he told his audience that
German immigrants almost all rushed to British
colonies to get under the blessed flag, and a man
who doesn’t know that only a fraction of either
German or British immigration goes to British
colonies now has something wrong with his educa-
tion. The chubby preacher went up to Cairns
to look at the Kanaka, and he was wildly
astonished to find it raining; he actually
didn’t know that there is a strip of very
wet country on that part of the coast. This again
shows a shocking state of ignorance. Dr. Bevan
was informed by the planters, and accepted the
information as fact, that though the death-rate
among the Kanakas was higher than among
whites, that was quite natural and reasonable ; he
evidently didn’t know that in a community like
that of the Kanakas in Queensland, where infant
mortality is almost wholly eliminated, the death-
rate, other things being equal, should be very
much lower instead of higher. He was told that
the Kanaka, Chinaman, and Jap were all
quiet, law - abiding citizens, and not having
sufficient brilliance to think of looking up the
criminal statistics of the Queensland Government
he passed the ridiculous lie along as solid fact.
Having been duly supplied with knowledge by
“Mr. DE Moleyns, brother of Lady Hopetoun,”
and not having heard any of the other side of the
question Preacher Bevan resolved that “ we need
some of these lower types of labor to build up a new
community like our own, ” though it is safe to bet
that Pastor Bevan couldn’t mention one new com-
munity that was successfully built up on the
use of low types of labor—not even if the informa-
tion would save his collection-plate from being
struck by lightning. Also, he resolved that it was
madness to enforce a White Australia law up
North, in latitude 20—the same latitude as Iquique,
Bulawayo, and the city of Mexico, and further
from the Equator than Lima, Callao, Guayaquil,
Pernambuco, Bahia, Caracas, and many other
places which the white man finds quite habitable.
The awful pastor says there is a “ great lack of
statesmanship” on the part “of the Government
and the parties that press the Government that
they try to compel the North Queenslander to
come under the action of laws similar to the
South.” He actually doesn’t know, or he dis-
honestly suppresses the fact, that North Queens-
land sent a strong anti-Kanaka majority to
the Federal Parliament, and that nobody
is compelling it to “ come under laws
similar to the South ”; it is doing so at
the urgent demand of its own Representatives.
Dr. Bevan admits that he didn’t go to any of the
mining districts —where the white man demon-
strates with his personal pick and shovel that the
White A ustralia d octrin e is practicabl e. And though
he says that the alleged ill-health of the colored
laborer is much exaggerated, it seems he didn’t
think of reading the awful nigger mortality statis-
tics of the Queensland Government; he just in-
quired off the planter, and was much impressed by
the figures “quoted to me” by that interested
party. The strength of the nigger-labor cause
lies in getting hold of fussy, pompous, amiable
old men like the silly Pastor Bevan, who only in-
quire into that side of the case where the wine and
cigars are, and who have spent a long life talking
feebly about Jeroboam till any power of inde-
pendent inquiry they ever possessed has left
them.
