More Reform Programme.
Premier Irvine of Victoria, like his predecessor
Peacock, is understood to favor a reform of the
House of 48 Rich Landlords by making every
person who has a vote for that dreadful body
qualified to sit therein. It isn’t very strongly
suspected that he will do anything, but he
is supposed to see that something should
be done. This is only logical. When
130,000 persons are qualified to vote at Leg.
Council elections and yet only 2000 persons in all
Victoria are qualified to sit in the Council,
when the creature is so much greater than the
creator that he disqualifies 64 out of every 65 of
him, or, in other words, when the creature is 65
times more select than the creator, and when the
haughty 48 Rich Landlords are allowed to declare
over 98 per cent, of their constituents to be too
degraded to associate with them —then there is
assuredly something wrong. Ex-Premier Peacock
realised this. So does Premier Irvine and they
both have some idea about putting it right.
Probably everybody in Victoria, barring the 48
Rich Landlords and Duncan Gillies, recog-
nises it. But there is a long, long way between
recognition and reform. The House of Land-
lords can’t be dissolved, can’t be swamped,
can’t be coerced in any way provided by
the Constitution. If Victoria had been an in-
dependent republic it would probably have long
since disposed of its House of Landlords
by revolution, but nobody ever cared much to
revolushe with a British fleet hanging off the coast
and British troops no further off than Singapore.
Apart from the fact that the Australian never
learned the art of revolution, there is always the
shadow of Imperial authority behind the dreadful
House of 48 Rich Landlords. A strong, fighting
democrat is required to put an end to the incubus
—merely being in favor of amendment counts for
no more in such a case than being in favor of rain
does in drought-time. Assuming that the House
of Landlords is in its usual frame of mind, and
declines to see, in the ordinary course of events,
that it requires removal, there are three possi-
bilities in view. One is plain revolution, as afore-
mentioned, but as there is nobody on hand who
understands the art that idea may be dropped.
Another is by the Assembly declaring its resolve
to go on strike till the Council climbs down —
to pass no estimates, to collect no taxes, to pay
neither the Civil Service, the police, the riiagis-
tracy, the public creditor, nor anybody else—to
close up the schools, the railways, the law courts
and civilisation generally—to let chaos come
again, and the fat man carry his life in his hand,
and the fat man’s tenant refuse to pay rent (for
want of anybody to enforce it), and his debtor
treat him with scorn (for lack of anybody before
whom he can be sued for non-payment), and
generally to shut things up, to turn off the gas,
and to go back to first principles. Probably two
days of the Great National Strike, if anybody
had the courage to strike, would bring the House
of Landlords to reason. A third way is to speak
gently to the 48 Obstructions and include in the
Bill for their abolition a life pension of £5OO or
£lOOO a year for each abolished M. L. C. in recog-
nition (ironically) of his great services. Probably
the Bill would pass then, and the loss could be
more than made good, when the 48 Landlords had
departed, by passing a new land tax law on the
N.S.W. model —a measure which would add,
probably, £lOO,OOO a year to_ the revenue. It
would be worth doing these things to secure the
total abolition of the House of 48 Landlords, and
it would even be worth it to secure an amendment
of that body—if the amendment was large
enough. But it requires a very strong man for
the work. Irvine certainly doesn’t look a strong
man. and Shiels is an amiable flickerer of no
account.
Another “ Solidarity " Party.
Now that N.S.W. has a Country Party in addi-
tion to the previous three, and the Country Party
has a solidarity pledge like that of the Labor
men, things are getting very badly mixed in State
politics. The “ solidarity ” idea is liable to pro-
duce curious results. Under it a party of 27 is
bound to give all its 27 votes in the way that 14
of its members approve, regardless of the opposi ■
tion of the other 13, and a party of 33 must give
its 33 votes as 17 members dictate, however much
the other 16 may boil inwardly. So these peculiar
results are just possible :
In favor Against Votes Vote
of Ministry. for Against
Ministry. Ministry. Ministry.
Country Paity 14 13 27
Labor Party 17 16 33
Cold Tea Prrty 8 5 11
Ministers 5 5
Oppositicn 49 49
42 83 70 49
Under the solidarity arrangement it is quite
possible for there to be only 42 members in the
Assembly who approve of the Government, while
83 disapprove, and yet for the Government to
have a majority of 27. And the defection of three
men might deprive it of 71 votes and place it in a
minority of 115, while the defection of one man
might change the majority of 27 into a shortage of
39. The situation is certainly a ridiculous one.
It might happen, no doubt, that 42 enlightened
patriots were keeping an enlightened Government
in office, against the wish of 83 degraded and
reactionary billet-hunters ; and 42 bridges and
culverts cost less than 63 bridges and culverts—
the number required to buy a majority in the
ordinary way. But on the other hand, a system
by which 42 opponents in a House of 125 can
throw an enlightened Ministry out, brings things
to a dangerous pass. If many more solidarity
parties arrive in politics the election of Ministers
for a fixed term, after the Swiss plan, will become
imperative. The solidarity arrangement, when
brought to perfection, would enable 32 members to
constitute a majority in a House of 125. and would
enable three or four men to turn a House of
almost unanimous supporters into one of almost
unanimous opponents. These things are too
sudden. When even one man could turn an
overwhelmingly big majority into a poor minority,
the present kind of Government—the kind which
can be ejected any day—would have to go home
by back lanes for fear of meeting that man and
being asked for £10,000,000 worth of public works.
A Ministry with a fixed billet for three years could
at least only be effectively squeezed once in three
years, which is something.
o
Symon’s Joke.
The Stmon party in the Federal Senate pegs
away steadily. On almost every item of the tariff
it proposes a reduction of the duty on the ground
that so much more will be imported under the low
duty, and so much less manufactured locally, and
the local Australian industry will be so far re-
tarded, and the Australian so far deprived of em-
ployment, that the low duty will yield as much as,
or more than, the high one. Its plea is that where
a 30 per cent, duty will yield £300,000 (iniports
£1,000,000) a 10 per cent, duty will yield quite as
much because the imports will rise to £3,000,000,
and £2,000,000 more will go out of the country
every year, and the work which the making of
that £2,000,000 worth of goods would afford to
Australians will be lost them. Then the country,
if it is to pay for these extra £2,000,000 of imports
in goods must do it in raw produce the
results of the most disgracefully sweated indus-
tries in Australia —the industries of the “man
on the land.” Symon and his Foreign-trade party
are the enemies of every decently-paid Australian
industry, and their pride when they carry a vote
for the reduction of a duty, and assure the Senate
that the reduction will so greatly depress the local
manufacture of the article, and increase the im-
portation, that the revenue will not suffer at all,
is suggestive of the joy of the gentleman who set
fire to his own capital and discoursed music over
the conflagration. The Symon party is pleasantly
frivolous about human suffering, and takes alight
and cheerful view of other people’s troubles.
Witness this extract from a last week’s debate :
Senator Styles thought that 10 per cent, would wipe
out all the woollen mills. What would happen if all the
mills were closed up ?
Senator Symon : Simply that they would be closed up.
(Laughter.)
The woollen factories of N.S.W. started a gradual
closing-up process as soon as the duty was taken
off, and by 1900 the output had diminished 25 per
cent, and the hands employed had dropped to 221.
A general closing up of the Victorian factories
would throw over 1000 employees out of work. The
N.S.W. policy of borrowing from three to seven
millions a year, in order to supply employment on
public works, can’t be adopted all round, or kept
up for ever, and going on the land yields a wage
of about 15s. a week on an average. Yet Symon
and his crowd laughed cheerfully at the prospect
of depriving all these people of employment. To
the followers of the affluent lawyer, who never
had to go on the land personally for his own crust,
it was rather a pleasant joke than otherwise.
The N.S.W. Censure Debate.
The hollow victory of Premier John See over
Opposition leader Solemn Lee in the recent cen-
sure debate shows nothing more clearly than that
the political power of the daily press of Sydney is
just now under a cloud. Mr. Lee has the unani-
mous support of the three Freetrade dailies, and he
obediently fashioned his programme in accordance
with the behests of the most militant and hysterical
of the three, declaring, for instance, in favor of
reduction of members at its suggestion. But all
in vain. The power that made George Reid
Premier cannot make Solemn Lee Premier. At
the last general election his forces were utterly
routed and no amount of press screaming has since
been able to reduce the two-to-one following of the
See Government. It is obvious, too, from the
result of the division, and from the speeches made
in debate, that the Tory movement for the reduc-
tion of the Assembly, while leaving the nominee
Chamber intact, has no strong popular support.
The Assembly showed itself emphatically opposed
to any reduction of its members, until such time,
at any rate, as a comprehensive Local Govern-
ment Act has been passed ; and this may be taken
as a fair reflection of the opinions of the constitu-
encies, for, despite any daily press representations
to the contrary, the average M.P. is not such a
fool as to fly in the face of the voters on any point
upon which they feel strongly. It may be accepted
as certain that, if the present state of public
opinion continues, the See Government will rot
only hold office throughout the life of this Parlia-
ment, but will carry the country at next general
elections—unless the Labor party, under a capable
leader and with a broadened platform, challenges
its position directly and appeals to the people
under the banner of Financial Reform. A Finan-
cial Reform Party must eventually supplant the
See Government—if not immediately, when there
is the opportunity of retiring voluntarily from an
untenable position of extravagance, then within a
very few years, when the inevitable crash eom/iels
economy. At present, the See Government
is favored by luck. The cessation of the
S’African war has relieved the pressure on the
London money market, and the next big
N.S.W. loan, which must be floated in a little
while, will probably go through successfully.
Probably, too, the big loan after that—a big loan
every six or 12 months is the logical and certain
consequence of an unbridled O’Sullivan —will go
through. But it is almost certain that the third
request for £3,000,000 or £4,000,000 will have a
cold reception—it possibly will be refused ; the
fourth is almost bound to be met with an indig-
nant “No”; and then somebody will have to face
the music. Has the Labor party enough states-
manship to take thought now and face the
situation ere the bailiff actually knocks at the
door ? We appeal to the Labor party because the
See party is hopeless in its extravagance, and the
press-bossed Lee party is hopeless in every way.
♦ .
All through the tariff debate there is one ques-
tion which crops up perpetually and which is
never answered. Reid, Gould, Bruce and
Pumpkin Smith, M'Millan, and Joe Cook were
all members of a Ministry which gave heavy Pro-
tection to N.S.W. newspapers, beer, sugar, and
various other industries. They sacrificed about
£50,000 a year revenue in protecting newspapers
by free postage. They sacrificed £50,000 a year
revenue or more by not making the excise on
local beer as high as the customs duty on imported
beer. They sacrificed about £70,000 a year by not
putting an excise on sugar to counterbalance the
import duty. Also, they sacrificed their alleged
Freetrade principles. Therefore, on the evidence
of their own acts, either these people were telling
a blank untruth when they said they firmly
believed Freetrade to be best for the country, or
else they were traitors deliberately trying to
injure N.S.W. by the partial adoption of a policy
which they knew would do it grievous harm. In
the former case they only call for contempt. In
the latter it is difficult to distinguish between
them and any other traitor.
Chief Justice of S.A. ruled lately that the
word “children” in a proven will does not
include illegitimate sons and daughters, recog-
nised as such by the testator, unless he or she
makes it quite clear that the word was not
restricted to its meaning within the law. The
blessed marriage law was not even made for
women, as is commonly argued. It was made for
“respectable” women only. The testator in the
Adelaide case was a man, but if he had been a
woman and a widow, instead of a widower, the
offspring born out of wedlock would have come
none the better off. A baby begotten through
adultery is a child before God and man, but not
before the Court of Probate. The direct descen-
dant of a spinster, or a widow, whose late husband
is more than nine months dead, cannot claim to
be a child under the mother’s will if she has been
parent to orthodox offspring of subsequent, or
previous, date. The law rises superior even
to the best dictionary authorities as re-
gards the meaning of the word “child.”

If only this law were administered logically, it
would have the blessed effect that Mrs. Grundy
would be denied the satisfaction of proving that
weak, wicked, unmarried creatures of the female
gender are ever guilty of having “a child.” The
Law would dispose of Mrs. Grundy’s base
accusation for all time. The difference between
the married and unmarried patients at Melb.
Women s Hospital would be that Mrs. Smith
can and does have a child, whereas plain
Belinda Brown can’t and doesn’t. The omission
of the matrimonial title from the card hanging
over the back of Belinda’s bed would exone-
rate from all suspicion of being a mother. But,
unfortunately, the law doesn’t go so far, and an
illegitimate child can still be a child when that
fact is of prejudice to it or to its mother. It only
ceases to be a child when it would benefit by being
a child.

♦
Sydney gave an enthusiastic reception to
Nellie Stewart and her stupendous hat—it looked
like the front wheel of the old velocipede with
feathers in it—when they returned to play “Sweet
Nell of Old Drury ”at the Royal. Trie drama is
partly historical, partly unhistorical, and partly
a proof that virtue is a very bad spec. It shows
how an honest orange-girl, with toed stockings
which don’t look at all like bare feet, by dint of
her beauty, courage, wit and kindness of heart,
fell upwards to be a king’s Flossie, and became rich,
benevolent, immoral, and universally admired. She
did much good with the money she acquired by
b“ing the king’s Flossie, and the influence that she
wouldn’t have possessed over that bad, blackish-
brown man if she had been a better woman
w.is used for good purposes. The uncertain heart
and the dazzling, toothy smile of the brownish-
blaokish king are striven for by three women—
two bad-bad women and one good-bad woman,
and the latter wins. She is the centre of a rather
well-constructed story, which is built up with a
vast disregard of facts, dates, and possibilities.
Long before the drama opens old Baronet Fairfax
walloped Jeffreys, afterwards Chief Justice.
Jeffreys darkly made a note of the fact, and took
the old baronet’s number for future reference.
Patiently and thoughtfully he executes the old
man, procures the exile of his son, who is
Nell G-wynno’s benefactor, drives his own ward
(who is Fairfax’s betrothed) to agree to a
marriage with the Earl of Rochester, runs
down the returned exile in Nell Gwynne’s
house and persuades the king that he is Nell’s
lover, gets Nell dropped with a thud from the
royal favor and the bright front tooth of Charles’s
smile diverted from her, sentences the young man
o'f-handedly to execution, takes possession of the
letters which would have proved to the king that
Fairfax really returned to see the other girl and
wasn’tstealing the royal Flossie, persuades the other
girl into an immediate marriage with the awful
Rochester by promising to release her lover ; and
then laughs a horrid, pock-marked, cross-eyed
laugh at the success of his scheme. And then,
while Jeffreys is out drinking, the Whole glittering
success falls through. Being an untidy Judge,
lie leaves the door and the box-lid both open
when he goes out. Nell Gwynne enters, disguises
herself in his clothes, and looks sufficiently like
him in the dusk to give orders for the release of
the imprisoned girl, to start her off with her tale
to the palace, to intercept Jeffreys’ correspon-
dence, and to collect evidence which bursts up the
show. It is a solemn thing to reflect upon, that
if Jeffreys had been sober and Nell Gwynne had
been respectable Roger Fairfax would have died,
and Rochester would have taken Roger’s best girl
to his den and there eaten her at leisure.
* • * *
Jeffreys, as a matter of fact, was only 35
when Charles 11. died, and he wasn’t a lord during
Charles’s lifetime. At the date of the present
play he was a splendidly handsome man of about
28 or 30, instead of the elderly and uncomely
ruffian of the drama, and unless Fairfax, senior,
spanked him in infancy the alleged assault was
practically impossible. Albert Gran probably
makes the Judge all that the author intended—
but he isn’t the real Jeffreys by miles. Harcourt
Beatty’s Charles 11. is a picturesque and regal-
looking character, with a tendency to staginess
here and there, but a very lovable king none the
less. This dramatic Charles is possessed of
beautiful front teeth and a desire to do good to his
country without knowing how. He is a mighty
loafer before the Lord—a kindly lier-down, and a
tired and reluctant sitter-up, and a well-intentioned
leaner against anything that will support him. He
is troubled inwardly by conflicting emotions and
dragged outwardly by three contending Flossies.
He wants to do justice, but can’t find it. He dis-
bkes bloodshed, and appeals gloomily to his dog
tor advice when his signature is wanted on death-
warrants. The real Charles was nothing like him,
but the stage Charles is a pleasant rake and an
honourable stealer of other men’s wives and
daughters, and Harcourt Beatty does respectable
justice to the character.
* * • •
As to Nell Gwynne, she ranks among
Nellie Stewart’s big successes. She is first a poor
and allegedly bare-footed orange-girl in toed stock-
ings who shakes the King in Drury Lane —why,
considering the undoubted attractions of Miss
Stewart’s bare feet, she should disguise them in
toed stockings is a mystery. Later she is a dash-
ing, beautiful and beautifully-dressed courtesan —
the weary King’s good angel, and the benefactor
of everybody. In an age when ladies seem to
have been unspeakably rude to each other in the
drawing-room she smothers the two other bad
women and sends them away broken up. Nellie
Stewart, with her wonderful vitality, plays the
character in a whirl of energy, beauty, jewels and
hair, and makes the play go with a rush all the
time. A few other persons who deserve a more
extended notice are held over till next week. It
is a gpod company, taken all round, and "Sweet
Nell’’ is a pleasant, though not very strong play.
Also, it is well-staged, and the management has
postumed it with a patient attention to the wigs
and coats of 240 years ago that is wholly com
mendable.

“ Facing the Music,” that quaint story of
the tempestuous house of Smith, has ««*Med down
to its stride at Sydney Palace, and all the members
of the company show a trifle better than on
opening night, The piece only goes, more slowly
because the players have to stop awhile occasion-

** In London Town ” is in its third week
at Sydney Lyceum. The gentleman whom it is
etiquette to refer to as the Genial Bland keeps the
play going as a b’uff sailor man who is supposed
t 0 a ff re at deal of affection for his mother,
and Ins regard for that aged relative is strongly
insisted on, though, as lie fails to hunt her up till
financial distress has landed her in the workhouse,
it isn t a very practical emotion. A pantomime
scene between Bland and the other man
w .hp also seeks the hand of the fair
pickle factory-girl wins loud approval from
the gods, but the dress circle, which doesn’t
propose in that fashion, looks dubious and
almost resentful. In general, however, it is the
Beenes from the slummy side of London life and
i? e P] ckle -f ac t°ry element, and the atmosphere of
the bloke and the back alley that give the drama
its chief claim to existence. Apart from them it
drags somewhat. One act is mainly filled by
people coming on in a motiveless manner, and
going off for the same reason, without advancing
the plot to any extent worth describing.
*# # *
The Woods- Williamson co. thought better,
or worse, of its intention to displace “ A Woman’s
Sin” after its first week with “Barabbas,” which
is an account of a man’s sin, with some feminine
shortcomings thrown in. Accordingly, Miss
Williamson’s version of “ The Manxman ” shows
up the foolishness of its heroine for one week
longer. The lady who respects her husband so
much that she feels it almost a sacred duty to
elope with someone else is convincingly enough
played by Miss Williamson, and the anguish
in her mind when she discovers that she cannot
love Pete, and that therefore it would be “ brazen ”
for her to live with him any longer, is a very good
style of anguish in its way. The old-fashioned
“ East Lynne ” type of eloping wife eloped from
straight and simple affection for the villain. The
modern wife goes astray because of the purity of
her soul. _ However, “Barabbas” begins its more
or less glittering career this Saturday, and a few
heathen dances are to enliven the early Christian
gloom of the drama.
* * *
Sydney Tivoli programme has been
materially strengthened by items from Miss Ray
Jones, a pleasant and bright singer, and the re-
appearance of the Lenton trio—acrobats and
comedians. Harris and Walters have departed,
which is a blessed circumstance; and no one
regrets the absence of the violent Irish comedians.
** * #
The Players Club was founded by Mrs.
Philip Lytton, once known in the profession as
Madge Hope ; and its threefold object is to bring
amateur theatricals in Sydney to a high standard
of excellence, to afford amusement at metropolitan
and suburban hospitals, and to assist any deserving
charity whatever. Judging by the recent per-
formance at the Theatre Royal the first object has
been achieved, for the principals in Pinero’s
delightful ‘ Weaker Sex ” gave a better perform-
ance than many that have held the boards in
Sydney theatres for weeks at big prices. It was
absolutely the best amateur performance this
writer has seen. Still, the Players Club is com-
posed largely of ex-professionals who have taken
t°_ juggling with business and finance and other
things for a living, but who can’t help breaking
out in dramatic outbursts occasionally, and these
professionals, being rested, give better perform-
ances than they used to do when they followed
the Hamlet’s ghost industry at full speed for a
hying. The Club’s Dramatic Studio is in the
Equitable Buildings, George street. Another
interesting feature about the production was that
the whole of the orchestral part of the show was
carried out by the Sydney Amateur Mandoline
Society under the conductorship of Mr. Robert
Francis Peel, and was very successful, there being
a total absence of the crash-bang horror that even
the best of Australian orchestras seem addicted
to.
#* * #
“ The King of Crime,” who was known as
“A King of Crime” when he first waved his
sceptre over this continent, is again throttling
Simon Iscariot at Melh. Royal with complete
success. Simon, whom the King calls Judas, sits
on the same old convenient seat where he sat
eight years ago in order that two vindictive hands
may steal forth from behind the curtains and
drag him to his ,doom when the clock strikes
twelve. The awful treachery for which Simon
comes to grief is connected with a dead miser in
the first act and a ditto blind lady in the third
act, the blind lady having perished casually
amidst a snow storm when the King, her husband,
was striking attitudes outside the Golden Pig hotel.
The clock and the King are the most striking
features of this festive horror. Next to them in
importance comes the blind lady, and an elderly
female of bad character who drops a packet of
snuff in her rabbit-pie, and consoles the consumer
with drugged brandy. Also, the innocent ward
of the miser and her brave lieutenant rescue one
another occasionally, whilst the hero of the snuffed
pie dodges around the Golden Pig, pursued by a
small' but virtuous soubrette from a rival pub.
Finally there is a ballet of many colors. The
ballet dances a can-can, and is invited to
drink at the expense of Judas, whereupon a
member of the brotherhood tears the mask
from the Betrayer’s face, and the girls repudiate
the refreshments with scorn. Shortly after which
the clock chimes midnight, and the hands of ven-
geance clutch their victim as aforesaid, and the
King, being shot down by gendarmes, dies happy
in the thought that his blind angel wife will
admire him for not allowing the wrong man to get
guillotined. Ihe spectacle of the King expiring in
the limelight to slow music, applauded by a
regretful gallery, is almost as impressive as the
incident of the Hands—but not quite.

“Dorothy,” at Her Majesty’s, seemed to
resume business on Saturday night at the point
where it was interrupted six weeks before. A
restoration of anything after a lapse of only a few
weeks is apt to be one of the saddest experiences
in theatrical management, but “ Dorothy ”i 3 a
charming musical exception which proves the rule.
Melbourne was moderately enthusiastic over the
show that adorned the tail end of the previous
comic opera season, and now the people who
missed the revival in its early freshness are glad
to have it warmed up for them. Florence Young’s
performance, always the predominant part of the
proceedings, has gone from good to better, and if
theother voices hang out signals of distress, as some-
times happens, Florence sails in with her top note
spread to the wind and gets them off the rocks
every time. Also Lauri— the Lauri, whose other
name is George—supplies a public want by being
torn to tatters, or getting drunk and falling down-
stairs. And the chorus necessarily improves.
The “Dorothy ” chorus at the outset was so rough
and unready that the improvement sounds excep-
tional. Next Saturday (illst) “ Florodora ” will be
revived, with a new baritone, Harold Thorley, as
the noble lord who yearns for 0 his Dolores, and
Miss Palotta, for the last times, as the auntie of
that rash young man.
** * *
Talking of choruses, the background mem-
bers of the “Thirty Thieves” co. deserve a
special vote of thanks. Only a first-class force of
voices could have carried Musgrove’s show into
its fourth week, and if the singers, as a body, were
not almost equal to the splendor of their clothes
the music would not make them appear quite as
important as they are.
»* * *
At Melb. Opera House, the excellent
broad comedy sketch by Tom and Mrs. Nawn is
an effective contrast to the joke in one act which
Mr. and Mrs. Sidney Drew introduced to Aus-
tralia last week. The new arrivals play a smart
little up-to-date reminder of “The Taming of the
Shrew.” Mrs. D. is a buxom bride, who has
started her honeymoon with a toy terrier in her
lap and a ruminative husband at her elbow. He
desires to adore his Cassandra, but she dotes on
the dawg. She dotes on so furiously that her
other darling—the two-legged one—goes mad in
self-defence, and reduces Cassandra to subjection.
Mrs. Drew shines brightest as an actress when
she is bossing the situation, but the less-imposing
Drew is at his best before he waxes frantic.
* * *
The World’s Entertainers at Melb. Bijou
are pretty much as they were in Sydney what
time Salerno and Bunth and Rudd had front
places on the programme. Take it altogether, the
Bijou show, in its reconstructed form, is All Right,
though there are moments of depression, when one
wishes things were different.

The Broughs have ended their season at
Adelaide Royal in a tumult of popular apprecia-
tion. After putting up four new plays they ran
through all the old favorites, playing “Mrs.
Tanqueray,” “The Village Priest,’ and “Niohe,”
on consecutive nights, as a final effort of versa-
tility. With the last-mmed they said farewell to
the small city, which claims to have always sup-
ported them more heartily than either Melbourne
or Sydney. _ Cecil Ward is to leave them shortly,
and Lovell is on his way from London to replace
him. Willoughby and co. have opened at the
Tivoli in “The Wrong Mr. Wright” to big
houses.
The Wild Cat Column.
Federal Treasurer Turner, being more honest
and more anxious to do the right thihg.than any of this
country’s State Treasurers, announces that every Federal
loan issued under his supervision will have a 2 per cent,
sinking fund attached. This will be sufficient to pay the
loan off in about 30 years. Of course, there is no guaran-
tee that future Treasurers won’t cut down the sinking
fund on future loans to 1 per cent, as the Act allows.
And there is no guarantee that the works to be paid for
by loans will last anything like 20 years ; for instance,
part of the first loan is for anew telephone switchboard
at Sydney G.P.O. to replace one—also built with loan
money and not paid for—which has become obsolete in
about two years. Probably, partly through wear-and-
tear, and partly through the progress of science, everv-
thing that is built out of the first loan will be dead or out
of date a dozen years hence.
Treasurer Turner claims that he has fixed things
so rigidly by his Bill that the sinking funds can’t be
suspended or tampered with. But even if that were
so, the loan game doesn’t pay. Suppose the Common-
wealth borrows £500,000 a year regularly—it will probably
borrow much more by-and-bye, but £500,000 a year is
talked of at present, and that sum serves as well for pur-
poses of illustration as any other. Allowing for discount
on 3 per cent, loans, cost of management, accrued interest
thrown in, and sundries, the Commonwealth will do
amazingly well if it gets its money at a fair average rate
of 3| per cent. So this is the drain on the public revenue,
assuming (1) that the Commonwealth borrows £SOO 000
every year, and (2) that it pays the £500,000 a year out of
revenue and borrows nothing :

That i 3 to say, supposing the Commonwealth settles down
to borrow £500,000 a year on the proposed very low terms
it will save a good deal tor 10 years and a little up to 19
years. Then it begins to pay more than if it had never
borrowed at all, and the borrowing business becomes a
dead loss. The loss increases till it reaches £287,500
per annum, and it remains at that for ever
afterwards. The saving is very, very temporary and
the after loss is permanent. Of course, however, the
sinking fund business won’t last. The clause will be
repealed when the burden grows heavy, and new loans
issued with no sinking fund. Possibly'also, as in Maori-
land, it will be decided some day, not'onlv to issue future
1 >ans without a sinking fund, but also to'pay the sinking
funds attached to the old loans out of borrowed money.
Besides, a time always comes when the Treasurer begins
to remark that as he has paid £250,000 off the old loans
this year his deficit of £251,374 isn’t really a matter of
any importance and the sinking fund goes to wreck in-
dir 'ctly. The Federal Parliament has unlimited powers
of taxation, so it is no earthly use saving it can’t raise the
money. All it wants is ttie erit to face the si nation.
The country has six loan-floating Governments already ;
it doesn’t want seven loan-floating Governments—for less
than 4,000.000 people.
• • • *
The Queensland Government proposes to scrape
up some extra revenue by a big increase of railway fares.
Heavy direct .taxation would be a great deal more
reliable, especially as regards the Brisbane suburban
railways, where increased fares will only drive business to
the syndicate-owned trams. Queensland has had some
good years of late, and if in the good vears it had exerted
itself to pay off its old deficits and save the interest
charge they involve, the bad years wouldn’t be nearly so
bad. Unfortunately Australia borrows in bad years to
tide over the trouble, and borrows in good years because
its prosperity “ justifies it in increasing its liabilities.”
And after each depression it rises again—only every time
it rises with more difficulty and with a bigger dead lift,
like a waterlogged ship whose burden is getting heavier
every hour.

* * * *
G-oldsbrough, Mort and Cos. is now trading on
£558,995 of its own capital and nearly £2,000,000 borrowed
on debentures. It will never be really safe until, by the
gradual accumulation of profits, it has paid off a great
part of this debt and become a concern trading much
more extensively on its own fends. The top-heavy mass
of borrowed money is always a danger. As regards the
disasters which nearly destroyed the co., and which
swallowed up so enormous a proportion of its funds, it is
not likely that it, or any other big pastoral co., will invite
a repetition of them by making advances in the drought
country again. The unreliability of that dreadful land,
where the “great national industry” of Australia has
only been kept alive by defrauding banks and financial
institutions, is pretty well known now. The Freetrade
party may still describe sheep-rearing in the West as a
“ natural industry,” as distinguished from the artificial,
but honest job of making protected boots or steel rails in
a town, but so far it has required much more coddling,
bonuses and extraneous aid to keep the Western pastoral
industry going than would have been needed to start a 1
iron industry for the supply of all Australia. And tl e e
is little save bones, lunatics and broken banks to sl.o
for all the money that has been sunk.
* * ♦ .
The N.S.W. Government has circularised the
unpaid property-holders in the resumed area of Sydney
inviting them to leave their purchase money on deposit
at the Treasury for (hree or five years at 4 per cent,
interest. In other words, the Government wants to bor-
row the money from them at 4 per cent. This is a pretty
steep rate, but during May only £9475 was raised locally
by the sale of 3J per cent, stock, and it is impossible to
go on the London market for another loan just yet. The
last loan was almost all, if not all, spent iri advance. It
was just about suftkient to replace the borrowed trust
funds, and though, these having been replaced, the
process of borrowing them can begin again, still there is
a lot of money still wanted to pay for the resumed area.
It would have been better if the resumptions had
been compulsorily paid for in 3* per cent. Govern-
ment securities, but it is too late to think of that
now. If the present device brings in one or two
or three millions it will stave off most of th ■ trouble
for three or five years. Presumably the amount of
these deposit receipts will be included in the total of the
public debt, though there are a lot of sums owing—over-
drafts, deficits, suspense accounts, loans from trust funds
and the like—which are often not includ and when the
alleged total of the debt is stated. The rents collected
by the Government up to date having been dishonestly
used as revenue, the interest due up to date will, of
course, be added to the principal of the debt. And if the
resumed area yields more in rent than it costs in interest
on borrowed money—well, nobody but a fool would
suspect See and O’Sullivan of any intention of putting
the surplus to a sinking fund to repay the debt. See and
O’Sullivan are not built that way—and the spectacle of
N.S.W. trying to get “ deposits ” at 4 per cent, for three
years, when the banks only pay 3J per cent, is one of the
results of See and O’Sullivan.

It would be interesting to know how old a
railway has to be before it is expected to absolutely
maintain itself, and ceases to have any of its small
charges debited to capital and paid out of loan funds.
Even when a line has become ancient history charges
connected with it seem to be still debited to loa'ns, and it
is expected to earn interest upon an ever-increasing
capital. For instance, take the following N.S.W.
samples :

These are a few samples taken at random from the list of
non-paying railways—but all the railways, paving and
non-paying, are in the same situation. Even when a line
like the Blacktown-Riehmond one, has been in existence
for 36 years the expenditure of borrowed money upon it
still goes on. Some of these lines will have’an awful
amount of loan capital to earn interest upon in another
century. Surely there must be some date in a railway’s
history after which all further alterations, repairs and
charges should be paid out of earnings.

* • * *
The Wilfley Concentrator, which was patented
and introduced to Australia some three years ago, has
met with the most phenomenal success, and is now to be
found in almost every up-to-date mill in all the States,
particularly Kalgoorlie and Broken Hill, where every
mine of importance is using them. Similar success has
been met with in every mining centre of the world, some
5000 of the tables having been sold. Its success has led
to numerous imitations and infringements of the Wilfley
patent. Lately the Company obtained an injunction and
damages against the Cammett machine and users thereof,
their patents being confirmed by the American Supreme
Court, and other of the imitations have since been taken
off the market. The Syndicate owning the patents in
Australia has decided to prosecute all infringements, and
action has now been taken in this direction in Victoria.
Users being equally liable with vendors of infringements,
they should exercise caution in dealing only with the
sole manufacturers, the Austral Otis Engineering
Company, South Melbourne, or their authorised agents.*

The Adelaide insurance co.’s are indignant with
the local Fire Brigade superintendent for asserting that
he has not enough men and appliances to cope with a big
conflagration. “ The stronger we make the brigade,”
they protest, “ the less people will he inclined to insure
and' the lower the premium rates will have to be.” They
complain that though they pay four-ninths of the cost of
the brigade they have only two men on the board of six
members.

* * * •
Meat of all kinds is rapidly getting to famine
prices in Queensland. The drought is blamed for it all,
but one big factor is that, for the past 30 months, huge
shipments at “sweet” prices have been sent to feed
T. Atkins and co. at the Cape. One big firm must have
cleared colossal profits, as their buyers bought six months
ahead at bedrock prices for cash, and in many cases the
result panned out cent, per cent.
For 37 years the Bank of Adelaide has kept its
directors’ fees at £loooa.vear; but now, with a London
office, a reserve equal to 50 per cent, on the capital, and
an increase of 100 per cent, in the business inside 10
years, the shareholders have sprung the allowance to
£ISOO. A suggestion to make the pay follow the divs.
was wisely jumped on, for in times of stress when profits
are down'to vanishing point, the average board has most
to do.
* * • •
Mr. Collins, a mining engineer, well known in
Bathurst district (N.S.W.) —not Frank Gardner’s Sydney
partner, the “Major”—has written an exhaustive paper
on Hill End goldfield, which was read at a recent meeting
of the Institute of Mining Engineers, London. Mr.
Collins works on extensive data recorded by his father,
who managed mines at Hill End. On the facts and plans
submitted and the conclusions drawn from them it would
seem that the late Duke of Manchester made a serious
mistake when he closed down a local mine which was
being worked by an English co. According to plans em-
bodied in Mr. Collins’ paper the co. had less than 150 ft. to
drive at the bottom level to intersect the belt of slates in
which the bonanzas were found in shallower ground.
This belt of slates was thrown out of its supposed course
by a slide, the existence of which had not been observed,
or, if observed, had been ignored, and thus the ex-
tensive exploratory work done by the English co. never
reached the crucial point. Mr. Collins’ paper is most cir-
cumstantial, and now that Govt, is talking about a pro-
ject to further test Hill End should command attention
and investigation. The best returns were got from
Hawkins’ Hill in 1873, when Krohmann crushed 436 tons
for 24,0790 z., and Beyers and Holtermann 415 tons for
16,2790 z. Poor, good-hearted Beyers ! Y\ riter met him
not long since in W.A. where he was eking out a miser-
able existence at dry-blowing. What a shocking contrast
to the dapper, frock-coated belltoppered man who used
to smile in N.S.W. Leg. Assembly! Beyers made only
one speech during a long Parliamentary career, and a
Sydney paper, which is always boasting of British fair-
play, in execrable taste reported the incoherent, almost
incomprehensibly-broken English, verbatim.
* * • •
Lobbs’Hole copperfield, Kiandra (N.S.W.), is
a remarkable one, and will be heard of to advantage
when Minister O’Sullivan can spare the money to make a
dray-track from the mountain heights to the valley. The
“ Hole ” is surrounded by precipitous cliffs from 2000 to
3000 ft. high. The Yarrangobiily River has cut a narrow
gorge, and mining operations are carried on right in the
bed of the stream, the working shafts being on the banks
just above high water mark. The overhanging cliffs are
composed of layers of conglomerate, limestone, and
breccia, and theriver has eroded thesedown to a bed of slate,
in which the copper lodes occur. The ore is theoretically
pure chalcopyrites, the general assay being about 33J per
cent, copper. Reeckmann and Forstrom have in a very
short time consigned about 500 tons of ore, which has
averaged 30 p.c. Last week they cut the lede 50ft. below
previous workings and found it 4ft. wide of solid mineral,
assaying nearlv 34 per cent. Despite the low price of
copper and many difficulties, the party are still consign-
ing ore at a big profit. There is only one other copper-
mine in N.S.W. of which this can be said. Other, parlies
are working on’parallel veins equally rich. The drawback
to the field is that ore has to be sent from the mines, and
supplies to them, on pack-horses for a distance of three
miles, and then for 10 miles the murmurings of the forest
are mingled with bullock-punchers’ prayers as they per-
suade their teams through a slough of snow and slush.
Once Tumut road is reached all is easy going. A route
for a road, with a fair grade to the mines, has been sur-
veyed, and The O’Sullivan has promised to have the work
done “as soon as funds are available.” Let him accom-
plish it, and he will, for a certainty, be immortalised in a
brass monument facing the Yarrangobiily cliffs. The
mine-owners will promise this.

“Jackar”: Be “Cobarite” (8., 3/5/’02): I
know nothing as to his alleged facts, but can endorse his
general contention that some of the mines’ inspectors
make most unreasonable demands on mine-ovvners and
put them to needless expense and annoyance without en-
suring the slightest extra protection to workmen. Here
is a case. A syndicate composed of M’s.P. managed to
secure a promising “block” claim. They sunk a shaft
170 ft. without a stick of timber, and their hauling appli-
ance was a windlass, the handles of which tvere not pro-
perly centred in the barrel. Fancy it! Men working in
a shaft 170 ft. deep under a lopsided-windlass ! But never
a word of protest was therefrom any inspector. M’s.P.
tired of forking out “pay” instead of drawing it in, and
abandoned the claim. Another party took it up and
erected a steam-winder. When they got down the shaft
they found it so crooked (it’s only a coincidence that it
was a Parliamentary shaft) that it had veered round from
East and West to North and South. It cost them £3O
to square it—the shaft. They intended to sink 30 ft. or
so in the hope of cutting the lode, and had just started
when an inspector came along and peremptorily ordered
them to stop sinking and not dare to proceed again
until they had divided the shaft and put in skids to
guide the'bucket. This was done at a cost of over £IOO.
Shaft sunk 30 ft., lode not found, claim again
abandoned. Thus this inspector compelled the pros-
pectors to spend more on unnecessary work than it would
have cost them to do their test in a perfectly safe way.
Yet work carried on in the same shaft, under absolutely
unsafe conditions, when owned by M’s.P. was somehow or
other not noticed 1 X have heard many strong oaths
about this business. You may have as many Bible oaths
as you may want as to the truth of every syllable of the
statement. Another fact. When a certain inspector
approaches a set of workmen his first question is “ day'
work, or contract, bovs ?.” If the men reply “daywork ”
he says “allright, boys, spell-ho! ” and proceeds to
discuss things in general with them. More Bible oaths,
if necessary.
* * * *
At various times leases have been taken up on
the Barrier (N.S.W.) for silver, lead, zinc, copper, tin,
gold, antimony, asbestos, cinnabar, iron, manganese,
bismuth, wolfram, cobalt and platinum, and now applica-
tions have gone in for leases for phosphoric and tungstic
acid. An Adelaide syndicate is hunting up these possible
sources for the supply of artificial manures, in which
there must be a big trade if S.A., with her easily-
impoverished land, is to stick to agriculture. The B. H.
Junction board takes recent good advice and cuts ex-
penses to the hone. Directors’ fees have been reduced
by half, and office-expenses come down by still more.
The South still does first-rate work, as concen-
trating work goes. It puts through now over 3000
tons of ore a. week, besides about 500 tons of old
tailings. The ore averages 18'2 per cent, lead, B£oz.
silver, and 17'8 per cent, zinc ; the tailings 20'7 per
cent, lead, 3'2 oz. silver, and 23*2 per cent. zmc.
The concentrated product (say, 725 tons) averages 64;8
per cent., lead and 220 z. silver, and 9*6 per cent, zinc is
still left In. They go on developing very big bodies in the
deep levels. Silver sticks below 25., and, according to
the co.’s advisers, it oughtn’t. The only explanation
offering is that the collection of the China war indemnity
and the re-distribution of the millions of taels which that
represents, is, for the time being, keeping this big buyer
off the market. If this is so, this cause will probably not
last long, because mere re-distribution will not supply
the new coin for which a growing people that counts its
wealth in silver is always calling.

As Adelaide is keen on tin shows it will most
likely be tried again with some amalgamated properties
at Euriowie and Poolamacca, on the outskirts of the
Barrier. Some of the early Broken Hill men dropped a
pot of money here 11 or 12 years ago, and to this day
most of them don’t know how they did it, as there is
plenty of good tin stone there, easily dressed. The
Tarrawingee railway, which goes out most of the way to
the fields, will give them a chance now that they did not
have before. But the people who are “ mashed on ”
tin had best find out something about their
charmer before they do the honest thing with her. Any-
how, to drop the girl-business, they must not figure on a
steady market at £l3O or more. In the last half-century
this is by far the top price ; between ’BB and ’9B, including
both, no year’s average was up to £IOO, and ’9B saw it as
low as £59Jk Certainly, while the price has been runningup
these last few years, “ visible ” stocks have been running
down; Sargaiit and Cos., the London tin-men, give the
figures for ’97 as 40,744 tons, with a regular fall to 20,703
tons last year. But the heathen Chinee holds most of the
strings of this market, and probably this is another of the
games he doesn’t understand. And the strings that the
Chinee don’t hold the Dutchmen hold, and they don’t
understand the game either.
• • • •
Amid all the depressing circumstances of
drought-time in N.S.W. the coal-mining industry shines
out as one of the few steadil.v-profitable of this State’s
undertakings. There are practically no unemployed coal-
miners or coal-mines in N.S.W. now, and fresh pits are
being opened up to meet the ever-increasing demand.
The impetus which the Federal tariff will give to manu-
factures promises a great local expansion of the coal
industry ; and to the colliery-owner the local is infinitely
more profitable than the foreign market.

Elliott Diamond Cos. (N.S.W.) : Reconstruction
scheme a gem of the first water—loo,ooo shares, 67,000
fully paid and 33,000 with a liability of 2s. per share.
Paid-up holders in old co. continue in that status; old
contributors still to find the cash with the prospective
solatium that they are to have preference in divs. to the
extent of 3d. per share. They are also told that “it is
confidently expected the continuance of operations for
three or four months at most will prove the mine and
poxsibly discover the rich run of wash opened up in the
adjoining leases.” The probability of anything payable,
for a co., being found in the district is very remote. But
assuming that the chances were otherwise why should not
the paid up holders in the old Elliott co. chip in now and
pay their share towards proving the mine ?

Sydney Harbor Collieries: Coal-seams struck in
shaft at nearly 3000 ft. now being driven on and gradually
increasing in size, with every indication of the shale band
which separates them forming into coal. The coal is
of excellent quality—about 17 per cent, of hydrocarbons,
72J per cent, of fixed carbons and 9 per cent. ash. This
is one of the pluckiest enterprises ever undertaken in
Australia.

showing. Reported that London directors have made

are pending for amalgamation with Baker’s Creek. This
would be a capital thing for the latter, but there’s not
much likelihood of its coming off.

Justice Kekewich, in London Chancery Court,
decided that Whitaker Wright is liable to refund moneys
subscribed by public to the Standard Exploration Cos. on
the ground that prospectus issued under Wright’s
auspices “ was not only misleading but absolutely false.”
Asco. had capital of £1,500,000, and the case decided will
be taken as a precedent, Wright will have a disgorgeous
time. Four hundred other cases already “ listed.”

made net profit (after writing off £77,011 for deprecia-
tion and £30,825 cost of new 40 stamps) of £154,020. Out
of this, 13s. a share, or £130,000, was distributed. Co.’s
position, financially and otherwise, very substantial.

Golden Horseshoe’s last balance-sheet showed
net profit (including amount brought forward) of
£424,566. From this 235. per share, or £345,000, was dis-
tributed, £BOOO set aside to form a debenture-redemption
fund, and £70,000 carried forward. Cos. wrote off no less
than £94,566 for depreciation, &c. Total costs (including
depreciation) works out at 645. 6d. a ton. Mine looks
splendid, and new battery now running well. £50,000
debentures to pay for new battery are redeemable in
three years.

course of erection on Flagstaff (Coolgardie) and Eclipse

Golden Pole (W.A.) last crushing shows splen-
did return of 12390 z. from 420 tons. Capital will, of
course, be made of this to off-load shares in adjoining
Waihi on the unwary. As properties are on parallel belts
of country, reefs have no connection with each other.
Golden Pole’s reef averages 3ft. thick of solid quartz,
underlying east, while Waihi’s ground is intersected with
narrow leaders, underlying anyhow, and many a call will
be made before any return eventuates. Golden Pole,
after its successful clean-up, asked the Government to
grant it a subsidy to sink for water. Request politely
refused. It was altogether too modest. 00. ought to
have asked Government to erect 30-head battery for
it.
• • • •
All sorts of rumors are being freely circulated
of booming Bay ley’s (W.A.) scrip. Management, to its
credit, has officially notified that the rumors are baseless,
the only change in the mine being that the stone in the
deep level is of “ good-looking ” quality, but would be all
the better if it carried gold. Bayley’s is, or has been,
nothing but a bonanza mine. It may produce gold by
the ton one week, and not by the grain the next.
• • • •
Bears have been having a great innings with
Cosmopolitans (W.A.) lately, and banged the shares by
circulating all sorts of damaging reports of flooding,
petering out, and losing underlay to Cumberland. Free
access to mine is allowed to press reporters and bona-fide
inquirers. Main underlay shaft is down 660 ft., at angle
of 45deg., carrying reef all the way. Levels driven at
320, 450, and 500 ft., for distances of 630, 850, and 220 ft.
respectively. At 500 ft. level, reef averages_l4ft., half of
which is worth 30dwt. Cumberland, adjoining on North,
recently cut out the strike, not the underlay, of the reef,
which rather enhances position of both mines. Co.’s
400,000 shares are badly held and weakly supported, and,
owing to the unlikelihood of early divs., subject to be
hammered by bears on a weak market. In consequence,
shares subject to frequent fluctuations. In further
sinking, the volume of water tends to increase. Air
pumps are utilised, and there has been some difficulty in
supplying both pumps and rock-drills with sufficient air.
Another compressor is being installed, and an additional
pump has been ordered, so that the difficulty will shortly
be fully overcome The levels now being opened up are
looking extremely well.
• * • *
Cumberland-Niagara (W.A.), whose cutting of
the continuation of the Englishman lode has created such
a scare among holders of Cosmopolitan, has 84 acres on
north of latter property. Cos. owns 20-stamp mill, and
has been crushing since March, 1899, the ore averaging
13dwts. April’s return jumped up to 23dwt. per ton,
actual figures being 8390 z. from 718 tons, the result of
cutting the Englishman reef. Shares have hitherto been
practically unsaleable, but may appear in the active list
in future.
• • • •
Associated Northern continues to develop very
rich ore in various parts of the property, especially along
the Oroya boundary. Prof. Geerzel has been engaged to
classify the ores from eastern borehole. For some un-
known reason shipment of rich ore to smelters has been
suspended, although quantity continues to accumulate.
Work on the Iron Duke chute in Oroya (W.A.)
North block still being vigorously prosecuted, and chute
already penetrated for 40ft., and face still in rich ore.
All ore as broken is being bagged for shipment to smelters,
and a large parcel of 6 to 18oz. ore may be expected to be
forwarded for treatment shortly. From its outcrop on
llannan’s Brown Hill this rich chute has now been proved
to have continuous course of about 2600 ft., the longest
run of rich ore known on the fields. Cos. consists of
250,000 shares, of which 236,000 have been issued. On
present developments co. should reach strong financial
position ere long. , , *
After years of obsolete mining practice, instal-
ling of obsolete machinery, and sinking of obsolete
small shafts, chairman Govett, of Ivanhoe (W.A.) mine,
is going to take the lead and adopt modern Transvaal
methods. Two to three-ton self-tipping skips are to
replace the 10 to 15cwt. trucks and bracemen. Obsolete
60-head battery to be gradually replaced by up-to-date
plant. Melb. office to be closed. Perth mint to be
patronised instead of London. Reduction in price of
dynamite to be fought for. Bearer warrants to be issued
to save cost of transfer stamps and fees. Secrecy to be
totally abolished. New system of sloping to be intro-
duced. Electricity to be installed. Costs to be pub-
lished. Co-operation instead of hostility to be practised.
A good programme. m * m
Lord Rothschild has deputed Frenchman Weil
to protect his interests by giving an independent report
on Merton’s Reward (W.A.). Property has been more
examined and reported upon than any other mine in the
State, but experts have been extremely conservative in
their statements. No one has said definitely whether the
lode strikes or underlies north, south, east, or west, and
outside the Reward claim nothing has been conclusively
proved, though a lot has been stated at times.
• • • *
Adelaide, most fickle of markets, is keenest now
on tin—Smith’s Creeks and Stannaries, both N.Q. shows.
At the beginning of April Stannaries were 3s. lid. ; by
the first week in June 95., and the rise to that had been
steady. Herbert Phillips, little known hitherto in con-
nection with cats, but blessed with a solid business
reputation, built mainly as local managing partner in
G. and R. Wills and Cos., is at the head of the concern ;
and that accounts for some of the confidence in it. So
far, the co. has been busiest tramway-building, on which
£40,000 has been spent. At the end of last half-year
(March 31) the co. had a cash credit of £9781, and £18,955
was due on debentures stock (£54,000 has been
issued altogether); about £SOOO then remained to
be spent on the tramway. The co. does not
propose to smelt, but will battery its stone (10 or 12 per
cent stuff for a start). The line should be finished by
the end of this month. The capital available will proba-
bly just about see the co. through.
“ Jacko”: Stannary Hills Tin, an Adelaide-owned
concern, has a debenture debt of £54,000, and, according
to the chairman, cannot afford to erect such a plant as the
prospects warrant. What a pitiable plight for a co. with
an alleged capital of £650,000 to be in ! Only 40,000 con-
tributed shares issued, and the money subscribed long
since exhausted. Authority was given to borrow £200,000 on
debentures, but only £54,000 worth were issued, apparently
because public did not display the anticipated eagerness.
Still there is a merry market for the shares —but how
long’ will it last? Probably until the battery starts
pounding alleged 10 per cent, tin-stone. The intro-
duction of the debenture system into Australian mining
ventures is a curse which will do incalculable harm. It
can be justified only in the case of a proved producing
mine, for which money has to be raised for extra equip-
ment. In any other case, there must ever be the sus-
picion that it is a deep-laid scheme intended to work for
the benefit of debenture-holders (who are nearly always
the largest shareholders) as against the small share-
holders. How simple it is for a combine of big share-
holders to refuse to pay calls and force a mine into
debenture-holders’ hands, thus squeezing out the enter-
prising, honest small man. The speculative public ought
certainly to beware any debenture-burdened mine.
• * * *
The sec. ot Tartana (North Chillagoe) writes :
The writer in N.Q. Register who indulges in sarcasm at
the expense of the Tartana people must, if taken
seriously, be assumed to suffer from the general ’Towers
complaint “ we-have-nothing-to-learn-about-mining-or-
metallurgy,” Blane, McLaren, Clark, and other trained
men have'suggested that 33 per cent, can be saved in the
cost of treatment in ’Towers stone, but, as the ’Towers
men retort, “We can’t do it!” that ends the matter, and
experts in milling practices all over the world are not
•credited with knowing their business. Yet the evolution
towards modern methods is moving even ’Towers men,
although they either won't admit it, or the teachings of
Schoorof Mines director McLeod are being imbibed
unconsciously. The ’Towers men, however, do not
stand alone in this connection—the majority of
mining men throughout the Commonwealth are cursed
with the impotence of ignorance about the fine points of
the industry. But this curse is being removed by the
B.E. men from the Sydney University. When their
knowledge is properly applied, mining is reduced to
commercial or industrial enterprise, and “ fashionable
markets ” fizzle out. Leaching copper ore is a process
which, as The Bulletin recently remarked, was known
B.C. The Tartana people do not claim anything new about
It, but their methods of mechanical application are up to
date for the rapid handling of crushed ore. In expert-
menting upon stone broken to the size of a man’s fist they
merely wish to learn what length of time it requires,to
leach the copper from uncrushed stone. That all the
copper can be dissolved from the oxidised silicious gangue
Of Tartana Hill is now ascertained beyond doubt. The
way to do it was to do it. No question arose as to
whether it could be done, because it has been done and is
being done at Rio Tinto. A slab of information for un-
informed critics may not be amiss. For local treatment
at Rio Tinto 1,189,827 tons of ore were mined in 1900,
and 1,294,827 tons in 1901. Every pound of copper pro-
duced was by leaching, and not a ton of ore was crushed.
The broken ore is laid out in heaps, and the sulphur is
oxidised in the course of from four to five years. No
burning allowed nowadays—natural oxidisation the only
procedure permitted by Spanish Government. The
reserve heaps at the Rio Tinto mines were estimated, at
the close of the year 1901, to contain 136,457 tons metallic
copper. The tonnage of these heaps can be approxi-
mated from the assay values of the copper contents —
in 1900 average, 2’744 per cent.; in 1901 average,
2'267 per cent. In 1901, 21,120 tons of metallic
copper, and in 1901, 21,100 tons were produced.
What the Tartana people claim is that their ore is natur-
ally oxidised to the requisite degree for immediate leach-
ing, and they can forthwith follow the lead of Rio Tinto,
Tharsis, Arizona, and other big copper co’s; for what can
be done in one part of the world can be done in Queens-
land, provided that men “ who have nothing to learn ”
are kept at arm’s length. The scepticism re leaching
Tartana ores carries one’s memory back to the early
days of Mt. Morgan when chlorination was intro-
duced—“ Dissolve gold from stone, it can’t be done ! ’’
Yet to-day 20,000 tons of ore per month are
chemically treated at a less cost per ton than milling some
Towers stone. As for Tartana being boomed—well, if
imparting information re irrefutable facts can be charac-
terised as booming, then it is being boomed. Anyhow,
there is not an army of canvassers commissioned to sell
the unallotted shares ; the shares now being subscribed
for are purchased on the purchasers’ own volition. The
days of the Government-bursting boom of Tartana are
over ; and, despite all sorts and conditions of adverse
influences, Tartana merrily marches forward.

Melbourne
SOCIETY
For the Duke of York’s visit Australia is
now grateful. Politicians dare not suggest any
huge Coronation expenditure to a people which
has seen Prince George. In England the great
mass of the people only views its royalties through
the medium of the extravagantly loyal illustrated
press—of papers like the Illustrated London
News, which gives in a current number a beauti-
ful and expensive two-page picture of a noble-
looking, tall, distinguished Prince of Wales
“delivering his great speech on the Colonies.”
His great speech ! But that can’t go down now
among Australians, who took the opportunity of
personal observation to size-up the melancholy
mannikin. Royalty is a busted ideal in these
States now. Why on earth, if only in the interests
of John See’s title, didn’t King Ned keep his
mournful-looking little son at home and allow us
still to be deluded by the English illustrated
papers ?
* • • •
When Sydney Mail reaches London with
its colored Coronation picture of the King’s legs,
now shown in Sydney shop-windows, “Mister
James” will surely be prosecuted for high treason,
sedition, or something. No Australian paper has
ever before doiie anything so damaging to Royalty
as the publication of those awful legs. The Bul-
letin invites the attention of the head of the
N.S.W. police, Mr. John See, a loyal subject and
knight-expectant, to the disgraceful outrage on
the Anatomy of the Throne.

The 'Committee of the unfortunate fund
that was to provide a smart' statue of Queen
Victoria, to help in the decoration of Melbourne
and prove a touching momento of the well-beloved
and most noble and gracious, &c., &c., is in a bad
way. It has practically given its order for a
£lO,OOO statue, and it has only £6OOO to meet the
exes. Who’s to pay the rest? The committee
would like some large, round, devoted Jingo to
step up wi h £4030, but plump Jingoes don’t
sink capital in defunct monarchs —they only frivol
away their thousands on rulers who can give
honors where honors are due. Probably the
weary plate will circulate again, but the crowd is
getting rid of its small change shouting for King
Ned just now.
Prince of Wales’ Birthday being an un%
usually quiet holiday in Melbourne—a race-
meeting and four football matches summed up the
amusements —the Pentridge officials celebrated
the occasion by flogging a prisoner (a man whose
death-sentence had been commuted to 10 years'
penal servitude and 25 lashes). The press was
excluded, and as the flogger is also “doing time,”
the system had the entertainment all to itself and
doubtless enjoyed itself thoroughly.
Latest N.S.W. organisation is a “ Council
of the Bar,” to do for barristers what the Incor-
porated Law Institute does for solicitors. It is
safe to predict, however, that it will never have
to undertake anything analogous to the re Gent
One functions of the latter body. Whether it is
because of lack of opportunity (they don’t handle
clients’ money) or lack of wish, N.S.W. barristers
are an honest if impecunious set, who take to
sweeping out offices, or copying, or type-writing,
or tutoring, rather than to crookedness when the
wolf barks at the door.
* * * »
Several Australian papers complain that
in the Transvaal and Orange River Colony, under
British rule, the two legal professions (barrister’s
and solicitor’s) are to be kept distinct, and “amal-
gams’’from Victoria, Tasmania, or other States
where the professions are conjoined, will not be
admitted to practice.
English, Scotch and Irish advocates and solicitors, and
all practitioners before the former High Court of the
Transvaal, are admitted, but not persons who for seven
years continuously have not practised exclusively either
as advocates or attorneys in some British colony.
The injustice is more theoretical than real. Under
the treaty of peace, the Roman-Dutch law and
the Taal language are authorised in the courts of
the new colonies —the former absolutely, the
latter permissively. Australian barristers and
solicitors would have little chance of competing
in the law-courts with the many excellent
lawyers now in S’Africa who are familiar
with both. Roman-Dutch law would require
much study, and it would take at least
twelve months very hard work to acquire
sufficient knowledge of the Taal to be able to
examine Boer witnesses who choose to give evi-
dence in that tongue (and they will nearly all so
choose). Legal practitioners of the Common-
wealth will do well to keep away from S’Africa,
especially as there is just now a wild rush of Eng-
lish .lawyers to Pretoria and Johannesburg. A
gentleman who was on the Supreme Court Bench
of the Transvaal, writing by last mail, states that
/fl new barristers were admitted on one day.
Withal there is little legal work to do, and little
chance of a boom in litigation for a long while to
come.
• * * *
N.S.W. ex-Governor Jersey figured as a
witness in the English “Article Club” libel
action (Suffield v. London Tkuth). Two letters
which the gentle Hebrew (Cowen) who ran the
club wrote to a railway magnate were "put in
evidence. Both were alleged to have reference to
negotiations on the part of the magnate for a
title. One extract:
I am fully confident of success from more than one
quarter, but Lord Jersey will suffice, as, in his capacity of
Privy Councillor, he will either do the necessary work
himself or put me in the way of getting it done.
Lord Jersey denied, in answer to a direct ques-
tion, that he had authorised Cowen to say that he
(Jersey) would use his position as a Privy Coun-
cillor to secure honors for anyone. The Australian
Agents-General were all drawn into the vortex of
this precious club, which has secured about the
very worst possible sort of advt. from the libel
action.
It is a point for the considera-
tion of the pale and iflabby crowd
which is shocked about the effects of
the N.S.W. and Victorian divorce law,
that divorce in these places appears to be
distinctly on the decrease. N.S.W.
and Victoria are the two shocking ex-
amples, and when they made desertion
a ground for separation the number of
divorces increased enormously as a
matter of course. In 1894 the N.S.W.
courts granted 311 decrees nisi, and
the monkey-shave crowd hasn’t got over
the shock yet. But the number of
decrees granted has fallen off steadily
since then—in 1897 it was 245; in 1898,
244; in 1899, 230 ; and in 1900 only 216.
Victoria granted 117 divorces in*lB97,
and only 93 in 1900. The great original
rush of divorces was mostly in the
nature of a clearing-up of arrears ; the
women who had been deserted at all
manner of times during the previous 25
years poured into the courts for relief.
Even, now the business is largely a
cleaning up of arrears in the way of
granting relief to applicants whose
cause of complaint arose in very far
back years. When these arrears are
extinguished it seems quite probable
that the divorce courts of the two
States, whose lax and un-Christian
laws so badly shock the holy section
of the community, will have nothing
very remarkable about them. If the
N.S.W. and Victorian law is made
the law for the whole Commonwealth
there will, of course, be a great rush of
business for a time in Tasmania, S.A.,
Queensland, and Westralia, but it will
be only for a time. Things will simmer
down, and after 10 years or so it is
possible that the records won’t be serious enough
to shock even the mildest pastor in the Common-
wealth.

The abolition of the barmaid in godly
Glasgow has not passed without hostile criticism
from some of the highly orthodox. Principal
Story, president of the Scottish Christian Social
Union, in a broadly liberal speech, declared that
to affirm that respectable girls in a respectable
employment should necessarily lose their respec-
tability and honor in the course of a year or two
was_ a scandalous statement, lacking in both
Christian charity and common-sense.
■ '* * * •
“ A Barmaid ” writing to Sydney D.T. on
the barmaid abolition question :
It is no argument to say that employment could be
secured in other directions, including’ domestic service {
that is a matter of personal inclination, and the latter
occupation is one that no young woman of refinement
and self-respect could possibly enter under any circum-
stances. If a barmaid has sometimes to hear language
not always correct. . . . The barmaid has never to sub-
mit to the insulting contempt and familiarity that is so
often experienced by the servant girl.
Which touches the spot exactly.
* * • •
A Bulletin correspondent:—
The W.C.T.U. (Sydney) and the holy, oily gentlemen
who hang about their skirts have had a long, long gloat
over the barmaid question. Only one ex-Hebe has rapped
out an answer. If all who once were there, but now are
exceedingly well-placed in Sassiety, should reply, there
would be consternation among the Elect. In one State
capital a lady once behind the bar is, and has been for
years, press mentor of the very Naicest; another made a
notably good wife to a journalist of the first flight, now a
doyen of the press. Yet another was the good wife of a
now-deceased Supreme Court Judge, and still another
presides with much grace and dignity in the hospitable
mansion of a leader of the Equity Bar. Scandal never
touched one of them since they were wed; nor before
that, so far as I ever heard, and I knew them all—they
are only a few amongst hundreds.
• » • *
Some of the Aurania troops tell weird
stories of Australian military discipline. For
instance, a number of N.S.W. troops who had
entered a S’African railway-platform, from which
all but officers were supposed to be excluded, were
remonstrated with by the Imperial officer in
charge, and told to clear out. Whereupon they
seized him and tossed him iri a tarpaulin till all
his teeth went loose. The Aurania men wink and
dig you in the ribs when you mention the plausible
apologies made for their little games in Capetown
and Albany, and remark that Colonel Lassetter is
a right good sort—one of the very best, in fact—
and that it must have cost him a ton of money
and trouble to see “ his boys ” through.
• • • *
Writes a Brisbane correspondent:
What’s come over the gallant Major-General Hutton,
and where are the displays of lurid oratory which we of
the Brisbane Defence Forces, were told to expect from
him ? Why, he’s as mild as milk ! Methinks the memory
of that fatal slip, “ God bless j ou” to the Commonwealth
Contingent, preys on his mind and fetters his martial
spirit, though rumor hath it that the Simultaneous
Missioners have gathered him in. Nary a “ damn ! ” fell
upon the ears of the expectant soldiery. Sons of
bullock-punchers, with appreciative lugs cocked to drink
in the honeyed language of their forefathers, hank-clerks
with knees aquake and swords suspiciously awobble
gazed open-mouthed as the gallant General rode
smilingly past. Another injustice to Queensland 1 Surely
our cup of Federal bitterness is full. Instead of
“ language ”we got retrenchment. The General exuded
it at every pore. When he addressed the massed troops
his theme was retrenchment, and if he only paused to
point out a fault in a private’s gear, he discoursed
retrenchment to him. The meeting of officers was
regaled with it, and in his final address to the troops
Major-General Hutton pathetically called upon us to bear
retrenchment “ with the same heroic spirit that animated
our brothers in arms to shed their blood for the Empire ”
Pur breasts beat high with the thought that future
generations would fondly speak of us as “ the Glorious
Itetrenchr' 1 ’’
• * * *
Alleged cable to Melb. Age:—
The courtesies which, since the peace agreement have
been exchanged between the Boer generals and Lord
Kitchener, and the many and striking evidences of
amicable feeling shown by the Boers generally, have
completely staggered the Continental press, which for
over a year has teemed with stories of “British brutali-
ties ’ and atrocities. The people of Europe are quite
unable to reconcile the growing friendliness between the
two races with the former misrepresentations made to
them by the newspapers of Germany, Holland, and
Austria.
Well, suppose Australians try now to reconcile
“the many and striking evidences of amicable
feeling” with the stories of “Boer brutalities”
and atrocities, with which the Age has teemed for
over two years!

The present severe epidemic of measles in
Adelaide is loyally attributed to “infection com-
municated by returned contingenters to barmaids,
waitresses, and hotel and restaurant employees
generally.” There’s a moral somewhere about this 1
“ F.” : The text for a
long sermon “Relations
between the Sexes,” and all
that sort of thing, is sup
plied by a matron who wrote
to “Rita,” of Melbourne
Herald, concerning the
“ shameless exposure of
women’s underwear in
drapers’ shops.” The blush-
ing correspondent complains
that articles of orthodox
female clothing are placed
in shop-windows and upon
counters, where they may
be viewed with impunity by
men—therefore the exposure
is shameless. What is the
shame, where is the shame,
and why is the shame in this
ordinary business display of
respectable female linen ?
Surely there is nothing im-
proper about a woman’s un-
derwear until she gets inside
it. The average man makes
no pretence of shrinking from
a public contemplation of
his shirts and pants exposed
for sale in a hosier’s window ;
neither does he protest
against the conduct of those
women who buy shirts and
so on for their male-kind in
the ordinary way of shopping,
and without the least con-
sideration for the salesman’s
sex. Woman admits the
decency of his pants, socks,
and shirts by talking about
them with a casual counter-
jumper. And yet she per-
sists in olaiming indecency for her own linens and
flannels. She is such an indelicate creature that
even the clothes she is going to wear become by
anticipation indecent. There is no doubt about
woman being the unkindest critic of her own
character.

An irate Brisbane father sought the
police last week and told how his 15 year-old
daughter, while out on an errand after tea, had
been seized and criminally assaulted by a
miscreant. The girl’s dress was torn, hair
dishevelled etc. etc. After some days’ investiga-
tion the damsel eventually confessed that she
hadn’t been outraged at all; but that she had
loitered on the road and then invented the yarn
to save herself a scolding. Luckily no man was
forthcoming so nobody was hanged this time.
The sweet maiden had torn her raiment most
artistically and suggestively.
• ' • • *
At Albany Creek (Q.) the usual girl has
just confessed that the man who “assaulted ” her
was an effort of the imagination—as lie generally
is. That man and that assault seem to be, to a
large section of. Australian girlhood, the one
subject of reflection, the one ambition, the one
romance, the one possibility of a break in the
grey stagnation of every day life. Like the
morbid girl-invalid who prayed that she might be
found worthy of operation she also prays, with a
difference, that she may be found worthy of
assault, so that she will be a heroine and an object
of interest to her unmolested acquaintances. At
least the results all point that way. Australia is
a country where no man should be out after dark
except in the presence of two reliable male
witnesses.
“ The Way back Carbineers,” a Sydney
crowd of ex-cohentin genters, who were anxious to
go back to the S’African war, on their own, from
“motives of patriotism” and because they couldn’t
get billets here, were baulked by a curious mis-
chance. Ketchener was willing to have them,
and provision was made for their passages, but
the cable messages to the chief of these paytriots
(a Mr. M'Millan) were wrongly delivered to poli-
tician M‘Millan, who referred them to the mili-
tary authorities, and they were lost in a maze of
red-tape, from which they emerged only when the
war had ended, and the “ Wayback Carbineers ”
had gone wayback again in despair.
