THE KING DIVINE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ London, June 2.— The driver of a hansom cab, evad-
ing the police, collided with the King’s coach, His
Majesty stepped from the coach on to the pavement, un-
discomposed, and calmly awaited the extrication of the
driver’s horse, which was struggling beneath the coach ”
—Cable.
Let every poet sing—
Who hopes to rise and
shine—
A paean to the King
Who rules by right divine.
When common men go
forth,
To city’s dangers meet,
They look to south and
north
And closely scan the street.
And should they get a
bump
From tram or Jehu’s wheel,
They straight erect a hump
And in their terror squeal.
But kings, the gods be
praised,
Are built of sterner stuff ;
They take, all unamazed,
Catastrophes like snuff.
Serene and strong they
stxnd,
Unmoved by fear or force,
Though all the riven land
Be full of kicking horse.
When common cab or cart
To State coach is opposed,
A King, God bless his ’art,
Is quite undiscomposed.
With Royal dignity
He calmly steps aside,
His fortiter in re
Replete with kingly pride.
He might have kicked and
cussed
And danced upon his crown,
Or sat down in the dust
Of dirty London town ;
Or yelled for the police
The Nihilists to grab ;
Or punched, for joy of
peace,
The driver of the cab.
But no such vulgar things
He did, for ’tis disclosed,
He stood the test of kings—
“ln danger quite com-
posed.”
So let us thank the gods
Our King can rise and shine,
And prove to common clods
His right to rule divine.
SIMON PHRASER.

The wrangle among old Sydney residents
about the spot at Sydney Cove on which Gov.
Phillip raised his first flagstaff practically illus-
trates the little value of most ancient history.
Unless some conclusive written matter so far
undiscovered comes to light, the spot on \vhich
Phillip raised his flagstaff will never be definitely
determined. The diversity of existing accounts
show the fallibility of much that is called history.
• * * *
Gov. Rawson’s allusion, repeated some-
what frequently, to the “ Beautiful Harbor ”
has started a few inquiries as to when that
battered compliment was originated. There is a
good deal of probability that it originated on
Pinchgut Island in Capt. Phillip’s first year and
that the father of the phrase was a convict with
a rope round his neck. But Capt. Hill of the
Second N.S.W. Corps writing from Sydney to
philanthropist Wilberforce in England, _ says,
“ We left the Cape on the 29th. of April and
entered in this beautiful harbour on 26th. June
1790.”
• ■* * *
Tas. ex-Governor Gormanston’s four-
wheeler, a conspicuous, bright-yellow concern,
with buff cushions, is now in the possession of a
wealthy Hobart alderman. The latter has loyally
refrained from painting-out the vice-regal color,
and whenever he takes drives abroad looms large
as the man who is keeping a Viscount’s memory
yellow in the hearts of an envious people. His
Ex’s, landau was bought by a Hobart cabby, who
promptly had the flaring yellow transformed to a
respectable black.
A TASTE FOR MUSIC.
FRIEND: “I suppose you are hard at work get-
tiny up that musical troupe of yours? ”
PROFESSOR: "Oh, no. What’s keeping me hard
at work now is getting up the audience."
The foolishness of the Law, which allows a
man to do as he likes with his own—or what he
considers his own—was shown in Melbourne lately
when the estate of James Healey was divided,
as per his last will, among a dozen Victorian
charities, leaving his wife and three children
in absolute want! If James, during his life,
had given his wages to a hospital, the
Law would have ordered him to maintain his
family or go to gaol; but the Law allows him, as
a corpse, privileges which the live man doesn’t
possess—to swindle his family. The Law, however,
takes care that all other just claims against a
deceased’s estate shall be duly met, excepting
only what should be absolutely the first claim.
An amendment of the Probate law to make main-
tenance of an infant family the first charge on all
estates, should be demanded by every reformer.
Meanwhile, the living should boycott any
“ charity ” which robs the widow and the orphan
by accepting bequests which should be devoted to
their maintenance, for such action is tantamount
to receiving stolen money—money stolen in very
mean fashion, too.

A gay young Sydney dog sometime ago
sailed for L >ndon, and after his departure his
people were interviewed by various persons
alleging claims against him —amongst them being
a young woman with a baby which she credited to
the absent roysterer. His people, to avoid
trouble, agreed to give the damsel an allowance—
which was regularly paid to her until the wanderer
returned and was able to prove, not only that the
girl’s statement was absolutely false, but that the
infant put in evidence had been borrowed for the
purpose. Meantime she and. her mother had been
enjoying comparative affluence, and the impostor s
marriage with a young man who couldn’t afford
it had been celebrated with much splash.

The other day Croydon (N.S.W.) had a
remarkable hailstorm, the scope whereof was
about a mile. Some of the stones were big as
eo-gs—and two lumps were picked up weighing
about 181bs. each, the result, most likely, of being
frozen when the hail reached ground. Hailstones
were to be picked up in the drifts on Saturday
four days after the storm. On Croydon railway
station the hailstones were about 9m. deep-
Concerning a phase of woman-nature.
Writes “F.C.” :
Tramming it down Plinders-st. (Melb.), t’other day, I
was interested to see a crowd of women outside the
Coupon Co.’s warehouse. Investigating, I discovered
that some practical jokers had spread the news that
coupons must be presented ere May expired. So on this,
the 30th, every “ thrifty ” housewife lined up to get what
she could for her stamps. It was dinner-time, and rain-
ing heavily, and on the office door was posted a newly-
written notice stating that coupon-redemption hours
were from 3 to 5, also that collectors had still ail June
wherein to redeem them, but nary a woman moved.
Passed again about 3, when the road was blocked with a
mob of sodden, dishevelled bargain-hunters, who had lost
more than the value of their coupons in damaare to their
clothes and their shop hair. Suddenly the doors opened,
and the porter fled before a rush like unto the scramble
of a million fallen angels through the gates of Paradise.
Woman’s faith in the man who offers to give something
for nothing is truly touching, and is only comparable to
the suspicion with which she regards the same man when
he asks her to take things easy and not rush him off his
feet. He offers her a fortune, and she believes him ; he
gives her sixpence, and she turns it over to see if it hasn t
a hole in it.
« • • *
Preacher Bevan, after spending a few days
in N. Queensland, says “it is the maddest thing
in the world ” to try to impose the same laws there
as in the south. Yet he doesn’t see anything
absurd in trying to have the same God there as
further south, and the same Ten Commandments.
Even the fact that polygamy is one of the habits
of tropical countries doesn’t incline him to allow
the man beyond a certain degree of latitude to
have two wives, and the man still further north to
have three.
Westralia is part of the Australian Corn-
monwealth which is supposed to be a
free country, yet there any common
policeman can stop a man in the street,
and, for no visible reason save that he
feels that way, ask him his name, where
he lives, and how he gains his livelihood.
The police can also board a steamer and pre-
vent a passenger leaving unless said passenger
goes to Fremantle police station“and gets a writ-
ten statement that there is nothing against him
and he therefore is not “ wanted.” They don’t
seem able to identify a man who is “ wanted”
unless he goes up to a constable and tells
him.
In Westralia, a man accused of
felony is charged before a magistrate,
and, if there is a case against him, he
maybe sent for trial. In the “higher”
court, he may find the v same magis-
trate presiding as a Mudge, who
may conduct the case so as to tell
against the prisoner, and instruct
the jury, in his charge, as he
likes. He can give as high a
sentence as 20 years. No legal
qualification is necessary foi these
“Judgeships.” As a matter of
fact, one holder of the office
graduated as a boundary-rider
and another as a tinsmith.

A Maorilander lately es-
cuped a breach-of-promise action
by settling it in a novel way. The
lady had served the writ, and
had engaged all the local legal
talent The hearing of the case
was eagerly awaited, but a truce
was declared, negotiations were
re-opened, and defendant speci-
fically performed the contract by marry-
ing plaintiff.
N.S.W. General Presbyterian Assembly,
in a report, states that, as the State looks after
orphan children fairly well, a special church
orphan society is unnecessary. This is the
unattached citizen’s view. If a couple of other
churches come into line with the Presbyterian the
time will be ripe for the assumption by the State
of the duty of looking after all orphans who are
left pecuniarily unprovided for.
» * * *
N.S.W. Presbyterian community has re-
ceived a donation of £5OO to pay the expense of
importing eight ministers “ from the old country.”
Being a Presbyterian minister seems to be about
the only avenue of employment in Australia in
which the supply of labor is less than the demand.
Yet surely it is not such a dreadful job;
the salary at first is low, but there are plums
at the top. Presbyterianism, too, in its later
Australian manifestations, is not such a gloomy
affair of Predestination and wholesale Damna-
tion as it was in the days of John Knox (and Dr.
Cairns). The “Higher Criticism” has changed
it wonderfully, and a Presbyterian of to-day
needn’t even believe in Old Nick. Well now—
music allowed, whisky more than tolerated,
“ broad ” views permitted, fair salaries given, and
many beautiful and gilded girls to choose wives
from, why don’t our young men who can’t go on the
land'go on the Presbyterian Church roster ?It
seems an insult to Australian enterprise that men
should have to be imported for what are, all
things considered, fairly decent billets. Of the
writer’s four sons, three are at once to be re-
christened Donald, Sandy, and Andrew, and
brought up as strict Presbyterians, on spec.
The Adelaide Water Inquiry Board has
for its members the Mayor of Adelaide, the
Engineer-in-Chief, and the Chairman of the
Central Board of Health. Yet the chief subject
of its investigation is whether the two latter
officials have done all that is necessary to secure
the purity of the city supply and to properly safe-
guard the health of the people. Defendants have
thus a majority in the Court and may rest satisfied
that the verdict will be all right from their point
of view. A suggestion has been made to call in
Dr. Gresswell, of Melbourne, or some other dis-
interested expert, to see fair play.

with a good supply of water in Prospect Reservoir,
and that scarcity is likely to continue until the
autumn of 1903.

Fagin has new methods nowadays ; accord-
ing to the merciful Judge Bundey, of S.A., he
makes a lot of use of the First Offenders Act,
representing to the youngster that, even if the
latter does get caught, his escape under this Act
is a “dead cert.” So Fagin stands back in the
comparatively safe position of receiver.

A Thursday Island message to Brisbane
Courier :
A curious development is reported to have taken place
in connection with the bread supply. The local garrison
contract had recently been given to a Chinese baker, but
the non-commissioned officers and men absolutely decline
to accept the bread, and are purchasing for themselves
direct from the late contractor.
Not at all “curious.” The men had probably
been reading “Stuffing,” and other Bulletin
stories, on the mysteries of Chinese cookery.
The Bulletin has never dared to reprint those
Chinese-cook yarns.

Said missioner Torrey at Ballarat : ‘‘lf a
girl only knew what the man thinks while he is
dancing with her she would never dance again.”
Evidently Pastor Torrey knows what the man
thinks, and knows it’s" something particularly
nasty. The parson’s process of mind-reading is
very simple—always think the worst. Logically
this lodges the bad mind in the reverend skull.
In point of fact the physical and mental activity
demanded by the dance is less conducive to evil-
thinking than the dreary process of sitting out a
dreary sermon at a close church in an atmosphere
of hymns and hers; and probably if the hlue-eyed
girl knew what the fellow next her in the pew on
Sunday was thinking she wouldn’t go to church
any more ; or, maybe, if the fellow next the blue-
eyed girl in the pew on Sunday knew what the
blue-eyed girl was thinking he’d never go to church
again—or he’d go every Sunday. It all depends.
“ TYING IT IN A KNOT.”
An insistent query arises about those
alleged Greeks stopped at Albury and sent back
to Wodonga pending investigations. Seeing that
both lots were alleged capitalists—the last crowd
had £783 concealed about their persons and a very
large bank draft following them —'are the
Wodonga publicans bloated millionaires that they
declined to accommodate them for a few nights,
or what was the matter, anyhow ? A man who
lets his wife be confined under a station platform
while he has command of considerable money is
either uncivilised or else—well there are several
“ elses.”

At a recent big Melbourne public function
a gaily-caparisoned dame alarmed spectators by
calmly lifting her skirt and pulling a dainty lace
handkerchief from her garter. Is this the latest
from ’Ome? If so, there may soon be great
possibilities in jewelled girters and openwork
stockings, while the man who can make a
“corner” in pneumatic calves will find fortune
in it.

Even the praying-wheel will have to go
now. Latest American invention is a lhckel-in-
the-slot parson. Forty persons drop in a coin
apiece, and the figure talks a Talmage or a
Beecher sermon for 20 minutes or so, accompany-
ing the delivery with suitable gestures. There
are so many points in favor of automatic parsons,
and their machinery is so simple, that it is a
marvel they have been done without so long.

Representatives of the two Australian
maritime federations, Steamship-owners and Sea-
men, will meet in conference during the month to
discuss rates and conditions for the next half-year.
Ships’ officials have at present the most pressing
grievances; their conditions of service are at
times unbearable, and their shipboard accommo-
dation of the worst. On one favorite passenger
vessel the “glory-hole” can only be reached by
travelling over the boilers; and the bunks and
mess-room are walled with iron plates, separated
only by a few inches from steampipes and boiler-
plates, whilst the sides of the ship are unpro-
tected by any interior sheathing. The heat
consequently is insufferable, the ventilation
miserable, and the chance of escape in the event
of a steampipe bursting practically ml.^

A Sydney tramway episode :
Near midnight ; in the silent, dimly-lighted ward of the
bi<' hospital the night-nurse hears the telephone-bell ;
runs noiselessly to it, and then, after a.short pause,
back to a vacant bed. She has just adjusted the
waterproof sheet when the tramp of feet is heard,
and the ambulance-stretcher cornea. The patient
is gently lifted to the bed. His right leg hangs
vertically from the splintered bone below the
ligature, while the left foot appears badly
smashed. The surgeons and wardsmen rapidly
cut away the blood-and-dirt-soiled clothing ;
the wounded man curses them. They wash
his body with antiseptics, and hurry away.
Lights flash out in the operating-theatre, and
the patient, now nearly unconscious, is again
placed on a stretcher and carried from the
ward. Half-an-hour passes. The patient,
still unconscious from ether, is brought back
to the bed. The stump of an amputated leg
is raised on a pillow ; the leg is in splints.
Hot-water cans are placed on each side of
him, and a nurse and a doctor sit, watch in
hand, holding the wrists. Presently the
doctor lets go the wrist, and, lighting a
match, slowly moves it to and from the un-
covered eye. Then he blows it out, and
departs. The nurse rises to place the screens
round the latest victim of the electric tram.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
You are my heart o’ the rose,
My soul o’ the haunting song—
But night bids the red rose close,
And sleep hushes echo long.
Why roses were made to close,
Songs to be hushed and cast by,
Night to end Daylight—who knows?
« Love me l ” —To-morrow we die i

State Excellency Rawson did his first brick-
laying and earned his first silver trowel last week
when the foundation stone of the new North Shore
xiospital was swung to its place. It was all very
funereal: the grey, rainy afternoon, the bared
heads, the silence, the vice-regal oration, then the
burial—of the bottle—with papers and coins,
instead of pickles, in its interior. His Ex. is
making great efforts to memorise names and
places and “the amount of the debt.” There is
always a debt mentioned to him when he lays a
bnck or attends a charitable meeting. “Mr.
Dibbs, echoed His Ex. meditatively when the
was P resent ed to him. “ Ah, Mr.
b 1 i ” /-r 16 funeral manager of the Commercial
Banking Company has a gravely-decorative old-
Koman face and presence, and if he had come
on the scene wearing a toga and a fillet, and
with twelve lictors marching by him, he would
have looked the part all the Appian Way. Mr.
.Dibbs recently received from the Govt, for his
f ° reshore P ro P ert y another cheque—for
_ Lady and Miss Rawson looked as usual Mid-
v ictorian, calm and affable. They went home
with Mrs. Dibbs, who lives adjacent, to have tea
and muffins. Mrs. Randal Carev, Mrs. Burton
Dibbs, Dr. and Mrs. Arthur and a lot of N.S.W.
medical men and clergy were on the platform.
Talking of Governors, a correspondent in British
New Guinea tells me that on the arrival and
departure of Governor Le Hunte at Port
Moresby 13 guns are fired, but as they have no
guns larger than the Snider, 13 kerosene-tins, each
with a fuse and a plug of dynamite, are used,
when the Governor steps out of his boat a native
policeman runs along and sets fi-e to the fuses.
Result: _ 13 loud bangs, 13 flying kerosene-tins,
and na-tives fleeing for their lives. His Ex.
thinks it great fun.
All official au revoirs to the Darleys were made
at the Hotel Australia, and the C.J. and Miss
Darley were on board the Moana an hour before
the steamers starting time. They drove down,
escorted by a few lancers, Miss Darley in a grey
travelling frock, carrying a huge pink posy and
obvious traces of tears. They remained on deck
waving farewells, until the liner was well off the
wharf.
Grace Palotta farewelled us last week, on last
night of the Royal Comic Opera eo. The pretty
Viennese is one of that class of artists who are
attractive by reason of artistic personality. She
can t smg, she can’t dance; her roles demand no
histrionic power, and yet there is a charm about
her slightest little juggle with her sunshade and
her train that magnetises both sexes and makes
her seem a genius. A large presence, which but
for innate refinement would be bouncing, a charm-
ing smile, exquisite tact in the management of
frills and feet and sunshade—that is Palotta !
The Scots College boys visited State Ex.
Rawson and had tea with him last week. They
had a good time, and the Admiral was quite the
great old commander of whom they had read in
their school-boy sea-stories, of the “From Powder
Monkey to Admiral ” type. Oneof the elder boys
had an elaborate speech written out, and was un-
loading the opening sentence with great effect
when his Ex. burst into a hearty laugh, slapped
him on the back and said : ** There, that’s enough,
tny boy; I know all about it. Shake hands and
say no more on the matter.” If his Ex. could
only interrupt the politicians’ platitudes in the
same manner!
The newly-formed Players Club made a good
start last week at Sydney Royal in Pinero’s
polished, cameo-like play, “The Weaker Sex.”
Ihe house was crowded, and even the gallery was
dotted over with evening dress. The staging and
scenery were excellent; in fact, for the simple
character of the play, the scenery could not have
been improved upon. It is a feature of Pinero’s
plaws to glide smoothly from comedy to tragedy,
and end in a maze of pathos, and send the people
away wondering what they would have done under
the same circumstances.
Between the acts Miss Wright’s pupils gave a
exposi tion of Spanish castanet dancing,
and flitted about like nice, round limbed fairies,
while a gent, (he was a “gent.,” not the other
thing) in evening dress snored sweetly to the time
of the mandolin orchestra.
A feature of the first performance by the
Players Club was the quantity of imitation
jewellery flaunted in the d.c. Had they been
genuine, there were at least two and a half millions
worth of pearls in that part of the house alone.
Une lady had a string worth, roughly, £lOO,OOO
Diamonds, mostly made in Paris or dug out of a
German factory, glittered in coronet-like orna-
ments* that many women wore on their heads.
Writer counted 32 magnificent gems in the hair of
one lady, just below, in front, the smallest being
thesize of a pea and the largest approximating to
a pigeon’s egg—face value, about a quarter million.
Also, there was a wealth of made-in-Paris rubies,
that gleamed blood-red against the goose-flesh
and drew attention to the extraordinary absence
of the recently popular turquoise.
Talking of jewels : Very few women wear the
stones that suit their complexions. If you have a
pale, spirituelle face, with blue eyes and a dreamy
expression, wear pearls and opals. They give an
atmosphere of purity that drags the soul right out
of the most case-hardened man. If you h ive dark
eyes and hair to correspond, wear all the rubies
you can beg, borrow, or steal, but let them have
their fire added to by a liberal use of diamond
sparks. It is a feature of the diamond that in
such eases it loses its own individuality and adds
its fire to the red glitter of the rubies, and at a
very little distance the effect is that of red flashes
of frozen flame. Opals, those crystallised minia-
ture sunsets, suit only fair complexions and clear
skins. The only person who really ought to wear
emeralds is the red-haired girl with the dark eyes.
Their beautiful green glow shows up the slightest
imperfection in the tint and texture of either face
or neck ; which is no doubt why Nature in mercy
has made them very rare and frightfully expen-

There is talk in England of an organised revolt
of clergymen against the selfish matinee hat which
shuts out the curate and the preacher from the
view of worshippers who haven’t secured front
seats. Already a few protests have been hurled
from the pulpit. The vast circumferential hat,
with birds, fruit and flowers protruding above its
crown, prevents fellow Christians from seeing
what the vicar has to say, also the effect of his
beatific smile is lost to the large majority of his
flock Moreover, if the fair sinners in front
would kindly take off their hats during the First
.Lesson they would be able to employ their hat-
pins in the stern religious duty of pricking the old
man and the children to keep ’em awake. The
projected movement against the wearing of ob-
structive millinery in the tabernacle seems
calculated to supply some long-felt wants.
All flesh is not grass these days. Much of the
flesh I see in the butchers’ shops is stubble, salt-
bush, and bottle-brush, and it has a blue, lean, and
pathetic look. In Cobar district the distress
through drought is acute. Some families are
partially living on boiled saltbush ! When the
humans have to take to sheep-food, what hope
for the sheep?
An experience of the warfare of the wasps
up the North Shore line. I had watched
a company all spring and summer building
an addition to a last year’s nursery under the
eaves in the hot, unmitigated glare that glares
10 t wes^ern Bld ® the Akenehi humpy.
When the nursery was as big as your head I pur-
posed to gi ve myself a field day, so I tied saplings
together till I had a pole 15ft. long, on to the
end of which I fastened a rake handle : then I
started to rake down the wasps’ abode. I did not
think they could see me at that range, or that they
could register cause and effect. Every one of
them was a Christian de Wet in foresight. A fly-
column, half a million strong, cnased me a
mile into the bush, catching me all the time. For
a week I went about veiled, and the Veiled
Prophet, Mokanna himself, hadn’t greater reason
to keep his hideousness covered than I.
Pitt-street restaurants, A.B C. and Shadler’s,
hitherto face-to-face rivals, have merged into one.
Ihe A.B. C. has swallowed Shadler’s. I suppose
under the new management the aggressive band
that played rapid marches and whirling waltzes
whilst one ate a bun or cutlet in Shadler’s will
be silenced. Goodness knows, Australians eat
fast enough in silence, but to the strains of the
Coronet waltz or somebody’s Coronation March
they simply gobble, as involuntarily as they would
hurry to the music of a quick-step in the street.
1 lie only really digestive music is something slow
and dreamy.
To balance the fact that we have in Sydney a
woman barrister wanting to practise, there has
bobbed up a male barrister who aspires to do the
rm° man S , a £ e a certain very domestic weekly.
Ihe regular conductor of the milk-and-water
page went away to Coronate, and the youthful
lawyer desired her to instal him in her place, and
she had to use considerable argument to prove
that his jurisprudence did not cover the treatment
ot teething babes, the knitting of socks and hoods,
renovation of last year’s frocks, and how to make

The fifteenthsmoke concert of the Electrical
Association of N.S. W., held at Aaron’s Exchange
Motel last Thursday night, was, I am told,°a
great success. All smoke concerts are the same.
Ihe men smoke and sip, and listen to comic songs
reflecting on the tyranny and rapacity of the lady
whom, when they get home, they style “ my own
sweetest,” and the big assembly of electricians and
friends cheered uproariously when Mr. Wilkie
sang a song about taking the Old Dutch out and
losing It. But it was a fearsome parody on “ the
Moly City ’ that broke up the company.
Rev. E. C. Beck, who succeeds Bishop Pain as
Rector of Darlinghurst parish, is a wiry, athletic
parson who looks much younger than his years.
He is a great favorite with boys and young men,
and doesn t go about carrying a halo, begging all
and sundry to get one of that make, or dropping
the corners of his mouth. Mr.) Beck was an asso-
ciate of King Ned when the latter was under
tutors and learning to “ put up his props.” They
en -n" i . friendl y encounters with the gloves.
Mr. Beck is unmarried.
. Dancing was once a form of worship—it is yet
m some places. If it could be so proclaimed in
Sydney and all the balls that are coming off during
tins week be offered up as prayers-for-rain there
would be such a lot of worship that perhaps a
deluge would result for the drought-stricken
country. The Mosman matrons’ dance at Needs’
rooms would be a most attractive prayer;
the Fleet hall on 20th at Garden Island a
powerful orison ; and the N.S. Hospital ball a
dynamic supplication. People dancing out their
invocations would look so much more charming
than showing the turned-out soles of their boots
as in the present style of worship.

somehow involved.

In Brisbane a wordy newspaper war is raging
between Anglicans and “ fancy ” religions as to the
religious observances on Coronation Day. Con-
sequent upon the Anglicans’ “special claim on the
King (and presumably on his Maker) and their
disinclination to worship with “schismatics,” there
will be two services held at different hours, and
the Governor, “prominent men,” and—er—the
Creator can attend both.
Brisbane just now is on the qiii vive of anticipa-
tion for sensational allegations which, it is said,
will come out in the Law Courts as to the death
of Dr. Wray of plague.
The gilded Mr. Tom Laurie, for years a Sydney
beau, of Kirribilli Point, is engaged to Miss Ivy
Dangar, of Kempsey. The bride-elect is tall and
fair. The bridegroom-elect is tall, dark and big,
noted for his faultless dressing, absolutely perfect
moustache, beautiful gardens, love of art, and his
chef. He has barrels of money—which, of course,
is only a detail.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“The Hunt breakfast at ‘Rupertswood,’ in
connection with the Findon Harriers’ opening
meet, was a sparklesome affair. Rupert Clarke
had prepared for an extra number of appetites
this year, and the stage of the ball-room was
pressed into use, thereby giving 500 guests an ample
view (if they wanted it) of Gov. Clarke and the
other exalted personages wrestling with their tur-
key. The speeches were limited—fortunately. His
Lx. made several ‘bulls’ in his disjointed oration,
though he was aided by notes. Once he
talked in a reminiscent strain of the time,
long ago, ‘ when my wife was young.’ At
this the whole 500 let out a volley of laughter.
In a dissertation on the joys of hunting, he men-
tioned that ‘ the chase was not as destructive to
the morals as horse-racing.’ A groan went up,
and then there was another smileful explosion.
“Five drags and endless carts and phaetons
bore a chattering, rustling contingent over the
roads of Sunbury while the local cockies gaped
from fences and barn-doors in open-mouthed
amazement at the noisy crew. The Huttons,
Sydenham Clarkes, and Millers had a coach
to their elite selves. American Consul Bray
drove in a plain enough buggy with S’Rupert.
Miss Eva Robertson was in a cinnamon brown
frock,, tabbed, stitched, flared, tucked and
machined in the thousand lines prescribed by the
Paris chiffon creator ; a cinnamon mushroom toque
kept the smart frock company. The wife of the
mining magnate, L. Brandon, cut the cutest
figure of the horsewomen, and Mrs. Johnston,
wife of the warrior warehouseman, led in the
field. Aide Browning carried round a camera,
and had all the smart girls hopping about like cats
on a grill every time he turned the cannon-mouth
towards them. After the hunt, or the wild efforts to
trace the fox, which went on for two solid hours,
the drags turned back to Rupertswood, and here
the crowd raised the echoes of revelry again in the
baronial hall.
“ At the Oaklands Club race meeting on Satur-
day the Gov.-General rode out to the meeting,
telling his red-coat friends that he had no choice
but to ride, as all his carriage nags were being
treated with short rations, in preparation for their
sea trip. And he said it without a smile.
“The sudden re-appearance of Mrs. Corbet at
‘Dorothy’ on Saturday night set the whole of the
circle and stalls in a simmer of excitement. Every-
one hungered to know the reason for that popular
matron’s sudden and unexpected return from the
Countess’s side on R.M.B. China. She arrived
with ‘Gussie’ Dangar, and joined Mrs. George
Chirnside’s party in the box. The baby Corbet’s
health is said to have made the mother anxious,
and to have caused her return, but those who
understood the position of affairs when the China
left Melbourne say things contrary reached a
climax.
“The drought probably lessens the amount of
money available for fashionable gaieties. Any-
way, the smartest seem unanimous at present in
regarding Australia as a nice place to live out of.
Miss Leslie Madden, one hears, is off to Germany
to study music along with her cousin, a daughter
of Mrs. Andrew Rowan. Sister Ruby, too, says
local rumor, meditates a jaunt to India and Europe
in company with her married sister’s connections,
the Osborne girls. The Steve Fairbairns and
many more of the squatter set are, or have been,
roaming, waiting for Coronation fun to begin and
drought dullness to end.
“ The ‘ Naicest ’ bemoan a future of retrenched
Government House gaieties in this ‘crude
Commonwealth,’ and threaten to settle themselves
on foreign shores. But somehow our travelled
exquisites always come back from Abroad to the
place where somebody is somebody, and every-
body knows it. It’s not the loss of the Hopetouns
personally that worries the hall-marked goods.
His. Ex., in fact, is in their black books, because
ever since he came back as viceroy, he has only
junketed with them officially. In his plain
Governor days he took pot-luck in Western-
District wool-palaces and chummed up with the
Camperdown clique ; but now, say his some-time
puppets, ‘We’re nowhere/ Federal frumps, State
sawneys, municipal marms—all have the pull over
us. When the Prince was here it was simply
monstrous ! We, if you please, were out of it as
completely as so many washerwomen.’
“ Lady Clarke, S’Rupert’s wife—not his step-
mother in this case—is president of the Vic.
Society for the Prevention of Cruelty to Children,
and she took the chair at the recent meeting of
the soc. which decided to help the good cause
along with a ball. Fashionable philanthropy
delights in assuming a low-cut bodice and a blaze
of jewellery for the sake of suffering humanity.
.The curate in the Thornton production, who
remarks nightly ‘This is a slippery world 1 ’
recalled a former Vic. potentate’s reference to his
own frequent tumbles-off as ‘ The ups-and-downs
of er—er—life.’ The chicksof said chronic faller-off
apparently inherit pa’s experience of the insecurity
of mortal things. One of them, a Countess, has
been legally wooing ‘hubby’ to return to his
deserted domestic perch. Truant hubby was
heard of in connection with those Hooley bothers
a while back, and has since been a bit hurt don’-
cherknow, by the remarriage of his Dowager-
Countess mother.
“ Victoria’s new Premier, Mr. William Hill
Irvine, is the younger son of the late Mr. Hill
Irvine, who owned a large flax-mill in the North
of Ireland. The Premier’s mother now lives at
Malvern with a son and four daughters, one of
whom is a very clever figure-painter. Mrs. W
H. Irvine was a Miss Mitchell, a daughter of a
Victorian clergyman in Newry. The late John
Mitchell and the late John Martin, celebrated
Irish patriots, were Mrs. Irvine’s brother and
brother-in-law respectively.
“The marriage of Miss Muriel Miller, the
second daughter of Mr. Albert Miller, of ‘ Whern-
side, Toorak, and Mr. H A. Currie, of squatting
fame, was alleged to be a quiet one, but sightseers
at St. John’s Church, Toorak, were satisfied that
no money or trouble had been spared to make a
goodly show. The dress was a triumph of art.
Its special point was its exquisite delicacy,
IfT'nming, layers of chiffon, and priceless lace.
White silk w.is worn by the leading bridesmaid,
Miss E. Miller, and the four children of the party.
Mr. W. Johnston helped the shy bridegroom
through the ordeal. A reception followed at
Whernside.’ The pair are going to travel.
“ Nofc generally known that some years ago,
before the Synod of Athens had sent out te Mel-
bourne a Greek priest, the Victorian oyster and
barrow Greeks,-of whom there were nearly a hun-
dred, used to go to the Rev. Hughes, C.E. Vicar
of St. Peter’s, for baptism, confession, absolution,
marriage, and burial. The Greek priest, Papa
Athanasioa Kantopoulos, decided, on reaching
Melbourne, that those ceremonies, performed by
the English church, were null and void, and re-
baptised the children. Mr. Hughes wrote a com-
plaint to the Patriarohs of Athens, Jerusalem,
Alexandria, and Syria, each of whom gave
judgment in his favor. So vital was the matter
considered that the Synod of Athens spent much
time in considering it, and sent a number of ques-
tions on the differences between the Greek and
English Churches to the Bishop of Salisbury,
which were published in pamphlet form, and re-
garded as of great theological importance. The
Anglican High Church party in Australia, includ-
ing the various sisterhoods, only recognises three
“ orthodox ” churches in the world—the English
Cat hoi io, the Roman Catholic, and the Greek
Catholic.
“Saturday night, when a comfortable crowd
welcomed back Williamson’s opera co., Florence
Young and Grace Palotta, as Dorothy and
Lydia respectively, showed up in their short
skirts and shepherdess bodices as a pair of well-
grown, well-nourished lasses. Florence, bulkier
of body, although more embonpointy and less
lissome than her colleague, boasts, nevertheless,
slimmer ankles and smaller feet. But Grace’s
muscular limbs and long, capable feet, though not
conducive to beauty in short skirts, have much
to do with the confident ease of her stage-carriage.
To be graceful, a large-sized beauty needs adequate
pedal support. Venus herself had big feet, one
supposes, judging from the tradition that she
made a noise when she walked.’ Grace looks
best in poudr4 white satin at the Castle ball;
cousin Florence makes her mark in the flowing
white lawns and laces of her dressing-gown. As
Squire Bantam, Howard Vernon is a perfect old
picture 1

. Enlarged Sleeve ” is a prevalent sartorial con-
dition which is going to be worse before it’s better,
typical arm-wear for daylight Coronation func-
tions at Ome, and for our own coming Cup rai-
ment, is stitched in flat pleats at the top, widen-
ing into huge, baggy puffs, above the cuff. The
skirt to be is pleated all round, but sets into a
shaped piece, fitting flat on hips.

“There is now a correspondence as to
how to dress on next to nothing per
year. The only woman of my acquaintance,
who looks 1 the _ thing ’ on a compara-
tively small sum indulges in a really good
coat and two or three skirts of the best materials,
made at the best places. On this basis she rears
her sartorial reputation. At sale-time she goes
round the best shops, picking up bits of good lace,
silk, &c., to be made into blouses by herself. One
cant have too many blouses, say experts. When
washing shirt-blouses first came in, the then Mrs.
Rupert Clarke came out from ’Ome with 100.

Ihis item has led for some time a peaceful
and retired existence on the notice-paper of
N.S.W. Assembly :
-M?/. J ; T °- h ■ Fitzpatrick to move : That, in the opinion
ot this House, the system at present in vogue of treating
as ordinary revenue moneys received from the sale, bv
i° r • other I wise . of the public estate, is one that
should be immediately discontinued ; and this House
urges that all future receipts from this source should be
set apart as the nucleus of a fund from which to meet the
s s orations in regard to interest on the National
oeot, and for the purpose of meeting, as far as possible,
tne cost of extending our railway system for the benefit
of the producers.
The writer would give 2d. to know exactly what
Mr. Fitzgerald thinks he is doing in this matter.
N.S. YV. s gross land revenue is now about
£2,100,000 a year ; the interest on the public debt
is over £2,500,000. Therefore all the land revenue
goes in interest now—and a good deal more. If
the £2,100,000 land revenue is specially set aside
to meet interest, what difference will it make, and
how much further advanced will the State be, and
now much will be left to build railways with? If
the land revenue were set aside to pay off the
principal of the debt there might be some sugges-
tion of progress about the matter.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
R.O. Archbishop Murphy, of Hobart,
finished his 87th year on Wednesday of this week
(Waterloo Day). He was born on the very day of
the battle.
It was rather rough on ex - Premier
Peacock that he should have happened to cele-
brate his defeat by tripping over the dining-room
carpet, falling against a chair and fracturing two
ribs.
N.S.W. Chief Justice Darley is a cousin
of the brewer Guinnesses, the head of whom,
Lord Iveagh, is supposed to be the richest
Christian in the United Kingdom, and is reputedly
worth £14,000,000.
Lady Lettice Grosvenor, to be married to
ex-Governor Beauchamp, is said to be a cousin of
Lumley Hill, the ancient Queensland Parlia-
mentary campaigner. Lumley still entertains
hopes of being Premier of that State.
The new N.S.W. Governor, Admiral Raw-
son, and family are homoeopaths, and are at pre-
sent being attended by a Sydney physician who
practises homoeopathy. The story goes that H. E.
will help to establish a Homoeopathie Hospital in
Sydney.
The Age reports that a well-known business
man was knocked down and had his nose severely
bitten during the peace celebrations at Bendigo
(Vic.). The man who goes to an Australian Jingo
peace celebration unarmed must expect something
like that.
If Seddon scores an Imperial Governor-
ship—as is said to be his ambition —it will not
create a precedent. Frederick Weld, once Maori-
land’s Premier, was subsequently Governor of
W.A., then of Tasmania, and later of the Straits
Settlement.
The long, thin Shiels reappears in Vic.
politics as Irvine’s lieutenant. The Casterton
man’s presence in a Ministry is regarded as proof
positive that it will die young—Shiels being the
pale, medium sort of politician who is only possible
in anaemic governments.
A cable :
The dominant note of conversation among the sur-
rendered Boers is a eulogy of British fairness, as shown
by the execution of two British officers.
What a handy thing is a scapegoat ! It is an
animal no Ketchener should be without.
No one has yet publicly sympathised with
Gapt. Wallington over Hopetoun’s sudden depar-
ture. It did look as if Wallington had a good,
steady job this time. Now the next G.-G. may
not want him. He ought to, of course. But
G. -G’s. are not famous, as a rule, for horse-sense.
“ W.”, Adelaide, writes :
It will be 34 years on Tuesday since Adam Lindsay
Gordon shot himself at Brighton (Vic.) and we have not
yet had a really adequate edition of his poems. Pity he
didn’t live in the days when even such encouragement as is
now given to Australian literature was available, for he
would assuredly have done more and better work.
The new Vic. Premier Irvine is a nephew
of John Mitchell, who “did time” in Bermuda
and Tasmania half-a-century ago for seditious
journalism in Ireland. Yet Irvine loyally assisted
Peacock and Trenwith to eject “ Tocsin ” Findley.
It is a wise •■ebel that knows how his nephew will
grovel.
How Methuen must curse in his innermost
heart the vanity of old man Roberts which
prompted him to postpone the direct attack
on Gronje’s “surrounded” laager until the

Irvine, the new Vic. Premier, is 44 years of
age, and is the youngest portfolio-holder in his own
Ministry. Murray and Taverner come next in
point of age, and Cameron is turned 60. While
studying for the Bar in Melbourne, Irvine, who
was a B.A. of Dublin University, earned his
living by teaching.
Chief Justice Griffith will be 57 to-day
(May 21); President Baker, of Federal Senate,
will be 60 to-morrow (Sunday) ; and King Richard
of Maoriland, who originally came “all the way
from Lancashire,” will be 57 on Monday. Also,
Lord Kitchener, of whom Seddon was good
enough to approve recently, will be 52 on Tuesday.
Hopetoun’s resignation is not the first in
Australia tendered by a Governor on the salary
question. When Vic. Parliament cut down Gov.
Barkly from £lO,OOO, plus £5OOO allowances, to
£7OOO without allowances, that potentate handed
in his official gun. A lot of haggling ensued.
Parliament sprang to £lO,OOO without allowances,
and Barkly stayed on.

Four old Parramatta King's School boys
have been killed in the Boer war. But General
Sir Baker Russell, of the Imperial service, is the
showiest feather in the old school’s military cap.
Several times of late years the school thought its
General would be taken into action, but he was
kept at other work. Baker Russell and Justices
Windeyer, Innes, and Simpson attended the
school at the same time.
N.S. W. Principal Under Secretary Critchett
Walker will be 62 to-morrow (Sunday). His
father (the Rev. James Walker, M.A.) was once
head master of King’s School, Parramatta, and
the son was born on board the Arabian, off the
coast of the Cape of Good Hope. He began
official life as sessional clerk of the Leg. Assembly
in 1856, so that he has nearly completed half-a-
century as a public servant .
Cabled :
The Edinburgh University has arranged to confer the
honorary degree of Doctor of Laws upon the visiting
colonial Premiers during the Coronation festivities.
It is known what sort of men now*nd tU-n rise
to be Premiers in Australia, and that any Univer-
sity of standing should take them in a lump on
any account and clothe them with its degrees, is
scholastic degradation. Foreign Universities
have been charged with selling their degrees;
what is this but a sale ? They may not be con-
ferred for money, but certainly they are not con-
ferred for merit.
Dear Bulletin.—Re your par. (June 7,
page 15, col. 4) concerning the Duke of Grafton.
The present holder of the title is father of the
Colonel H. J. Fitzroy who first went sheep-
farining and was then Chief Justice Way’s Asso-
ciate in Adelaide, and who, among other things,
had to pay (by order of the court) tailor G. P.
Doolette so much a week on an unsatisfied judg-
ment summons. “Fitz,” as he was affectionately
called 20 years ago, is still Earl of Euston and a
great masonic swell, but will be Duke some day if
he lives long enough. All the rest of the story is
correct. — Yours, W.

One of the victims of Philp’s retrench
ment” is F. M. Bailey, the competent and
enthusiastic Q. Botanist who draws at present the
huge s<dary of £250 a year. Bailey’s book, “The
Grasses and Flora of Queensland,” is nearing
completion, and would save tons of money to the
observant squatter and selector, but Philp and
Co. (who paid £170,000 for those historic dredges)
want Bailey’s mite, and they are going to get it.
“E.D.” concerning a certain Aus-
tralian Judge’s theology:—
Mr. Justice Williams, of .Melbourne, is an Aus-
tralian Japhet in search of a religion. Since
being delivered of his rather juvenile book on
rational religion, an uninspired re-hash of Tom
Paine’s “ Age of Reason,” His Honor has been
wallowing in the theological bog, but at last he
is satisfied that he has found firm land for the
soles of his feet in a sort of better-class spiri-
tualism. The Judge employed a recent
“ Pleasant Sunday Afternoon ” preaching to a
large audience on the folly of any belief in the
resurrection of the physical body. He
pointed out that a man who lives to be 70
has used up, perhaps, ten physical bodies.
“Which of these is to he resurrected?”
cried His Honor, with the air of a con-
queror. But Justice Williams believes in the
survival of the “spiritual body.” In the
future, he says, we will be the same indi-
viduals without the physical body, and possi-
bly “ after reaching a certain stage we may
be re-incarnated in a physical body.” How
desperately His Honor strains at the gnat, how
cheerfully he swallows the mosquito ! What
proofs are there that the destruction of the soul
is not at least as complete as the destruction of
the body ? Many phenomena are advanced to
establish the existence of some mysterious soul-
force outside of life. What then ? Your man
of 70 has had as many spiritual as physical
bodies, using the term spiritual as synony-
mows with mentality. Which of his spiri-
tual bodies survives ? The boy of seven
is spiritually as different from the man of
70 as he is phvsically. Provided there is
a spiritual force apart from the material
form, what proof is there that it is not
disintegrated with death, and-dissipated
as the decayed body is, and joins the
mass of force, the play of which has re-
strained even the most serious sceptical
minds from declaring physical death to
be Finality ? But from the belief that the
individual perishes utterly there is no
escape hut in the phantasies of faith. When
you have admitted that a certain some-
thing in man called spirituality is indestruc-
tible as are the atoms of his physical body, you
have done no more for religions and beliefs in
future lives on “spiritual planes” than you do
in agreeing that the dust of Egyptians who lived
10,000 years ago may still be blowing about the Sahara,
and may go to the making of other physical bodies
10,000 years hence. Man only worships th# world-forces
he cannot yet comprehend. Familiarity breeds contempt
and infidelity.
Died at Glasgow, April 4, Jas. Wyllie
(“the Herd Laddie”) draughts champion. During
his visits to Australia he beat all the local stars,
though some drawn games were scored against
him. The old man, who was very deaf, made his
living by playing draughts, for which he charged
opponents Is. per game. He left behind him a
few lrundred pounds, the result of his invariable
formula : “ Putt doon yer shullin’.”
Of the 75 Reps., 24 are teetotallers,
27 non-smokers, five vegetarians or almost
so, while six—Barton, Forrest, Knox, Mac
Paterson, M‘Eachern and Braddon—are reckoned
epicures. The Tas. fire-eater, Cameron, studies
his palate in the matter of smoking, as he imports
his own cigars; while Yes-No Reid carefully
avoids rice, tapioca and other starchy matters —
for obvious reasons. Crouch, of Corio, set. 33, is
the House’s baby ; Braddon, 73, its father.
Sachse, M.L.C., the last hair on the tail
of Irvine’s Ministry, got most of his experience in
North Borneo as consulting engineer to the Govt.,
and did some minor exploring work. His predi-
lection for the society of an ignorant people drew'
him into Vic. Leg. Council. Next to Agar
Wynne, who has eyes on the Premiership, Sachse
is the most ambitious man in the House of Rich
Landlords, but much bidding for office has only
secured him the position of boy-in-buttons to the
new Cabinet.
Baroness Burdett-Coutts has invited the
Australian XI. to gaze upon the Coronation pro-
cession from her house in Piccadilly, and if the
boys take any interest in human curios they will
accept this chance to be entertained by a unique
personage. Miss Burdett Coutts was a million-
airess of 24 when she attended Queen Victoria’s
coronation in 1838. She relinquished her spinster-
hood at the sufficiently ripe age of 67, and her
husband, who is about 40 years younger, has been
carrying out his contract ever since. There never
w:is such a potent argument in favor of late
marriages for maiden ladies, or such a beautiful
warning to the young men who consent to make
them happy.
Tasmania’s senior coroner and J.P., Cap-
tain Edward Dumaresq, celebrated his 100th
birthday on June 10. Mr. Dumaresq belongs to
an old military family. He and his elder brothers,
on account of their father’s services in America,
got a military education at Sandhurst at Govern-
ment expense, and were fitted into commissions in
the British Army. The eldest brother, Henry,
was made a colonel by the Duke of Wellington on
the field of Waterloo ; and the second son,
William, fought through the Peninsular war.
The centenarian enjoys good health, a slight deaf-
ness being his only trouble, and life on his splen-
did estate at Longford still seems to have attrac-
tions for him. Mr. R. H. Dumaresq, M.H.A., is
the captain’s dutiful son, and was born in 1839.
Primate Cowie, of Auckland, who retires
from duty at 71 years of age, was always a staunch
advocate of reciprocity between Australasian and
British clerics. If British clerics were admitted
to work on this side without examination, he held
that Australasian clerics should be similarly
admitted in Britain. He himself was educated
and ordained in England. He is a competent
musician, and has followed mechanics as a hobby
all through life. Repairing the settlers’ clocks wasa
regular habit with him when visiting through the
country. H has constructed a very useful and in-
expensive sewing-machine, and has made many gar-
ments as specimens. In religious matters he has
been always liberal, and has been a friend to the
Maoris since his arrival. He was preceded in
Auckland by the first Bishop Selwyn, who
returner! to England, and became afterwards
Bishop of Lichfield.

The new Victorian Ministry is not likely
to prove strong. Irvine is personally popu-
lar, and- has good points, but he is not heavy
metal enough for a leader. Shiels, as Treasurer,
will show himself too clever by half. He is a
financial theorist, who studied his subject at
Melb. University. The return to office of Bent,
the first of the busted laud-boomers to get on his
feet again, is not likely to be hailed with wild en-
thusiasm. Taverner, while a member of the
Turner Govt., made a reputation for inactivity
and general indifference. Davies and Reid,
M.’sL.C., the reps. of property, have both held
office before. The former is useful as a legal
critic ; the latter is the sort that passes in a crowd.
The three Ministers who hold a portfolio for the
first time are M‘Kenzie, Murray, and Cameron.
M‘Kenzie is a man of solid capacity, but is
handicapped by blindness; Murray is famed
for his pluck, unflinching Radicalism, and light
and airy speeches, but has yet to give evi-
dence of the possession of ballast; Cameron, who
was a game-keeper in the old country, has deve-
loped into a painstaking farmers’ representative.
The pale and blameless life of Dean
Cowper ticked out at midnight on Saturday, - 4th.
With the exception of eight years in England, the
Dean had seen 82 years of Sydney life—from
Bligh to Rawson, Admiral to Admiral—and
had done as much to mould it as the
conventional Church of England clergyman may,.
The old man dying in the meagre cathedral close
was not more apart from the Saturday night,, m
George-street, than his church from the seething
life of the great city all around, The Dean came
out as chaplain to the A. A. Co. a few years after
John Dunmore Lang arrived “on his own. It can
hardly be said the two worked side by side. The ope
belonged to that painfully severe place on the hill
overlooking the two quays and the Rocks, the,
other w hoi 1 v and exclusively to the Cathedral close.
•A painted saint in a church window would have
been as effective in direct warfare against Hell
and the Devil in Sydney as Dean Cowper,
next to the bishop chief patron of the Anglican
Church; and never a saint, painted or real,
gave less cause of offence. Still, his was nob
a futile life, if a great example is anything; and
to the thinking man it is much—perhaps more
than all the fierce efforts of the evangelicals. The
Cathedral bell and its message in Sarah Grand a
“Twins” book is suggested by the Deans life
and death. Regularly as the bell, undoubting as
the hell, he tolled out his message, and now the
requiem tolls. Still George-street roars along,
and to the same old tune.

Aboriginalities.
To Contributors. — ln the case of ethnological and
natural-history items, etc., always give date and
place of experience ; or as nearly as possible. Thus
many interesting observations will have scientific value.
Animals in search of fodder in West
Queensland adopt strange devices. Horses have
been noticed of late pawing in stagnant pools and
then dipping down heads and bringing up a
mouthful of swamp weed. Others have actually
been seen diving, and appear to be acquiring some
proficiency in tiie art. A Dalby paper tells of
cattle confined in paddocks with river frontages
dying of starvation, while others, in prickly-pear
enclosures, without any water at all, are actually
fattening. Prickly-pear is being brought just
now into Brisbane by waggon from seaside resorts,
as the only salvation for suburban dairy cows.
Bananaland will have to protect the pear yet in
place of exterminating it.

Nature’s remorse in some parts of the State.

A mob of 3000 sheep, moving up towards
Broken Hill from the lower Darling desert,
near Menindie on a patch of wild spinach, the
gift of a recent thunder-shower. One gorge of the
unaccustomed green stuff killed 700 on the spot.
* * • *
Several South Australian traders on the
lower Murray and Darling have not been able to
make a single trip since Jan. No trade has been
possible on the Darling during 20 of the last 30
months.
* * * *
On the Northern N.S.W. rivers, the Colo-
nial Sugar Cos. is giving 10s. per ton for maize-
cobs, delivery taken on the river bank. The cobs
are crushed, and, when mixed with molasses, form
an excellent fodder for stock, and large quantities
are being sent to the drought-stricken districts.

“ Hensco ” : Re the use of saltbush as a
vegetable. On the Barwon and Darling, 40 years
ago, what we called the “cabbage saltbush” was
pretty generally so used. I have eaten it
boiled whole with beef, and chopped up and
mixed with sausage-meat.
A Narrabri pastoralist tells that “ in 1877
and 1878 he lost by drought over 2000 head of
cattle and about 12,000 sheep. The present
drought practically started in 1896. Out of 9000
sheep he purchased in that year, 7400 died. In
1898 he bought a run near Cassilis, and his losses
in sheep totalled 22,000, and, although he has only
stocked at the rate of a sheep to six acres, his
flock has been reduced to 10,000, half of which are
being fed in Narrabri and the remainder atßurren.
Probably not one of his 10,000 sheep would turn
the scale at 201 b. if dressed.”

A bit of drought-stricken Western Queens-
land
Sixty miles a day on a large, heavy conveyance—a bad
cross between an Indian gharri and a hearse—drawn by
five fly-spotted equine skeletons. A stifling day. The
■ky a great, flaming oven. Grass withered ; water gone ;
famine-stricken, blear-eyed bullocks, staggering patheti-
cally. Grotesque oarioatures of sheep—mere bones
holding up the pelt. Plain after plain of parched wilder-
ness. Not a tree nor a shrub for miles; nothing but
dust and desolation. Skeletons and bones everywhere.
A foetid smell in the air. The hateful crow flickering
from carcase to carcase in fiendish exultation. Not a
soul to be seen for 60 miles. A station 1 No hearty
welcome here—only a depressed grunt. The mail—the
only thing to look forward to in this wilderness of desola-
tion—a lingering life of misery ! The sun goes down a
ball of fire ; then a shanty appears on the horizon. Two
looney fellows lounging on the verandah, stupefied with
liquor. A careworn, weary woman, prematurely aged,
nursing a sickly-looking baby. Four silly-looking chil-
dren—old-fashioned, sore-eyed, dirty. A heavy drink-
sodden host. A horrible decoction called whisky ; hard
salt junk, damper, and a black unnameable stewed sub-
stance. A sleepless night, battling with mosquitoes, and
other insect pests. Daylight, and a repetition of the
same thing. It was Sheol I —Trotter.

“ M.” :Re droughts : Hay or chaff could
never be delivered for less than £4 a ton, and could
hardly be grown and reaped locally for less than
£2, and then not more than two tons of it to the
acre. Green ensilage can be put down for less
than ss. a ton, and a big crop of maize or sorghum
will cut between 10 and 20 tons per acre. Some
few sq. miles of the native growth would, in a
good season, cut nearly as much, and ensilage will
keep for years, and will not burn. Squatters of
the old sort assert that nothing of the kind ever
did or ever will pay. “Let’em die when they
must and stock up when the rain comes.” But
the fathers of these men were just as pig-headed
about fencing runs. Time alone and such fierce
experiences as those of this year burn conviction
into them.
* • • *
“ Tom Collins ” : Re strong men. A Vic-
torian threshing-machine owner wagered a bottle
of gin that one of his men would
carry a bag of wheat a mile at a
stretch. An exceptionally heavy bag
was conveyed to the next cross-road
in a cart, and, disregarding our advice
to take the load across his shoulders,
Dinny backed up to the cart, and
gripped the top lugs, while his boss,
as a further security, looped a swag-
strap round each wrist and sewed
the fall to the bag. The first quarter-
mile was covered without discomfort;
then the load began to sag, defying
Dinny’s attempts to buck it forward
into position But he pegged away,
stooping lower and lower, till his
back was horizontal, while the after-
part of the bag drooped over his
hips like the tai 1 of a platypus. Pre-
sently the lugs slipped through his
paralysed fingers, and the swag-
straps bit into his wrists till his hands
were the size of boxing-gloves and
the color of beet-root. His breathing
grew stertorous, his face turned from
brick-red, through purple, to black ;
but still he lurched along, game to
the last, only to go down 200 yards
from .his goal, with the bag on top of
him. Had he got the bag up properly
it would have been an easy win for
his boss.

* * * *
Things are in such a state in West Queens-
land now, writes a Bulletin correspondent, that
the trees are actually dying, and birds are be-
coming quite tame. When camped they are a
nuisance—scores of butcher birds, magpies, and
swagmen’s chums surround you, and will take
food out of your hand.
« • * *
“ Bynna ” : The unexampled severity of
the present Australian drought is evidenced by
the fact that in various parts of the continent the
indigenous trees are dying off—a thing which, in
N.S.W. at all events, has never been known
before. Present writer’s father, though he often
questioned the blacks about the long-dead belts of
myall, box, &c., on the Bar won and Namoi, could
never get any reasonable explanation—a circum-
stance which seems to indicate that if the
phenomenon is due to such another fierce drought
as the present one, its occurrence must have been
in the remote past. In northern N.S.W., com-
mencing, say, at the Liverpool Range and reach-
ing northward to the “Snowies ” in New England,
considerable areas of timber, chiefly box, are
either just dead or dying; and the same state of
things extends far out to the westward.

“ Don ” : Me Bowman’s bottle - tree
(“ Werrimee,” B. 31/5/’02). I have known it used
as fodder by German farmers in Dugandon dis-
tnct (Q.)» in the ’ZO’s. Horses fed on it were only
watered twice a week, and drank very little then.
Bottle-tree has also to my knowledge been used
on the Dawson for a year or more, and I know
several people who keep stock fat on it.
IT HAS EVEN REACHED OUT-THERE!
The Q. drought is having a dire effect on
the native animals. Not only have they died off
in tens of thousands from want of water, but the
Q. unemployed, for the sake of making a little
tucker-money, are shooting them off in thousands
wherever possible. The native bear and opossum
are the worst sufferers.

A drover, travelling from Glen Innes to
Grafton for grass with a mob of dying cattle,
thus notified the Glen ■ Elgin squattage of his
approach: “A funeral procession will pass
through your run to-morrow.”
“ Bob *R.” : “Will J.’*(B. 22/3/02) is a
trifle out of date in his appreciation of a bag for
extinguishing bush-fires. With the fires we get
about Junee (N.S.W.) a bag would be of as much
use as a penny squirt on a city blaze. Bush fire-
fighting is now reduced to a science, in which the
water-cart-cum-pump is a leading feature. Hardly
a station in Riverina which has not at least half-
a-dozen of these “extinguishers” ready for
action at a moment’s notice. No skill required to
work them —“elbow-grease” is all; and one
water-cart is worth 50 beaters with bags. Note,
however, that I’m referring to Riverina stations —
not to 4-acre allotments.
• * * *
“Matchbox”: Dr. Ahearn’s theory re
vital loss in tropical climates still holds despite of
A. C. (3/5/’O2), who ad vances the exception as a
general instance. The average Australian is
developing the race-characteristics of the
aborigine. He’s spindle-shanked, large-footed,
narrow-chested, his nose is flattening, and his ears
(Lord preserve us !) —ask the pedagogues. The
area of ear is becoming alarming. Every kid is
provided with two flaps capable of voluntary move-
ment —a development to meet a demand ! This is
a great country for flies —and the future Australian
looms up as something that might fill the situation
without using his hands.

“ W.J.S.” : He tall men (“ Riverina,” 8.,
22/3/’O2). Some years ago I was working on
Durham Downs Station (Cooper’s Creek, Q.),
whereon 48 men were employed as carriers,
shearers, shed-hands, etc., and of these 13 went
from 6ft. 2in. to 6ft. 6in. I have also been in
two strike-camps, in which the tallest men were
respectively 6ft. 7in. and 6ft. s|in. Dozens of
others went over 6ft. Some thousands of the
finest specimens of physical manhood on the con-
tinent were in the ’9l and ’94 camps.

“ Thurla ” : Some effects of the drought
on bird-life in New England district
(N.S.W.). Found a kookaburra, t’other
day, at foot of tree, dead but still warm.
Some days later found another cadaver
alongside the first. Both jacks were mere
skeletons. Since then have found asoldier-
bird and a magpie dead under trees, both
evidently from starvation. Mags, jacks,
’keets, and other parrots crowd around
the only surface water for miles, and
furious battles take place for precedence.
The mags and jacks keep a sharp look-out
for any scraps of food from the camp. A
few months ago I rescued a butcher-bird
from the fate of the fly in the amber. He
had found a dish of strained honey, and,
probably mistaking it for water, had
plunged in for a bath, and got caught.
When found only his head was free, and
that was being gradually submerged as
more honey kept falling from the strain-
ing bag.

This method of calculation, by the way, is

dealers — e.g., 51bs. of grapes @ 3d. a lb.=
15 pence=l/5 ; 7 melons, @ 4d. each =2B
pence=2/8. It often comes off, and when
it doesn’t, apologies are easy. The decimal
coinage is arriving.
“Zero”: His name was Medcraft. He
came to our office to pay his account, and handed
in a cheque which he had just received, and which
was payable to “Medcalf or order.” I told him
to endorse it “Medcalf, not Medcraft,” and
directed his attention to the script in the body.
“Well,” he said, “I can write my own name
all right, but ” (pointing to the script and holding
his head sideways) “ how can I make that long-
tailed ‘l’? Fact is, mister, I ain’t got no ‘ I.’ ”
Ultimately I pencilled the endorsement, and lie
traced it over with ink.
“F.” : Was with a droving-party on the
Murrumbidgee some years ago. Spent a very
merry evening at a wayside pub., and one
of our men brought a bottle back to camp
and thrust it into a Wellington boot.
Next morning a rush for revivers, but
to our dismay the bottle was found
broken and the stuff spilled in the
boot. Each one eyed it dubiously tdl
at length Greasy Bill, the owner of the
boot, remarked that it must be all
right, as ha washed his feet pretty
regular.

It isn’t all beer and skittles employing v.
Chinese cook. The other day, at a station near
Cloncurry, the musterers observed their Chow
oulinary artist pouring out some 301bs. of flour, a
quantity of tea and other table necessaries on the
ground. When these preliminaries were com-
pleted John rolled himself on the mixture — and
died then. What fantastic notion had taken
possession of the poor devil’s brain? He was a
great opium-eater.
• * • *
“ Alone,” re a big breeze :
“ Alone ” : Just had a look along the track (near
Bacchus Marsh) of the last cyclone that swept across
Victoria. Have seen nothing like it in the Windstorm
line since the willy-willies of the West. Sound gums two
and three feet tnrough, that wouldn’t tear up by the
roots, are snapped off short. Others are blown right out
of the ground ; and some have every leaf and limb torn
off, whilst the bare trunk is standing. In one place a
dairy-farmer’s house was struck by it, and blown into
fragments. Unfortunately the owner, a very old man,
and a young fellow employed on the farm were caught in
the storm near the house. The young fellow declares it
blew him ten feet in the air—anyway, as a matter of fact,
it picked the old man up, hurled him over a fence, and
literally dashed his brains out on a post. When the
cyclone had passed the heavens started pelting lumps of
ice so large—well, so large that if I stated the size of some
of them it would only provoke the sco.is of unbelievers.
« * * *
“ W.N.” : Re recent Bulletin par about
the benefit of a volcano in Central Australia.
There is not much likelihood of one breaking out;
but since a large part of Centralia is said to be
below sea-level, why not cut a canal and let the
sea in ? We should then have a vast evaporating-
pan, instead of burning plains—nurse of hot
winds in the interior. The Barrier would then be
a coastal range and western N.S.W. and Q.j also
S.A. would probably have a regular rainfall. The
canal could be made navigable by small craft,
anyhow, and the inland sea would be dotted with
fertile islands. What we want is “Australia
Facing the Problem of the Interior.”
* • • •
Two residents of Yarram (Vic.) were
lately fined 2s. 6d. each with costs for keeping pet
kangaroos, and the decision of the Bench has
caused considerable feeling in the district. Many
residents are keeping ’roos for the purpose, so
they say, of preventing their extinction, and
object to give them up to the police as the law
demands. What is the matter with the pet ’roo,
anyhow ?
» * « *
At Fremantle (W.A.), t’other day, three
newly-arrived niggers, covered only by a blanket
apiece, were marched through the main street with
chains round their necks, the ends of the chains
being held by a policeman. They were being
taken to the lock-up, en route to the nigger prison
at Rottnest, for some trivial offence against their
“owners.” Westralia is proud of the fact
that, in proportion to its population, it spends
more on its aborigines than any other State. But
the money goes in salaries of white officials, in
chains flogging-gear, prisons, and arrangements
for enslaving the nigger, and he doesn’t seem to
appreciate all this care and attention.

FLIPPY’S SUICIDE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We found his broken boots, his hat and coat
Beside the river, ’neath the brooding trees,
And lying near, a touching little note
That told us all in such sad words as these :
“ Fair sick o’ this here blanky place I be.
In heaven there’s rest fer pore old blokes like me 1 ”
’Twas signed with Flippy’s name. We almost cried.
We’d known him long, and knew no good of Tim,
But sins are all absolved by suicide,
So each recalled the best he could of him.
“ I mind the time he drank six quarts o’ beer
Within the hour,” said Ned, and shed a tear.
We gathered up the things, and mooched along
To Quinn’s to drink Tim fortune in a peg.
The bar was filled with bellowings of song—
A cheerful crank was dancing on a keg.
Some kindly soul had filled him to the brim.
Well might we gape—the happy drunk was Tim !
“ Here, here, I say ! ” disgusted, Oody cried,
And flapped the letter under Flippy’s nose ;
“We thought you was a blessed suicide ! ”
“ Thatsh ri’,” sighed Tim, reminded of his woes,
*• I meant to drown myself, but Pete, I think,
He come an’ ast me could I do a drink.”
E. DYSON.

23 HUNTER STREET.
Writes “ Quartpot ” :
D’Arcy Uhr*s claim (B. 17/5/’O2) to have crossed orer-
land from Queensland to Port Darwin prior to Geo. de
Latour is quite incontrovertible. One Cox had also done
the journey, as had Phil Saunders, Adam Johns, and
mates, who had discovered the Union reef before I .atour
thought of the trip. The above-named were, in the N.T.
when I arrived there, late in ’72. Latour did not arrive
until ’74, and I well remember his coming. He and his
party had sp it up ere reaching the Roper, and two of
them, Elvey and Nation (I think this was the name), made
a bee-line for the Telegraph, which Elvey reached
somewhere about Daly Waters or Powell’s Creek, Nation
having died on the way. Latour related that the Roper
blacks, when he reached Leichhardt’s Bar, had brought
him food and done all they could for him, being glad to
see a white man again. The Telegraph Dep6t had been
deserted several years at the time, and they had no doubt
missed their flour and tobacco. We were much surprised,
therefore, some months after, when we found they had
murdered 0. H. Johnston and severely wounded his com-
panions, Rickards and Daer. These two managed to get
back to Daly Waters, but Daer died soon after from his
wounds. They had gone to the Roper, after lost, horses
and bullocks, and were attacked when they arrived at
Leichhardt’s Bar. This attack was supposed to be due to
Johnston’s resemblance to a man who had once wantonly
shot a black at the Depdb. Latour and Ballon at this
I ime were on the way to Queensland from Port Darv in.
Poor Ballon was killed by blacks on the journey, but
I atour got back to Queensland,

“ Gutiyah ” remarks :
J". D. Moore, who writes as an old-chum in 8., 24/5/’O2,
I take to belong to a class which is over-fond of blowing
about its “pioneering days.” Present writer knows a
good deal about the Fraser-trouble in Queensland, and to
a certain extent the raids made on hostile tribes at the
time were justifiable. But there was no excuse for the
atrocities perpetrated on some of the lubras who fell into
the hands of miscreants, who pretended to espouse the
cause of the Frasers merely for the purpose of gratifying
their passions. Also, the authenticity of “ Old Chum’s ”
experience is badly discounted by the latter part of his
article. He displays his ignorance effectually when he
asserts that in all his experience he never met a constable
who was worth a tinker’s anathema as a bushman. Has
he never heard of the constable who, unaided, captured
the Dora Dora murderers after they had made their way
from the Vic. border, right through N.S.W., into the
heaft of Queensland ?

“Chris.”: “Alone"
(8., 26/4/’O2) is a good pen-
name for a person who still
clings to the worn-out)
notion that a billy-can in
which the water ia boiling
will not burn the hand.
The reason that the heat is
not immediately felt is that
the blood in the oourse of
circulation from the heart
loses some of its heat before
arriving at the extremities.
If the hand, before the
can were placed on it, were
raised to the same tempera-
ture as the blood at the
heart, the sensation of
burning would be felt at
once. Let “Alone” put
two billies on the fire, and,
when both are boiling, pick
up one and put it on his
hand till he begins to feel
the heat, then put that one
down and replace it with
the other. If he keeps the
second one on his hand for
half the the time he could
keep the first, I think he
will find the picric acid
necessary. Ergo: it is not
from the boiling water that
the heat radiates slowly,
but from the body of the
person who is trying the
experiment.

“ Waif ” : Sydney’s mirage was hymned
'by Wealsman, but it’s not “generally known”
that Melbourne can show a good one on Mount
Medico, Collins-st. East. You have only to sit
‘down at noon in the middle of the road, a few
yards along Collins-st. from Spring-st., facing
west, your eye level with the road beyond the
•crown- The street ahead becomes a shimmering
silver stream. Cable ears are gondolas, and the
doctors’ broughams are fairy funeral barges. You
■ dreani you are in Yenice —till a car comes round
.the curve or the police intervene.
“Andy”: Found in this (Manilla, N.S.W.)
district, on May 24, 1902, a monster tuber, which
most people think is a yam. It weights bs.,
ia 4ft. in circumference lengthwise, and 2ft. Bm.
round the girth. Can any Bulletin reader state
whether this is a record yam for Australia ?
* * * •
On a Queensland station recently a black
boy was thrown from a horse and badly
broke his arm. Gangrene set in and the
only chance of saving the boy’s life was
to amputate the arm. But the station
possessed neither chloroform nor surgical instru-
ments. Finally the storekeeper on the station
tried the job with a sharp,knife. After lie had
cut off the arm Condy’s fluid and dressings of
carbolic oil were applied, and in time the wound
healed.

“W.W.”: Died here, recently, a collie
slut which, for the last six months, had suffered
from a running sore in the chest. Owner thought
she had been shot, and that the bullet wasstill in
her chest, but, when the body was opened, a piece
of wood, about sJin. long and fin. thick, was
found under the shoulder-blade —broken in two
pieces. The flesh had grown on to the stick at
one end.

“ Alick ” : Black gins catch lobsters by
tying a white rag to big toe and putting the
toe into crevice. Lobster hooks on ; gin pulls up
foot and sticks it out behind, where gin No. 2 is
waiting with a bag and detaches the lobster.
Once saw a gin fishing in holes on a reef at low
tide, when a wobegong shark fastened on to her
hoof. Wob. is not a vicious fellow, so he didn’l
“chew,” but simply hung on. Meanwhile, the
gin squealed like a demon, and t’other gin
promptly dropped the bag and cleared. The
shark opened his mouth to take a bigger hunk,
and the gin whipped her foot up—not much the
worse after all. Nevertheless, she lay down and
howled for a while. Then she got a big waddy
and went in search of the other lady.
A photo, post-card from Ada Colley
notifies The Bulletin that she has been singing
at the Theatre des Westerns, Berlin, in a dream-
like, low-necked dress, cut billowy at the sleeves,
and emblazoned with jewels and precious beads.
Ada, on the back of the post-card, is styled “die
Australische Nachtigall,” which title sits easily on
the soprano, who of yore used to toss her head
after achieving a miraculous top-note, and remark
incidentally that she had “got it that time.”
Probably Miss Colley is not more of a nightingale
now than when she sang flute music to Riekards’s
public for a fraction of the salary she commands
m Germany. But her plumage is finer, and her
pretty face and pose have the charm that comes of
long experience in “ star ” successes. From a
showy Australian girl she has developed into a
dashing woman of the world. Rickards could
afford to make a tempting offer to Ada Colley.
ADA COLLEY, U.? TO DATE.
“ Myall ” to The Bulletin :
In my opinion the rupture ’twixt the present N.S.W.
Lands Minister and the Farmers’and Selectors’ Association
referred to by “ T.” (8., 7/6/’O2) is of grave concern to the
State. Chief cause of said rupture is the improvement-
lease policy of Minister Crick, by which large areas
suitable for residential settlement have fallen into the
hands of local and absentee financial co’s. for periods
ranging up to 28 years. Areas so disposed of, capable of
supporting several families in comfort, now find employ-
ment for a solitary boundary-rider. Also, said financial
co’s. have no trouble in securing the greater part of the
station expired leasehold under improvement-lease,
despite all opposition ; while in the case of a struggling
squatter an improvement-lease proposal is mostly referred
to the local land board, and invariably disallowed.
