In the recent London libel action, Suffield
v. Labouchere (of Truth), Sir Edward Clarke’s
brief was marked 500 guineas, with refreshers of
50 guineas a day. It has been calculated that, as
the case collapsed early, this great gun’s time was
paid for at the rate of £43 6s. Bd. an hour !
Judging by letters from S’Africa, the
military censorship is an amusing thii g. All
books dealing with S’African history are taboo,
and even in Capetown public library the local
history department is locked up. Probably it
was discovered that the study of local history led
to sympathy with the Boers—anyhow, it was
evidently found advisable, in Imperial in-
terests, to cut off the supply of knowledge.
A wildly - ignorant censor stopped Banyan's
“ Holy War ” —he took it to be a work written in
defence of Kruger. He had never before heard
of the inspired tinker, and suspected him of being
Johannes Van Bunyan, or something to that
effect. All letters coining from disaffected locali-
ties are opened and read, and all books and
pamphlets are strongly suspected. Apparently,
Boers and pro-Boers are a reading crowd, for the
censor’s attitude of gloom towards books, and per-
sons who have books addressed to them, almost
amounts to a disease.
“ Inkoss ” writes :
Re “T’othersider” and his interview with returned
“ cohentingenters ” (B. 31/5/’O2), “ T’othersider ” is either
lying, or else his informants were, if they told him such a
bloodthirsty tale as that Boers W’ere regularly slaug-htered
when captured, because their captors had to feed them
out of four biscuits a day if they kept them alive. For
the 12 months I was in Afriea I always got “ five ”
biscuits a day—short rations were the exception, not the
rule, in the latter part of the recently-ended campaign—
and never did I have to forego a crumb for the sustenance
of the prisoners. VVe were with De Lisle’s Brigade
throughout the term mentioned, and saw as much
fighting and brought in as many prisoners as any
other column, yet not on a single occasion were
our rations affected by the prisoners captured. It
was the custom for the columns to carry “ reserve ”
supplies, and out of these the prisoners and women and
children were fed. During the whole of my service in
Africa I never saw, or heard of, a single act of brutality
on the part of a member of De Lisle’s brigade towards
either man, woman or child on the Dutch side ; though,
I am prepared to admit— and am certain, in fact—that
outrages disgraceful to men and dishonorable to the
British Army have taken place. The British are not all
saints, and the Boers are not all sinners, or vice versa,
but there is a mixture of good and bad on both sides, and
I even allow that there is more of the former amongst
the 250,00 . British than there was in the Boer army of
66,000 men.
BROKE!
There was a day when I was fat
And young, when the girls called me Bert
When I wore eyeglass and top hat
A collar and a stiff front shirt.
Then I could get drunk like a lord
On champagne, portwine or brandy—
Now it’s seldom I can afford
A few pence for a small shandy.
I had then horses and I wore spats
I had my house, I had my club
Now they say I got snakes and rats,
Hy only house is now the pub.
They say I was handsome and fair
In the old days when I was flush
Now somehow I don’t part my hair
I’ve lost my soap and my toothbrush.
I have no friends I have no hope
Not a shilling, not half a bob !
I want three yards of a good rope
To give it up as a bad job.

HOUSE OF REPRESENTATIVES. ;
LOAN FOR PUBLIC WORKS.
What Mr. George Turner urged yesterday is
true enough, no doubt. But what a scathing
commentary is it on the false position in which
the States stand to the Commonwealth. Trans-
ferring, as they did, their greatest sources of
income to the Federation, they made no provi-
sion for any proportionate expenditure going
with it; and they are in the position of hangers-
on and waiters on Providence, saved only by
the legal need of the Federal Treasurer return-
ing three-fourths of his gross income.

State, let them rid themselves of tnis foul
blot. Failing this, let the people of the
State demand their expulsion from the Go?
vcrnment.—Yours, &c., J.A.F.

“The Queen (April 2Gth) concludes its Paris
letter with an account of the golden wedding f§te
of ‘Madame Marchesi,’ otherwise the Marquise
de Castrone. The old lady ‘looked radiant attired
in white-satin damask draped with beautiful lace
and a white tulle and lace toque ’ —the Queen
being a truly feminine journal in its disregard for
commas. Of course there was a concert for
Madame’s pupils, a solo by Mdlle. Elise Parkin-
son getting special praise. Melbourne’s smartest
will be interested to hear that Lady Maud
Warrender (out here with . the Brasseys) sang a
couple of Massenet songs, and the Duke of Man-
chester’s bride was present bound in mauve cloth,
strapped with taffetas of the same color. Duchess
®f Marlborough, the Stuart Wortleys, and a
choice assortment of other guests are mentioned,
but never a word about the Marquis who married
Marchesi 50 golden years ago. That aged person-
age is seemingly a very minor item in his clever
wife’s belongings. He is said to do a little on the
Stock Exchange, but judging from the silence that
enshrouds M. de Marquis, he doesn t shine as a
speculator.
“ The two enterprising Melb. girls—Miss
Grantham and Miss Lucy Barton—who meditated
a musical descent upon Tasmania last summer
but were advised to abandon the idea, have now
definitely decided to go West and give some
‘ recitals ’ at Perth about the end of the month.
Miss Grantham, the sweet soprano whose
Marguerite performance was a particular success
at the last show of Marshall Hall’s Conservator-nun
pupils, will supply the vocal numbers, whilst Miss
Barton puts in piano solos and accompaniments.
With or without the. assistance of local talent
these adventurous artistic spirits ought to find a
welcome in Perth. Meantime they will give a
send-off recital in Melbourne.
“ A humorous chorus-charmer relates ttiat
always, before she trips on the stage, she runs her
hands carefully over her tights, etc., to see that
no pins are puncturing her pads, for, she says, it is a
common trick with the bona-fide plump girls, when
waiting in the wings, to make a cushion of the
padded unfortunates. This same jolly stripling
relates how puzzled the mistress of the ballet was
when her tall, lean figure was added to the ranks.
On finding she wasn’t an expert dancer, the mis-
tress nearly wept. She ran along to the manager
and, throwing her almost at him, exclaimed,
‘ Look at this bag of bones ! In Heaven s name,
what can I do with her ? ’ . ‘ Fill her out, and
make a boy of her; the height will do. At
any rate, do something, for I’m pledged to place
her in the ranks.’
“The next marriage of interest will be that of
Miss Muriel Miller and squatter Currie. After
that ‘Whernside,’ Toorak, will be bereft of any
daughters, and Mrs. Miller will find the big ball
and reception rooms filled only with the joyful
romping memories of the nice bright girls
she always chose for the companions of her well-
brought-up brood. Sons in a house never com-
mand the same joyful conditions. Their parents
friends are entertained, not their own, since it is
the prerogative of the boys to be chummy with all
sorts. If they are not, then they are prigs.

“The sensation of the day at the recent Hunt
Club races was provided by a newly-engaged gad,
who had her dress hoisted above ordinary kilt-line,
so that onlookers ha I not only a full view ot
her fancy hose, but of her honi-soits as well.
Mrs. Lindsay Inglis (nee Pratt) scored a more
legitimate effect with a turquoise-blue Eton suit,
with white velvet fixings, and a nut-brown toque
with pheasant-wing decorations. Her blue silk
underskirt was a bright idea, Being of the same
tone as the frock, the eye was not rudely shocked
when the outer garb was hoisted.
“ The sauciest of the block promenaders is so
extraordinarily precocious, and so prodigal in the
display of her fatted calves that no one believes
she is only midway in her teens. This makes her
angry, so she secured a copy of her birth-certificate
from the Registrar of Titles recently, and carries
it abroad in her pocket. An unbeliever is im-
mediately confronted with the startling credential.
“ Conductor Slapoffski, in charge of Musgrove’s
‘Thieves’ picks up extra crumbs as a singing
teacher. Nellie Stewart puts her voice in his care
and May Beattie, ’tis said, follows suit. Madame
Slap, has been all the way to Rockhamption (Q.)
to sing to an ‘Orpheus Club’ concert. .It s a
wonder the Northerners have so much juice left
in them, considering that all their water-supply
lagoons have lately turned to mud.
“M. Paul Maistre, the French vice-consul in
Melbourne, has published a very readable and
useful yam about Australian life, human and
vegetable, under the title of Hans la Brousse
Australienne —a book that certainly ought to find
its way into all schools, hereabouts. Ihe local
color, and the up-to-dateness of the language
commend it to anyone who has sought knowledge
of French-as-she-is-spoke from old-fashioned class-
books. M. Maistre prattles, for instance, of that
Eurely Australian institution the bunyip—‘Le
unyip, dis-je, est un monstre legendaire erhe par
I’imagination et la superstitieuse terreur des noirs
indigenes du continent australien. _ Suivant ces
pauvres sauvages, le bunyip serait un animal
enorme, d’une force prodigeuse et d’une laideur
repoussante, bete apocalyptique dont on a donne
les descriptions les plus fantasques et les plus
fastastiques, differ ant toutes plus ou moms les
unes des autres &c. &c.’ As a lesson in French
tongue for Australian students, young or old, the
little work is seductive from cover to cover.
“Miss Eloise Juno’s rooms were crowded,
t’other night, when her pupils showed what they
could do. They spoke most distinctly, made
themselves heard without effort, and made no
attempt to be stagey. Edward Lauri holds that
Melbourne wants a dramatic school. I have
always thought so, because elocution-teachers, as
a rule not professional mummers themselves,
train the heads and hands of pupils, but leave walk
and general deportment to chance. Miss Juno is
one of the exceptions.
“ Suburban best parlors on palaver afternoons
shrill still with tales of the weird sayings of the
Yankee Missioners, but the heart-whole admiration
once bestowed on the astute sensation-mongers
has been turned off at the meter as suddenly as
the Gov.-General’s gas, and a deprecatory snigger
now accompanies the most telling yarns. The
story about the husbands and wives who had
never quarrelled being invited on the. platform
and there and then prayed for as ‘liars,’ you
probably recognise by its fine old chestnutty
flavor. But it is true that one of the missioners
read aloud to a meeting a letter from a woman
(suppressing her name), and then remarked cheer-
fully : ‘ Now, hasn’t that woman infernal cheek
to write and ask me to pray for her ? ’
“ All the smart little school-girls now play
cricket. Merton Hall (C. of E. Girls’ Grammar
School) sent a team lately to the Presbyterian
Ladies’ College. While the home team was
gallantly defending its wickets a tiny mouse
suddenly ran across the pitch. All play was
suspended, of course. The girls with one accord
ran after the mouse.
“ Mrs. Robert Harper, Treasurer George
Turner’s wife, and Mrs. Everard Browne were
among the favored few commanded to a private
leave-taking of the Countess at Govern-
ment House. One of the dames, who always
held an admiration for Lady Hopetoun and
no sign of fear in her heart, relates a moving
tale of that hour’s private audience. ‘ Australians
never understood the Countess, and she was
oonscious of this,’ is the statement of another
favorite, who found the Countess cordial and
communicative at the final hour. All say
she was almost sorry to go. The souvenirs sent
her were fully appreciated, and of the few floral
offerings the Countess wore some, carried some,
and sported besides on her hat-brim a big, up-
rearing hanger of silver and rubies that had been
presented by a sporting friend.
Yours affectionately,
AKENEHI.
“Alas! where are the yeomanry of England
to-day ? Swallowed up in the devouring maw of
the aristocracy, 500 of whom, calling themselves
peers or hereditary legislators, have possessed
themselves of from one sixth to one-fifth of the
entire national soil! Out of 509 peers 438 hold
14,250,000 acres. To these 438 untoiling lilies of
the field the cultivators, or rather the consumers
of agricultural produce, pay a yearly tribute called
rent, of £12,000,000 sterling!”— J. Mokiuson
Davidson.
Said Jonathan to John.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Said Jonathan to John “ My boy, the woes of age
are grieving you—
Your joints are not so supple as they were in days
of yore;
Your wits are not so keen to-day, and youthful
vigor’s leaving you ;
Your fist is not so heavy in the games of I eace
and War.”
Said Jonathan to John again, with air of gentle
breeziness:
“ I’ll always lend a helping hand when trouble
gets you down ;
I’ll run your steel and iron trade without the least
uneasiness,
And take a hand at shoddy when the trade is
looking down.”
Said Jonathan : “Moreover, John, your ships are
rather lumbersome
(Of course we know Britannia sweeps, the mistress
of the sea) —
But still, for one a bit infirm, your navy’s rather
cumbersome ;
And Ocean wants a Bouncing Boss —about the
build of Me ! ”
To Jonathan said John again : “ How truly good
and kind you are,
But ’tisn’t I that hold the reins— 'tis Money runs
the show t
And if to take our British trade and navy both
inclined you are,
And Money strikes the bargain, then of course
they’ve got to go.”
Said Jonathan to John again: “I listen, John,
delightedly—
I’ve just another bargain while the matter’s on
the brew :
We’ll take your Church and King and Queen—
and run the lot unitedly—
And, if you’ll name your figure, why—we’ll take
< your Island, too ! ”
PHILANDER FLAM.
The Inky Way.
“ A.D.” : Arriving in this great country
at the cocksure age of 16, I had the pleasure of
exchanging courtesies and a little borak with a
certain caustic Melb. editor (Sara. Winter, of the
Herald). Pushed for cash, I chose journalism
as a trade likely to bring in dollars suddenly,
and without outlay. Stepped into the above
newspaper-office, one cold, wet day, interviewed a
sub., and was shown into editorial where 1
discovered a long-armed, loudly-objurgating fury,
thrashing paper to right and left, and with
dripping uen delivering deadly upper cuts between
written lines. The editor-fury looked up as 1
entered, and the fever in his face was as malignant
as though his left hand were in the fire. ‘ Want
journalistic work?” he snapped. “What are
your qualifications?” He stood, up, .with his
back to the fire, rubbing his hips with
clenched hands. I told him that I had never
written a line for print, but, with the salt
of several oceans still in my hair, and
possessing a first-hand knowledge of four conti-
nents I had a confident desire to start on his
paper at once. Quoth the editor : Foreign
items will keep, and we’ve no room for the travel-
note journalist. Can you do something local and
simple ? Could you give us something interesting,
say, on sheep’s-head ?” I bowed mine and stated
meekly that I would look in with my copy next
day. A moment later he was back in the chair,
thrashing, tearing, cursing, waving his long arms
and stamping paper under his feet. Saw him
again following day. “Ah,” he. said, “ you re
the sheep’s-head man.” Then in a voice of
thunderblast that reached the outer offices and
was heard by the cold-fingered comps, above:
“Here’s the sheep’s-head copy, ahoy there !' 1
handed him the MS., which he grabbed with both
hands, while his protruding eye rushed over the
pages. “ Nothing here about, sheep’s-head, ” he said
sharply. Then, with a horrified gasp, I tore the
MS from him. “Excuse me, sir, I must have
handed you the pig’s-feet article I've written for
the Argus.” Then the editor-fury sat back and
laughed consumedly. That was my introduction
to Australia’s champion objurgator, bullock-ex-
perts not excepted.
. • • •
The great ambition of every daily paper is
to score a “scoop”—to give news of an event a
full day ahead of its rivals. The Herald recently
brought off a scoop in Sydney with the tidings of
the doings and execution of Morant and Hand-
cock. A “scoop” of the Daily Telegraph m
the same city was to record Judge Windeyer’s
famous break-down when the news of Dean s con-
fession of guilt came to hand. The Judge was
just starting on another helping of turkey at a
private Adelaide dinner when the news came, and
he was so affected by this belated vindication of
his first prejudice against the accused that he
wept into the gravy-bowl. A parson present
carried the glad tidings to Bonython of the
Advertiser, and thence it went to Melbourne
Age and Sydney D.T. Adelaide Register,
Melb. Argus, and Sydney Herald completely
missed the picturesque sob.
• • * •
Dr. Leyds is reprobated by Sydney D.T.
because he adopts the expedient of supplying to
European journals pro-Boer articles, and news,
attractively written-up, free. “The temptation
offered to economical proprietors or lazy editors is
irresistible.” That was just the trick of the anti-
Bill party in N.S.W. (of which D.T. was the soul)
during the Federal contest. Articles were sent,
gratis in proof, to the country papers against
Federation, and often inserted through sheer lazi-
ness on the part of rustic editors. Also it was an
old trick of the Foreign-trade party, which sup-
plied articles gratis damning Australian indus-
tries in the interests of M'Millan and the-other
agents of the German and British manufacturer.

“Pressman”: The success of The Bulletin
has noticeably ameliorated all the Australasian
dailies When present writer was dumped down
in Melbourne, in the early sixties, the style# the
Argus and Age was a slavish imitation ot the
nonderously-dull London Times, and was anathema
maranatha to all visiting Americans. On one
occasion, in those days, a junior Age reporter
ventured on a mild joke m a par. David byme
was furious, and wrathfully exclaimed : Do you
think my paper is in competition with Melbourne
Punch?” Now both Age and Argus copy the
Bulletin style in a considerable degree, and go
to the ill-advised length of printing comic pars,
about bank-shareholders’ meetings.
* • • •
Sydney Daily Telegraph has always
pursued the policy of giving its “staff” a
fairly free leg. Members are not troubled
much with rules and regulations, and the
go-as-you -please air of the office has been
found good for encouraging individuality. Pre-
sent most notable members Courtney, chief
leader-writer, a man who is always leading about
in leasii a brutal bull-dog of a fact, to let it loose
on any one guilty of imagination ; argumentative,
satirical; a fine article-writer in opposition ; con-
fesses to a profound hatred of journalistic life.
Nall, chief sub-editor, once a churchwarden m his
leisure moments, at which time he was a just,
severe ruler; now a grower of roses in his spare
time, and a just but gentle boss; his kindly
eye to the “young men,” and his accurate
judgment of news, does much for the paper. Peters,
chief descriptive reporter (represents the paper
in the Federal Parliament, and inflames the Cabinet
to very madness by his artistic caricatures of their
proceedings), a vivid writer with, at his best, more
than a touch of Kipling in his style ; deferential
to office-boys, crusty with magnates. Papered,
leader-writer, a passionate lover of his work, who
has climbed to his high position past every rung
of the ladder; an effective writer with a valuable
couch of imagination. Norman, night sub editor,
a quiet, golden-haired man with cameo features,
suitable more for a leader-writing billet, since a
passion for correct English is not to be best
gratified as “night-sub.” Ward, “leader of the
staff,” most pertinacious and careful of news-
getters, has been, as Government-office man, in the
secrets of as many Ministers as Critchett-Walker.
Piper, with more knowledge of the world than the
average pressman, has a sedate, Johnsonian style
and much savoirfaire. Furley, the admiral of
the shipping department, credited with running
the best “Shipping Column ” in daily journalism.
There are others; and John Farrell, who deserves
a whole article to himself.
• * • *
“ N.” : Every city reporter knows a class
that one of the craft has named Strulbugs, after
the purposeless literarians in “Gulliver’s
who had to keep ear-flappers as servants to remind
them when anyone was kissing their wives. lhe
Strulbugs form the nucleus of the audience at
pretty well every gratis concert or lecture, no
matter whether it is on politics, music, geology,
anthropology, gynaecology, any other ology,
mining, racing, religion, spiritualism, womens
rights, teetotal ism, licensed victuallers’ rights.
Atheism—whatever can be imagined so long as it
is on the nod. The Strulbug absorbs and absorbs,
like the fat weed, on Lethe’s wharf. He never
says anything. The reading-room Strulbug is
another variety. And there is the police-court-
audience Strulbug.
* * • *
A truly remarkable paper is La Prensa,
of Buenos Ayres. It is in a building which cost
£400,000.
On the ground floor, besides the usual business office',
are a luxurious consu ting room, where a doctor, with
live qualified assistan s, attends to over a hundred
patients a day free of charge; and a museum, where all
the products and manufactures of Argent na may be
inspected without charge. On the second floor is a well-
stocked library, particularly rich in legal, medical, and
engineering works, and open at all times to students
without payment. Adjoining it is a room where Spanish
is taught at the expense of the journal. On the same
floor is a hall that serves as a general rendezvous for the
people of Buenos Ayres. On the third floor a palatial
suite of apartments is set aside for the use of distinguished
visitors, who are pressed by the editor and proprietor,
Senor Paz, to make it their home during their stay in the
city. There is in addition a Salon des Fetes, copied
exactly from the drawing-room at Fontainebleau, where
literary, charitable, and scientific meetings are held on
the invitation of La Prbnsa. Elsewhere is a hall given up
to political gatherings, and also a laboratory where far-
mers may have samples of soil analysed free of cost All
this magnificence comes out of the profits.
* * «
When the late Justice Webb (Vic.) landed
in Melbourne as a young man in ’53, he went to
Edward Wilson, of the Argus, and asked for em-
ployment as a shorthander. Ihere were two
other similar applicants at the time, and Wilson
sent the trio to a public meeting, saying that he
would expect them to read their notes to him on
their return. On the test being applied, it was
found that Webb, who had been trained in Gur-
ney’s office in London, read his hieroglyphics as if
they were newspaper print, while the other two
could only decipher their records with great diffi-
culty. The appointment was, therefore, given to
Webb. His reputation in the office was that of a
first-class shorthand reporter, but, being utterly
destitute of imagination, he was never known to
write a decent paragraph. It used to be said of
him that had a fire destroyed half the city he
would have mentioned the fact in three plain sen-
tences. After a time, Webb became Govt, short-
hand writer, and eventually studied for the bar.

daily paper of democratic tendencies —an evening
paper for preference.

POINTS
Jingo topsyturvydom reveals Premier John
See leading the advance guard of Australian pro-
Boerism. The Anti-War League unanimously
embraces him.
Loud howls heard in N.S.W. Leg. As-
sembly, t’other night, appear to have arisen as a
result of Premier See’s refusal to guarantee a
certain member a “stiddy” billet in connection
with the proposed new Licensing Act.
Nothing short of a miracle was the con-
version of the Jingo-dingoes in Australia. They
went to bed at night with chops foaming for Boer
blood ; in the morning, peace having been secured,
their approval of the generous terms given to the
Boers was full and complete.
David Syme is surely losing his grip on
affairs and men. He helped to wreck the
Peacock Ministry because the Laughing Man
wouldn’t throw Trenwith overboard, and he now
has to play second fiddle to the Argus in piping
for the incurably-Tory Irvine.
Printed that Philp is going to reconstruct
his team, also that “Little Bethel ” Cribb, now
State Treasurer, is to be replaced by brother-in-
law Forsyth. The bre’r-in-law industry is a
flourishing institution up North and with careful
pruning and manuring is better than the fictional
rich uncle.
As in Victoria so in S.A., the bogus
“Reformers” have tripped themselves in their
hurry, and, by advocating the handing over of
the railways to a private co., have stupidly torn
the bait away and shown the hook. And yet
these wolves want to know, in an injured tone,
what makes the Laborite object to their scheme of
economy.
RUNG OFF.
It was recently announced that Lord Beauchamp was
presenting a portrait of the King to Sydney Art Gallery,
H.M. having consented to give sittings to Mr. Longs tail,
the Vic. artist. Mr. See communicated with the Agent-
General, requesting him to ascertain if the Queen would
also give sittings to that artist, so that a companion-
picture might be placed in the gallery. A cable was
received yesterday from Mr. Copeland, intimating that
H.M. had graciously agreed to the proposal. It has been
suggested that the ladies of Sydney should provide the
funds for the portrait of the Queen.

The desperate effort of Sydney Daily
Telegraph to secure the remission of the fodder
duties fizzled out woefully. Fuller, M.H.R.
moved an amendment on a Supply Bill (practically
a vote of censure) dealing with the matter and a
miserable seven voted for it. Six of them were
N.S.W. men who vote against the Govt, always,
i n principle.
A suggestion which ought to be popular
with the daily papers, so anxious for “ economy ”
on the part of the State, is that all Govt, advts.
should be inserter! in the Gazette and Hansard
only. At least £5OOO a year could be saved in this
way. Contractors and other people interested in
Govt, advts. would buy the Gazette then, and
the advts. would have all the publicity required.
The programme of the new Country Party
in N.S.W. politics is mainly to the effect that it
wants liberal encouragement to settlement, liberal
assistance and cash votes to aid the mining in-
dustry, liberal aid to the pastoral and agricul-
tural industries by the State furnishing a water
supply, a liberal supply of railways with reduced
freights—in fact it appears to be a Begging Party,
pure and simple.
Sydney Telegraph ex-editor Brient writes
to his old paper from England to say that Dr.
Graham, the one-time Freetrade member for
South Sydney, is “coquetting with the reciprocity
heresy which is eating into the very heart of Cob-
denism in England.” And at this end of the log
the new D.T. editor is declaring that Cobdenism
in England is as sound as ever, and is founded on
the imperishable rock.
The thin and melancholy Kirton, given an
lion, position in the new Vic. Ministry to con-
ciliate Ballarat, is debited with intending to
retire from politics and devote himself to his other
business as soon as this Parliament lapses—with a
gold railway pass for life on his watch-chain. A
man may be the last joint in a Vic. Govt.’s tail
for half-an-hour, and it gives him the freedom of
the State railways for ever after !
Apropos Kanaka fitness for plantation
labor. During the last month 18 males died in
Bundaberg district and of these twelve were
Polynesians. And this in a district where the
adult male white population is several times
greater than the adult Kanakas. No wonder the
pro-slavery organs are able to brag that work on
the plantations improves the average Kanaka
physique. It does, indeed —by exterminating the
weeds.
R. T. Vale, M.L.A. for Ballarat, has tele-
graphed wildly to Vic. Premier Irvine that the
inclusion of John Murray in his Ministry is “an
insult to every loyal subject.” The Vale push
called the Boers cowards and mean, treacherous
ruffians, and so on, while the war lasted, whereas
it now describes them as brothers and desirable
fellow-subjects ; and Murray’s unpardonable sin
is that he spoke of them all along just as the Vale
crowd speaks of them now.

for life—become pensioners on the Department
till death do us part. A boon that is as good as
£7O a year to each. This is Economy 1

The one clever bit of dialectics in the long
censure debate in N.S.W. Assembly was Minister
Crick’s challenge to the Opposition on their re-
iterated accusation of the domination of the
Labor party:
When hon. members talked about the domination of
the Labor party he would ask where it came in on the
statute book. What Acts did the Opposition want re-
pealed? Was it the Old Age Pensions Act, or what Act
was it?
Unless the Opposition is prepared to repeal, what
is the use of talking about domination ?

Several murders have lately occurred in
the Solomon Islands, and the Government agent
of the labor schooner Rio Loge, which has just
returned to Mackay with recruits, says that the
cause of the natives’ unrest
is understood to be that they are angry at hearing that
they would shortly be stopped from going to Queensland.
The Government agent greatly feared that there would be
more murders committed on that account.
Alarming news indeed —but modified consider-
ably by the fact that the Govt, agent who tells the
story is the paid servant of the piebald Phhp
Government. At all events, if the Kanakas are
so easily roused to murder they are far too savage
to be let loose on Q. sugar plantations.
Sydney Telegraph asks “Are we Too
Generous to Politicians ? ” and points out that in
the U.S. the State politician is, on the average,
worse-paid than in Australia. But, unfortunately,
the U.S. politician, being badly paid in many
cases, often pays himself, and the corruption of
American politics has become a stench and a bye-
word. Every Australian State should abolish its
useless Upper House; reduce the Assembly; pay
the remaining members better ; and, above all
things, pay its Ministers better, and give up the
foolishness of expecting really able men to devote
their lives to politics for the chance of managing
for a few years the concerns of a large country, at
less than the salary a bank manager gets for a
steady life billet.
The wildest man in Vic. politics just now
is schemer Outtrim, whose cunning over-stepped
the mark to such an extent in connection with the
Irvine no-confidence motion that it was impos-
sible to give him a place in the new Govt.
Outtrim—“trim” is good, and so is “Out ’’—has
been a man to reckon with at all times during the
last 10 years, because he hasn’t a scruple about
changing sides if there is the slightest chance of
his getting on top. When Irvine’s motion was
on, Outtrim had a counter move on hand, but
missed his opportunity, and, while he was outside
relieving his feelings, the division was called for.
He is alleged to have carefully counted the
numbers before voting—on the winning side.
And, spite of numerous Age suggestions, Outtrim
found that he had trimmed himself out.
The fodder question is still doing duty as
a stalking horse for the importing gang, hut the
public yelp grows feeble nowadays. If N.S. W. or
Q’sland wants to import fodder duty-free, it is
only necessary .for tlm See or Philp Government
to explain that it will* pay the duty and the thing
is done. The State Government gives its cheque
to the Federal Government for the amount of the
duty, and gets the money back in the division of
the surplus at the end of the month, and the
pastoralist has got his hay or lucerne duty free,
and the State Government has paid nothing, and
the Federal Government has lost nothing. If it
was fodder that was wanted, instead of a chance
to heap mean slanders on the Government which
is against Kanaka labor and which refuses to
further subsidise certain daily papers with free
postage, this would have been done long ago.
All the high-falutin re the impossi-
bility of legislating against Japanese im-
migration in the face of the Anglo-
J&panese Alliance is utter foolishness
in face of the fact
that the Japs,
actually legislate
against us, and
have stringent
laws to prevent
the overrunning
of Chrysanthe-
mum Land by a
horde of white
shopkeepers.
None of the Aus-
tralian commer-
cial magnate!,
who demand the
free entry of Japs,
for the sake of
commerce, can go
to Japan and start
selling Australian
meat retail to the
Jap. housewife. Tt
must be sold through a.
little brown agent by little brown butchers
in a little brown shop owned by a little brown
subject. They don’t apply any language
test. They simply prohibit the white trash
competing, and, if it does compete, it is run-
in—and a Jap. prison, according to all accounts,
is a beast of a place. Also, without any such pre-
cedent, the idea is utterly absurd. The Alliance
was formed for a definite purpose —the protection
of joint interests in China, it no more affects
Australian immigration than the fact that Jones
and Smith are in partnership to sell potatoes in
George-street affects Jones’s right to the sole
control of his own wife, married to him for
housekeeping and other purposes, and living with
him at Potts Poiut.
Though there is much talk about retrench-
ment very little is being done. The only serious
attempt at anything was the abolition of Wragge
and even that has been repented of. The fact
hasn’t soaked in yet that the Commonwealth
barely musters 4,000,000 people, and 4,000,000
people can’t afford to maintain seven Upper
Houses, six Agents-General, six official weather-
prophets, five or six Govt, astronomers, six Chief
justices, seven Governors, nine Government
Houses, six Coronation shivoos, and a whole lot of
other like things. Not one sacrilegious hand has
been laid on the blessed ark of any one of these
frills. When real retrenchment is proposed
there immediately arises a clatter about the
necessity for doing things “in a way commen-
surate with the Dignity of this great country.”
A fair specimen of the reforming State
politician :
Mr. Meagher gave emphatic expression to the view
that the imperative duty of the present time above all
else was to exercise a thorough economy. ... He did
not object to getting money for reproductive and neces-
sary public works, but it was clear there would have to
be economy somewhere. (“Hear, hear.”)
Pretty nearly every politician said the same
thing. They all wanted to be drunken total
abstainers, seeing cold-tea snakes in strict moder-
ation, and to raise enough revenue to meet
expenses without increasing taxation, and to be
strictly economical without cutting down the
influx of loan money, and their haste to explain
that they didn’t want their strict economy to
reduce anybody’s pay, or throw anybody out of
work, or deprive anybody of his culvert was
pathetic.
Now that all three Melbourne daily papers
have taken one attitude re Parliamentary reform,
the well-managed Ballarat Courier boldly takes
the field in opposition and has scored well by
doing so. The Courier people have, since the
start of Federation, been making sturdy efforts to
demolish the power of the Age and Argus in-
land, and have by no means failed in their fight
against city monopoly. Their first big move was
in the disclosure of some of the important tariff
information so jealously guarded by Kingston,
and it turned out correct enough, too. By sup-
porting Peacock’s Govt, against the virulent met-
ropolitan attacks, the Ballarat daily has also made
itself pretty certain of getting as much of the poli-
tical news that’s going as any of the Melb. papers
—the policy of which for years has been first to
manufacture “situations” and afterwards to go
round and bullyrag the little local politicians into
carrying out their schemes.
The Barton Government’s proposal to
spend £3OO on religious services at Coronation
tune fell through most dismally. Nothing ever
deserved its fate more completely, and all the rest
of the proposed Coronation frill deserves to go
with it. If the people really want to rejoice at
that time there is no reason why they
shouldn’t, but all the artificial processes of being
glad on the Estimates, and being drunk by deputy,
and being loyal by sending some one else to the
war, and being enthusiastic by owing the money,
are objectionable. A lamentable tendency to fall
back on the Government, a leaning towards the
follies of State Socialism, and a dropping away
from the bracing principles of Private Enterprise
characterise the Fat Man of to-day. Illumina-
tions and the sending of contingents to aid in
non-Australian wars, should be all left to Private-
Enterprise. while the State hustles round ami,
attends to its railways.
They really have a vague idea in Maori-
bmd that Seddon stopped the war. Witness this
fr m a leading article in Canterbury Times :
His arrival in South Africa has been followed by what
are probably the most significant indications of an early
declaration of peace which have yet reached us. The
Premier will himself be the first man to smile cheerily at
this charming coincidence, and to suggest, jestingly, that
it is an obvious case of cause and effect; and no doubt the
incident will be a text for many cheap witticisms from
those whose professional jealousy bids them maintain at
all hazards that no good thing overcame out of Nazareth.
But this gently humorous side of the subject suggests
one of more practical significance. Mr. Seddon has, of
course, no official standing in South Africa, and he unit
take no formal part in any deliberations or negotiations
affecting the peace proposals. But we know him, and,
knowing him, we realise that a man of his pronounced
virility cannot fail to make his presence felt, if not
among the absolute leaders of the Boers, at any rate
among such lesser lights as he may be thrown in contact
with. The very personality of such a man is likely to
appeal to the peoples of South Africa, and it may be
taken for granted that Mr. Seddon will find out a way to
impress upon those whom he meets something of the
benefits of the free and enlightened constitution we
enjoy in this colony.
There is something very beautiful in the Maori-
land paper’s remark—evidently made to prevent:
people falling into an otherwise most natural
error—that Seddon wouldn’t personally conduct
the peace negotiations, but just beneficently buzz
round.

VITADATIO
Canterbury Road, Mount Pleasant,
Tunstall, Victoria.
Mr. PALMER, June 1,1901.
Dear Sir,—Last November I felt very unwell and had
tO'kake to my bed. I called in a doctor, he examined me,
and said that I was suffering from Tuberculosis of the
Bowels. He prescribed for me, and, not feeling better, I
decided to call in another doctor who examined me also
and said the same thing. He also prescribed for me, and
still feeling no better I was strongly advised to take
VITADATIO, and after taking it for a short time I began
to improve, and eventually regained my long lost health.
I was three months in bed and suffered a great deal of
pain, and had it not been for VITADATIO I believe I
would have died ; but to-day, I am thankful to say, I am
in .good health, and have gained a stone in weight, and
can honestly recommend to any person suffering as I
did.—Yours faithfully,
ELIZABETH DENNY.
We certify that the above is correct.

NEWMAN, CHEMIST, 82 King-street, Sydney.
N.S.W. M.L.A. Dacey, in the recent vote-
of-censure debate, made some remarks about the
Sydney daily papers which were very carefully
suppressed in their columns. He pointed out
that the country paper proprietor at, say, Bourke,
had to pay 13s. a cwt. freight on his printing
paper, whilst the Sydney printed papers went up
free. The services done by the railways to the
great dailies he calculated as worth at least
£40,000 a year, and he demanded that the amount
should be charged to “ the corrupt oligarchy ”
running the morning papers. Mr. Dacey con-
cluded :
I impeach these papers with having received illegally a
subsidy out of the public coffers; I charge these men
with having received that subsidy indirectly in the shape
of having their merchandise carried free. I want to lay
emphasis upon the word illegal. The best proof I can
give hon. members that it was illegal is this: that if it
had been legal they would have gone to the law courts to
force the Postmaster-General to continue the free car-
riage of their papers.
And not a word of this in the daily press 1 Truly,
Hansard has its uses.

i Francois D’Assise, the physically-incap
able consort of giddy ex-Queen Isabella of Spain,
died in Paris, the other day, aged 80. Isabella,
who is at last too old to be giddy, looked after her
venerable friend in his last illness, _ and his
daughters (by courtesy) also rallied to his bedside.
A humorous family gathering. The present King
of Spain is a grandson (by courtesy) of Francois,
though all the world knows that Isabella’s lawful
husband was in no way responsible for the birth
of his wife’s offspring. A harmless, pious old
fellow, he was foisted upon the young Queen by a
Court clique, in the hope that she would die
childless, and leave the Spanish Crown to pass
into the Orleans family, her sister being married
to the Due de Montpensier, son of Louis Philippe
of France, on the same day that Isabella took the
hopeless Francois to her arms. The opposition
clique defeated the Orleans intrigue by en-
couraging their Sovereign Lady in her natural
amorousness, and she provided, heirs to the
Throne at long intervals as time went on.
Francois was quite indifferent re the paternity of
his nominal descendants. He never quarrelled
with Isabella during her reign, and although they
formally separated in 1870 because the ex-Queen,
then fixed up comfortably at Paris, decided to be
giddier than ever, it was understood that Francois
took a pleasure in having the goodness to make
himself scarce. The friendship of an uneexed
husband for a merry wife has amounted to a,
gentle devotion in more cases than that of Francois
D’Assise and Isabella. Man, when free from
animal passion, has a clear view of his duty to
both sections of society; whereas, the average
man never quite understands what is fair to a
woman.

Irrigationista on Australian rivers can
work up a sweat any time by starting a, discussion
on winter watering. After long talking over it
the Mildura people have just voted in its favor by
a big majority. The argument is that a watering
in winter allows the sub-soil to get a soaking—the
surface mops up the summer drinks. Apart from
this special one Mildura gives four soaks a year,
at an average cost of 6s. 7d. per soak per acre.
Patents fob Inventions. —Arthur Griffith
an 1 Allen, International Patent Agents and Con-
sulting Engineers, University Chambers, Phillip-
street, Sydney. Patents, Trade Marks, Copyright
throughout the world.,
“A.C.H.”. Be South Australian educa-
tional sweating:—
In B. 31/5/’02 the writer doesn’t make enough of the
matter as regards the “ unpaid monitor ” and the
“ acting-assistant.” In the first place, young people who
display teaching ability—often with the hope of being
ultimately appointed—give their services for nothing. In
a great many cases the attendance at the school does not I
warrant their being placed on the staff, and as, of course, j
the head-master knows this, he should plainly tell these
young folk to seek a livelihood elsewhere. But it is ]
obviously to his advantage to retain the help he would
not otherwise have, and so he keeps them hanging
on by vague promises—I know of one girl who
has been teaching three years for nothing—wait-
ing patiently for an appointment, and it is quite
usual to wait a year or more. As to the second evil, there
are a great number of acting-assistants—mostly women—
who, having passed an examination (supposed to be about
equal to the test applied to a 12-year-old scholar) are
appointed at from £36 to about £60. But, be the teacher
genius or dullard, appointed thus, no increase ever
comes. Many persons have been working for years,
giving, in many cases, good, skilled _ labor — but,
because they have not been trained (i.e., crammed
at the University) their status remains the same.
It would be interesting to know to what extent the
department benefits yearly by this contemptible piece of
sweating. As for the discipline of the State schools, every
teacher would need the personal magnetism of a Napo-
leon, with the patience of Job, to conform with the regu-
lations —written and unwritten. For this and similar
reasons the flower of S.A. teachers have gone to the Far
West, where the education conditions offer able men
chances to rise; yet the bumble-headed department,
instead of taking warning thereby, actually last year cut
down the salaries of the newly-trained teachers. Now
S. Africa is asking for teachers, and many S’Australian
' instructors, disgusted with this parsimony, will gladly
go there.

* * * •
Antaeus gelding Antique dodged the cute
crowd at last and scored sensationally in Warwick Farm
Pace Welter, Saturday. Previous week at Canterbury an
apparent attempt to back him at the last moment scared
Mo., so that only a small portion of the “ commish ” went
on, but the public rushed and took anything offered down
to evens, and Antique finished a poor sixth or seventh.
At Warwick Farm, backed to win a big sum, he rushed
past the judge a 10-lengths winner. Antique is the most
promising sprinter seen out for some time. His dam is
Fuchsia, a daughter of Marvellous and Primrose, the
latter being by Reprieve, a son of Yattendon. Fuchsia
was the dam of Sunflower. Mr. H. O. White bred Antique
at Havilah. n
* * * •
Country performer Milos, who failed at Moore-
field, made amends in Warwick Farm Stakes, Saturday,
bv humping a heavy load of money, and getting home
easily. Quite recently he annexed a double at Tamworth.
Milos gives promise of proving a good wealth-accumulating
medium. He is a gelding that takes the eye well, and
can endure weight and travels fast. Present owner, Mr.
Inch, had that successful nag, Cast Iron, and did well
with him.
* * • •
Aged gelding Fairy Prince annexed Warwick
Farm Handicap (Im. 2jfur.) comfortably, Saturday, and,
i hough usually unreliable, acted so as to encourage
backing him again. It’s over two years since he won
before, and then on the same course, and in a similarly
named event, but distance was lm. 3fur. Fairy Prince is
by Staekdale out of a Stafford mare.
/• • •
Winner (Burraneer) of Newcastle Cup last week
seems to be a confirmed in-and-outer, for, though gallop-
i ig well on the track, he has disappointed his party very
often. At Canterbury Park a month or more ago, backed
heavily from tens down to a very short price, he ran like
a cart-horse. In Newcastle J.C. llcp., day prior to Cup
same prad lined up first pick, and finished among the
last of 16. Still his stable came again for the Cup, and
placed i g wealth down, too, to see him close on the
prize comfortably from weightily-supported first-favorite
Hazel Lad. Kuhn was up on Burraneer in both New-
castle engagements. The nag is by Gozo from Jenny
Wren, and consequently full-brother to Blue Nobby He
was bred at Tucka Tueka (N. S. W.).

* * • •
Sydney crack jockey Sixsmith fell fatally from
Darvin in Clifford Plate, at Toowoomba (Q.), through
stirrup-strap snapping. Since his apprenticeship with
loin Paytin, Sixstnith’s successes have been numerous
among them Doncaster Handicap on Parapet. Four years
ago he sustained a badly-fractured thigh on the training-
tracks, that stowed him away for months ; and at New-
castle, two years since, he had a fall which invalided him
six months.
* • • .
Crosscountry jock H. Underwood, after
several close shaves, met his death when riding Inkerman
in'Hanover Steeple, at Flemington. Nag, an unreliable
lepper, negotiated all jumps hut the last of the three post-
aud-rail fences, andjthen his full weight crashed on Under-
wood’s chest. Schooling T.O. recently Underwood had his
collar-bone broken, and suffered another nasty tumble off
Grafter on the first day of recent Randwick Prince of
Wales’ Birthday meet. He had few equals at the busi-
ness, and was grit to the core ; his handling of the pony
Reckless over big ieps will be long remembered. He
never scored in a G.N. event, but knocked at the door
when, on Zouroff, he ran second to Buzzi, with poor Jack
Laverty up. Ever since that jostle at Randwick, two
years ago, when Underwood struck 11. B. M‘Laughlin
with his whip, bringing about a year’s disqualification,
things went wrong, and one fall after another culminated
in the Flemington fatality.

The fine cricket display by Grace and erstwhile-
Australian “Billy” Murdoch at Crystal Palace, Eng-
land, recalls the match 22 years ago (the fourth test, Eng-
land v. Australia) at the Oval, when Grace made
152 runs out of England’s total of 420. Australia
responded with 149 ; and, being 271 runs to the bad, had
to follow-on. Murdoch carried his hat right through the
Australians' second innings for 153, and set England a
task which was wiped out with the loss of but five
wickets. Then they were antagonists ; now, grey-haired
and bigger round the waist, they occasionally play side
by side, and show the young ’uns that there is fire in the
embers yet.

MR. J. B. BELFIELD, Amateur Billiard,
Champion of N.S.W.
Photo, by Horla, Sydney.
Amateur billiard supremacy of 3NT.S.W. tourney
ended last week. J. B. Belfield" won the honor and
attendant pots ; his last win was from Condon 500 to 328.
Condon’s best efforts were 68 and 25 and Belfield’s 74 and
65. During this competition Belfield compiled two fine
breaks—one (156 unfinished) is the best amateur accumula-
tion on record under existing rules. The other was 116
also unfinished. In one heat Belfield had the fine
average of 17-82 which would do a front rank pro; credit
Bast heat was between “ Mai ” Spencer (brother of ex-
champion ‘ ■ Pyramids ”) and previous owner of the
premiership (H. Rumball)' to define the runner-up.
Spencer won, 500 to 330 ; his best breaks were 80 and iff
while Rumball, shaping in poor form, couldn’t rise higher
than 60 and 27.

Victor Trumper will, if he continues his
successes much longer, put up a record for an Australian
cricketer in England. In 11 matches (in six of which the
Australians only batted once) he has scored 851 runs
more than twice the total of Darling, and almost as many
as Gregory, Noble, Hopkins and Duff comhined. He hi ■
four centuries, and without the assistance of a single not-
out innings, has an average of 53. Moreover, he has
proved himself a more than useful r-hange Iwt-ier.

Out of the first dozen matcnes (a third of their
programme) the Australian cricketers in England only
once completed two innings. That was in the match
against Yorkshire, which they lost. Thrice they did not
finish one even ; once they did not as much as start, and
lour matches were won in one innings. Practically every
engagement has been interfered with by rain. The XI.
had scored, up to the end of the second test-match. 3335
runs for 127 wickets against 2877 for 166 by their
opponents. J

Inter-State ’Varsity three-mile 8-oar race on
Port River (S.A.) included Sydney, Melbourne, and S.A.
crews. Melbourne worked at a scorching pace, with
Sydney well up and Adelaide hopelessly out of the hunt
ear| y. Leaders ultimately succeeded by over three
lengths from Sydney and without putting on any extra
pressure. Time (16.44) is a record for ’Varsity 8-oar
events. This was the 16th tussle, Sydney having won
eight, Melbourne four, and Adelaide two. Sydney’s crew
this tune averaged 11.2 and Melbourne’s 11.5. First race
was rowed on the Yarra in ’BB, when Melbourne won.
Next .year Sydney couldn’t muster an eight, and Mel-
bourne s reps, went to Adelaide and were beaten. Sydney
succeeded on the Yarra in ’9O, and the Parramatta in ’9l.
Cup subscribed for by Oxford and Cambridge oarsmen
was contested the first time in ’93, Melbourne proving
best. Next and following years Sydney scored in Mel-
s 0 nf * Adelaide. Adelaide succeeded a second time
in 96, then Sydney won twice (successively), and Mel-
bourne once. Last year’s contest fell to Sydney.

N.3.W. amateur golf premiership, decided in
connection with Hampden Cup competition, occupied
last and previous Saturdays—two rounds each on Botany
and Bondi links. Bogey was severe in both cases, but
play, on the whole, didn’t touch a high standard. Last
year’s winner, Mr. E. P. Simpson, finished first couple of
rounds 6 down, which was lucky, as he fell off much
next two—faulty putting lost holes and placed him below
Messrs. E. Macarthur, Oritton, and Dr. Frazer; first-
named was the only entrant to finish “ square ” in any
round. Mr. Simpson had a margin of 3at th» end.

Sixth round of Sydney first-grade Rugby
matches ended on Saturday. Three of the four struggles
were hotly fought. Glebe squelched Newtown, 8 to 5 ;
Western Suburbs beat North Sydney, 11 to 9; South
Sydney accounted for Balmain, 6to 3; ’Varsity fairly
walked over Eastern Suburbs, finishing best by 19 to 8.
Glebe, Western Suburbs, and Uni’s are now on the same
mark—top of the list—with North Sydney next.
• * • •
Dwarfed Cape Malay pug. (misnamed “ the
Kaffir ”) met Broken Hill man, O'Neill, at Sydney Golden
Gate A. 0., Saturday night, and loaded him up with
stoush during seven rounds of gory fighting. O’Neill
owns pluck and power, but has little skill. So the colored
gent went on making sausage-meat of the white’s coun-
tenance till the latter’s seconds elevated the “ wipe.” A
sympathetic crowd showered silver into the ring for the
vanquished in recognition of his pluck. “The Kaffir” has
held off fighting for long mainly through ha\ ing to meet
men of much greater height, and thus competing at a
big disadvantage.
* • • «
Having walloped Scanlan at Newcastle, McColl
is now due to fight Bill Doherty for the Australian
championship. They’re both about the same weight, 12
stone, and are neither over clever, but real born scrappers.
If Doherty hasn’t deteriorated since he hashed up lathy
black dude Felix in Sydney a year or so ago he certainly
ought to defeat McColl who is very deficient in science.
Still, the fight would be well worth seeing; as the ex-pick
and shovel man can take lots of battery before his
digestion gives out. McColl swings right and left mostlv
and occasionally uppercuts for variety, but could never
hit a clever man of the Foley or Jackson school. Still he
hits like a steam hammer when he gets a chance, and is
full of vigor and courage. At Newcastle Scanlan’s show-
ing was deplorable compared to his first bout with
McColl. Mick Dunn taught him a lot of boxing science
but when Scanlan got into the ring with the hurricane
he forgot the new science and couldn’t remember his old.
Hence this slogging.
* • • •
Heavyweights Yankee Jim Scanlan and Billy
M‘Coll bruised before several hundreds at Newcastle
Theatre last Friday night, for N.S.YV. supremacy, £lOO
a-side, and 60 and 40 per cent, of a big gate. Each scaled
within a few lbs. of the other (about 12st.), and shaped
trained to the moment. Scanlan money was most plen-
tiful—his backers had to lay sto 4 and 3t02 to “ get on.”
Betting was brisk and big till M’Coll people stopped.
M’Coll, fighting furiously from “ Time,” soon had Scanlan
floundering. The Yankee was floored twice in the first
round, and looked sure to go out before “ corners,” but
great grit, superb condition and Mac’s ignorance of how
to get aclincherin saved him. Offers of 3 to 1 on were free
now where same section refused 5 to 4 against their choice
before hostilities opened. Second round was all M'Goll’s,
but beyond flooring his man he could not go. Scanlan
rose each time stupefied and slow, but managed to sprawl
through owing to utter absence of method in M’Coll’s
efforts to finish. Scanlan isn’t a good fighter at best, and
this was his worst showing in Australia. He poked
instead of hitting, stood in all sorts of “ green ” attitudes,
and never once stepped in with his punches. M'Coll sat
down after the second round, pumped and breathing
hard, while t’other, while looking badly mauled, was all
right otherwise. Third round also went M’Coll’s way,
but, having tired, there was little effect in his thumps.
Then Scanlan came on some, and, through rounds 4 and
6, held his own so much that offers of 6 to 4 “ on ” him
were free; stiil he couldn’t deal it out hard enough to
hurt. M'Goll, freshening up remarkably in the sixth,
attacked fiercely and soon stretched the ’Murkan and won.
House was the largest ever seen in Newcastle, hundreds
going up from Sydney to view the struggle.
* • m *
’Murkan ball puncher Brown-Parker, whose
ring-name is “Kid” Parker, had a bad time with
Bananaland middleweight Phillips at Adelaide. Parker
flopped down frequently to avoid what looked like sudden
death, and was loudly jeered by the crowd. The thing
dragged heavily, for Phillips failed to use his chances, till
in round 6 the ’Murkan make-believe ran fair into Phil-
lips’ swinging right and passed out. This Yankee has
fought four or five times in Australia, and on each
occasion has come near to ruining the sport in the taste
of the public.

Perhaps the most interesting scrap to be
picked from the Sydney dailies’ obituary notices
of Dean Cowper was this bit from the D.T. man’s
interview with the Cathedral verger :
I knew the Dean’s father before him as archdeacon,
and I knew the Dean for 50 years, over 40 as Dean.
I came here when I was five years old. My daddy
was here before me—that is, here and at the tem-
porary wooden cathedral. The Deep was a plain, prac-
tical Gospel preacher. My word, clergymen have changed
since I can remember. There are clergjunen now—l hear
them say so sometimes who don’t believe in eternal
punishment—the real Hell that is ; but the Dean did it.
lie would never go with the times. He always stuck to
what he thought was right. You would never catch him
wavering this way or that, to please anybody.
It is indeed comforting to know, now that the
good old nonagenarian Dean is gone, that there
still remains someone about the Cathedral who
really believes in Hell —and no gammon.
Concerning a new Australian invention :
Hundreds of kinds of filters are sold, but as the pro-
tracted and exhaustive Dr. Sims Woodhead Inquiry (by
a conjoint Board of the Royal Colleges of Physicians,
London, and Surgeons, Eng.) showed, barring three or four
filters of the candle type, every filter vended not merely
was ineffective in excluding disease-germs, but water
absolutely pure before filtration, was found highly
charged with germs when drawn from the filter, thus
proving that the filters themselves were rapid
germ-breeders, and therefore actually increased
the danger they were supposed to prevent. Con-
sequence : Most of these filters have been discarded,
and the educated public has turned its attention to the
Pasteur-Chamberland and Berkfeld filters. These, how-
ever, have to be taken to pieces daily, otherwise, from the
accumulation of ddbris on the filtering medium, the
efficiency of the filter both in re quantity and quality of
filtered water will be reduced : a Pasteur-Chamberland
filter capable of filtering (while kept constantly clean)
sgals. daily can, by neglect of cleansing, be reduced lo less
than a pint of filtered water per day, and the can fie in
time becomes charged with disease-germs. As filter-
cleaning is troublesome (you never can trust the
servant who says she has cleaned the filter),
the manufacturers have raked the world, so far unsuccess-
fully, for an automatic cleaner. A Melbourne inventor,
A. I. Madden, claims to have filled the bill with his auto-
matic cleansing filter, the efficacy of which is certified to
by a host of Australian scientific authorities. The Madden
filter (now on view at 3 Wynyard-lane, Sydney) is
attached to the service-pipe in the kitchen,' and the
household or cooking tap is attached to the cylinder
which contains the water to be filtered ; every time this
household tap is turned on or off the filtering medium or
candle is automatically scraped, brushed, wiped, and
sluiced, and is thus constantly kept absolutely free from
germs and debris, and free to yield its maximum output
of pure water. The filtered water can either be stored in
the kitchen, or conveyed by pipes direct from the filter to
any other part of the building to which the pressure from
the main will reach. If the new filter will do what is
claimed for it, as would appear to be the case, it should
be a very big thing.

More Land Grant Shindykitism.
Certain individuals, among whom that pro-Jap
enthusiast, J. Langdon Parsons, is conspicuous,
are renewing r the agitation for the completion of
the S. A. Transcontinental Railway by a syndicate
on the land-grant principle. It is difficult now to
pick up a S.A. newspaper without finding in it
the wail of Parsons or some of his followers on
behalf of that land-grant syndicate railway. The
return of this aged delusion to public life is an
evidence that popular superstitions take longer in
dying than even the oldest inhabitant or the
Waterloo veteran. The advocates of the land-
grant.railway swindle put it forward confidently
as a means by which railways can be constructed,
worked, and made to pay in the hands of a syndi-
cate when in the hands of the State they would be
impossible. And even the opponents of the idea
very often oppose it simply on grounds of
public policy, and apparently fail to see that,
even leaving public policy out of the question, the
whole proposition is a transparent fraud. A
private syndicate, with & grant of 1000 or 2000 or
more acres for every mile of line might, with the
rents of its land added to its other earnings, make
the transcontinental line pay when the S.A.
Railway Department without any such grant,
couldn’t do so. But it is certain that if a
private syndicate could make the line pay
with such a grant the S.A. Railway Department,
with a similar graut, would make it pay a great
deal better. The syndicate invariably builds most
of its line with debenture money raised at 5 or 6
or even more per cent., and it wants 5 or more per
cent, on any money of its own that it puts into
the business, and it usually has a Loudon office to
maintain, also a local office, and a large board of
directors, and other expenses. The State can find
the money ac 3;f per cent., with none of the other
charges, and in point of cheapness the State
in nagement comes out on top every time. If
there is any inducement in the shape of a land-
grant which will enable a private co. to pro-
fitably build the S.A. transcontinental rail-
way, then the State Railway Department can
build it with the same grant; and make it pay by
including the rents received from the land among
the takings, and pay the debt off the railway m
time by the sale of the land, and do all this a great
deal better than the syndicate can. The syndicate
business can only be the more economical of the
two if it is deliberately proposed to lure the
syndicate on to a speculation in which it will lose
its money. A land-grant syndicate can only
supply a transcontinental railway more cheaply
than a land grant Railway Department can by
losing the capital it puts in, and it can only
be assumed that it will do it more cheaply
by assuming that it is going to lose its
capital. If that is the idea the less Aus-
tralia has to do with it the better. I his
country doesn't want to deliberately rum any
company and send the poor foolish widow who is
always being beguiled into'such things to the
workhouse. It may curse a good deal, and lean up
against a post more than is advisable, but still
there are limits. Deliberate public fraud is on the
other side of these limits.

A bit of description ffom the far West : —
About a mile outside Perth (W. A.) is the Government
>oorhouse. It is not a “ house,” but a group of ram-
hackle one-storey wooden buildings, placed any and
verv how, as if they came down in a shower. At one end
•f f he dining-room the primitive sanitary arrangements
re placed, and at the other is the dead-house or morgue,
,, h ere the bodies of hard old-timers recline, generally two
r three times a week. The “ buildings” cover a out two
cres of groun T, and f00 men are “ in residence -sav 150
i trd cases to the acre. It is the only thickly-populated
pot in the Great Emptiness of Westrallft.
John Norton, in the N.S.W. censure
debate, interjected something about Sulla being a
“ bloody monster,” and Acting-Speaker Cann
promptly called wild Northumberland to order,
stigmatising his phrase as “ indecent ” and
“ dirty.” Thereupon such eminent literary autho-
rities as Minister Perry and Minister O’Sullivan
interfered to point out that the Nortouian remark
was a “ classical quotation ” and quite harmless.
But Cann, who is a strong man and a fair man
(securing, in spite of his lack of Mr. Perry’s exten-
sive knowledge of the classics and of philology,
far more respect for the Chair than any other of
its present temporary occupants) stuck to his guns
and got a withdrawal. He was quite right. It
can reasonably be required, of Mr. Norton at least,
that he should in Parliament say “bloodthirsty,”
or “blood-stained,” or “sanguinary,” to avoid
misconceptions.
Apropos of that double-meaning word “bloody,”
a M.L. town was once inexpressibly shocked when
(strolling journalist Dick Lee being in temporary
charge of the local paper) an article descriptive of
a hanging concluded —
The hangman took his bloody money and departed.
Australia, following the example of the English
Hooligan, has made so free use of the adjective
as a swear-word that its use, in a legitimate
sense always gives a shock, and it might as
well be banished from decent language—especially
as there are several workable synonyms.

Under the S. Australian law the Parlia-
mentary candidate has to swear to his election
expenses. Those returns lately filed show, as
others have done before, that it isn’t the big
country district, but the compact city electorate,
that mops up the money. It cost the money-big
crowd £140 all round to win or lose the metropoli-
tan seats in the Council; the very heavily-gilded
Brookman spent a few pounds extra “on his
own.” In the Southern district, stretching from
the Victorian border to the suburbs of Adelaide,
Attorney-General Gordon spent only £10 3s.;
while even the beastly-wealthy J. L. Stirling
only managed to spend £52. It cost
Mayor Cohen and his colleague (Dixson)
£383 apiece to win their city seats, and
Premier Jenkins’ suburban win got away with
£292; while, on the other hand, Old-Man Bur-
Soyne won the vast Flinders district on £9 5s. Id.
>f course, Burgoyne cut it very fine; still, the
country average was well under £40 —Treasurer
Butler won his Barossa seat for £35. Even in
the city and suburbs, the Laborites had to
look hard at the ha’pence; so while Jenkins was
spending £292, and Opposition leader Darling
(verra Scotch, as he is) £178, Labor-leader Price,
in the same district, had to be satisfied with an
outlay of £16.
Apropos of the Federal Government’s re
markable conduct in testing immigrants as to
their knowledge of English only, whereby the
Hindu nigger generally gets in while the
white German, Italian, Spaniard or Portu-
guese is generally excluded. The Immigra-
tion Restriction Bill as originally drafted
made English the sole test language. Par-
liament, however, struck' out that proviso
and made the test a knowledge of “any
European language.” That was a suffi-
ciently plain intimation as to how the
statute was to be interpreted, and the
Barton Government is fooling with that
same statute in a very high-handed fashion
indeed.
A. S. Beavan, who died, in Sydney,
June 12, after toiling patiently through
many years of ill-health, long edited the
Town'and Country Journal. He was
appointed editor of the Echo (defunct)
and Sydney Mail, when F. W. Ward went
over from the Fairfaxes to Sydney D.T.
There was no doubt about Mr. Beavan’s fitness,
but he could not fit in with his surroundings, and,
after a brief and unsatisfactory time, resigned.
None of his after ventures were successful or
happy. He had a rough time in W.A,, and,
returning to Sydney, did not find many doors
open. One of the worthy, affable, hard-working
old school of pressmen, who asked but little of the
world and only got a small percentage of that.
Frederick F. Smith, to The Bulletin : —
Dear Bulletin.— Kindly give me space in your columns
to dr,i w public attention to the fact that Evangelist Geil
has failed to accept my challenge offering to'give £25 to
the Melbourne Children’s Hospital if he can prove his
statement “That during the American Civil War an
American officer, an infidel whose name he would not soil
his mouth with, on being confronted with the enemy,
ran away from his men and hid himself in a pigst.ve,” Mr.
Geil to give £25 to the same institution if he fails to sub-
stantiate his assertion. Although no name was given all
those present were aware that the reference was to the
late Col. Ingersoll, and as Evangelist Geil has not denied
the fact, he stands convicted of an infamous slander on
the memory of a dead man. In the absence of any reply
to the challenges of Mr. Ramsey and myself in Melb.
Herald, I can only come to the conclusion that Mr. Geil
is so much taken up with preparations for the safety of
his soul in the next world as to have neither time nor
inclination to speak the truth in this one.

“ The day after Mr. Irvine became Premier, Mr
Peacock broke two ribs.”—News Item.
Alick (hilariously) : “ If I think of Irvine's job
much longer Til break the rest of my ribs.”
The faith-healing sect received a rather
curious set-back a few days ago at Bendigo (Vic.)
A coroner’s jury found a verdict of manslaughter
against a local farmer, who treated his ailing girl-
baby with prayer, instead of procuring medical
aid, the natural result being that she died. At
the trial which followed, however, prisoner’s
lawyer stated that the father had no idea at all
that the girl-baby was seriously ill. The trouble
was supposed to be some trifling matter of no
account. If accused had had the slightest idea
that the illness was serious he would immediately
have given up his reliance on the Lord and sent
for the doctor. So the jury acquitted him, as an
individual whose faith was limited, and who only
trusted in the Lord to cure the patient when the
patient was bound to get well in any case.
Nervous sufferers will find it worth their
while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advt. on page
33 of this issue.*

He milk. Savory item from British
Medical Journal :—
The slightest acquaintance with the methods of the
farmer, the dairyman and the milkman shows that pure
milk is an unknown substance on our tables. Secreted,
perchance, in a healthy mammary gland, the fluid is
drawn through unwashed rustic hands into a pail placed
under the dung-spattered udder and belly of a cow who
spends at least half her time in a dark and noisome yard,
carpeted with a slush of decomposing urine and foeces,
and papered with the splashings of the same, while the
air is thick with the bacterial flora of the bovine alimen-
tary canal. What wonder that the bottom of a milk-can
nearly always exhibits a rich sludge, and thus serves the
purpose of cesspool or septic tank ? It has been cal-
culated that the inhabitants of Berlin consume in their
milk 3cwt. of excrement per day.

“ A.O.H.” supplies a personal confession :
Recent Bulletin story, “The Saving of John Sandi-
lands,” reminds me of an incident. The best boy had so
far failed to make the necessary declaration, and it was
this time or never. We were spending his last holiday
together, and the morrow would see him Westward ho.
We put in the afternoon on the hills, had tea at the local
hotel, and then went upstairs to the drawing-room. The
Fates seemed kind, and a spell was in the air. We were
alone. Glances grew more and more tender; the very
words trembled on his lips, while visions of a five-stone
engagement ring that would make all the other cats die
of envy floated before me. All of a sudden weird noises
came from the balcony—shrill shrieks and deep gurgles.
The boy rushed out, and there, stuck in one of the half-
open balcony doors, was an 18-stone lady boarder of the
establishment. She had wanted to get inside, had tried
to squeeze through, and had proved too large. The spell
was broken, and just then we had to leave for our train.
It was “ never.” The fat lady died a week later. If she
had only died a week earlier what a difference it would
have made 1

A correspondent who knows :—
Your notions on good, hard, paying work in gaols like
me well, but is it not time somebody spoke out about the
farce or the horror of “ Solitary ? ” It is farcical if regarded
as a means of reforming or cowing any human being. Its
horror appears when its effects are followed out to their
last and worst results. We are going back in this way to
the fantasies and obscenities of the fourth and fifth cen-
turies. Men and women went out in thousands then to
holes in the rocks of the Nubian deserts. They believed
the Devil couldn’t get out there. But he did; and vexed
Origen and Anthony. We try to keep our undesirable
people away from the Devil by making their caves even
more secure than those of Nubia. We force them back
on their own reflections by means which they cannot
avoid. And what comes of it? A good many bodies are
left in a case scarce better than Origen’s, and in the fre-
quent St. Anthony phantom-fights humanity does not
often score. The real hard “ old hand ” gets as well used
to it as did the anchorite. “A man can put in 10 hours
anyhow,” he says, “ and for the rest can’t he sleep or
plot robbery ? ” The whole business wants a thorough
reform. Common sense and daylight need to be let into
it; and now seems the time for something of the sort to
be done. There is a spirit of humanity at work in the
way of gaol reform, and it was wanted, for old Darling-
hurst was as brutal as old Newgate, and foul as old
Sodom. But the willing spirit must get out of the
shackles of “system” and superstition, and put on a
working body, guided and controlled by plain common
sense, humanity and justice
A few days ago, a man, found guilty of
receiving a watch stolen from the person, got six
months’ hard, from Judge Real. Brisbane
Courier (June 3) report of trial contains some
lovely patches:
The prisoner had served with the fourth contingent in
S’Africa, and a sub-inspector of police testified that he
came of respectable farmer-folk, near Toowoomba, but
that, since his return, he consorted with thieves.
Judge Real remarked :
The patriotism of going to fight seems to have had a
very bad effect on men. The other man, sentenced for
receiving last week, was a contingenter, too.
A little further on, the Judge added :
If joining the army has had such a demoralising effect
on you that you intend to join, not only the army of de-
fenders, but the army of thieves, it will be my duty to
deprive you, etc., etc.

The Institute s
Medical Publications.
QfINGE the inception of the FREEMAN and
O WALLACE ELECTRO-MEDICAL and SUR-
GICAL INSTITUTE, Elizabeth and Bathurst Sts.,
SYDNEY, in the year 1890, medical publications, num-
bering over 50 large editions, and comprising a total ot
500,000 medical volumes, have been aUhe ottiwvs
of their publishers, and distributed to the IN SI lIU lit o
patients and public generally over the various territories
of Australasia. The first of these works was published
under the title of “RESCUED AT LAST,” which,
after running into several large editions, was replaced by
“CLINICAL EXPERIENCES,” in the year 1898.
Each year has seen a revised and enlarged version of this
famous work, the public demand for it being of so con-
tinuous a character that constant publication was
resorted to to ensure a direct supply. This is but one ot
the many publishing items in the general cost of an
INSTITUTE which reaches a grand total of £lOO,OOO in
assuring its usefulness and scientific completeness.
The present volume of “CLINICAL EXPERI-
ENCES ” is as elegant in appearance as it is useful in
contents, and is especially valuable to anyone residing
out of touch with their family doctor. Where medical
aid is unobtainable, that is where this valuable work
finds a place. No home in New South Wales, no home-
stead in Australia, should be without a copy. The fact
that it is given post-free to the public for OJNlii
SHILLING IN STAMPS assures its entry into every
home. It is a whole Library of Facts, and worth a
POUND A- PAGE to persons living in the far por-
tions of the Australian Empire, where a knowledge of
how to act in cases of sickness is indispensable.
Everyone should have a knowledge of sickness, and to
some ex' ent be their own doctor. Then, in cases of ill-
ness, they would know how to act. I heir proper tutor
is a GOOD MEDICAL WORK, sound in its advices,
and reliable in its information. Its presence in a home,
when the loved one is stricken down suddenly, means
perhaps the saving of that loved one’s life. It
sible to guage the value of “CLINICAL EXPERI-
ENCES,” for it is a sterling authority upon all diseases,
and its advices upon hygiene make it indispensable to
everyone interested in a true standard of bodily health.

IflOR the instruction of the readers of these lines, it
may be said “CLINICAL EXPERIENCES,
which was published in January, 1902, in a 20,000 edition,
comprises 550 pages of medical liferature, 150 full and
half-page medical illustrations, 24 full-page lithographs
of the interior and exterior of the Institute, 31 chapters
upon general diseases, full-page portraits of the
Specialists of the Institute, an Appendix, the literary
contents of which comprise 22 readable articles upon
various matters, a valuable list of 50 prescriptions for
household use, a collection of press notices from the most
notable journals of Australia, and a Medical Vocabulary
of 1000 words. This Appendix is also charmingly illus-
trated. Each chapter and article is represented in the
Index, so that readers can find any subject at a moment s
notice.
The first chapter, on ANATOMY, contains 44 de-
scriptive pages, profusely illustrated with 36 life-size
and lesser illustrations, instructive to anyone desirous of
knowing the complex character of their being; The
remaining chapters deal with diseases of the HEAR!,

Greymouth Lagoons dredge (M.L.) likely to
stiict about thp end of the month. 00. owns 88 acres on
l iiiick water Creek, and 232 acres on Snowy Creek, tribu-
lanes of the Grey River. Reported that a large number
of bores were put down on the various claims, and the
poorest return was at the rate of sgr. gold per cubic yard,
and the best 19gr.; the average for the 320 acres being
111 1 g'r. To be hoped that this Snowy Creek proposition
will have a better fate than the Snowy River (N.S.W.)
The latter on boring-tests was represented to be worth
several dwts. per yard, but dredging results showed about
as many dwts. to the acre.
