At the end of its first year there were 17,300
miners on the N.S.W. (State) Miners’ Accident Fund ;
and during thes year the sufferers from 2558 accidents
were relieved, on the average, for about 3} weeks. Forty-
seven men, killed by other accidents, were decently
buried. The two facts show pretty well what sort of a
■ ailing the miner’s is ; yet this was a year, it is explained,
freer from acfcident than usual. Of the dead men 20
were married their widows, and where they had none,
their dependents, are now pensioned. Also their 80
■hildren are to be maintained, on the average, about six
.' ears. Altogether, £6478 was paid out; and, roughly,
£25;000 has been invested to keep the pensioners going.
It seems that the miner’s fortnightly 9d., subsidised
equally by the owners, and helped by the State, is ample ;
anyhow, there is a provision for varying the scale on an
actuarial examination. One unsatisfactory feature, and
almost the only one, is that the total number of members
is no more than 55 per cent, of those stated by latest
returns to be working N.S.W. mines.
* • • •
Victorian sharebrokers protest with much show
of righteous indignation against the system which pre-
vails of private information being sent from mines to
market operators in anticipation of official reports. The
protest must be made on the principle “ assume a virtue,
if you have it not.” There is probably not a broker in
Australia, worth his salt, who has not confidential advisers
working in the mines which are principally dealt in on
’Change.
Re low-grade iron ore and crippling freight
charges to coal. A two-million co. has been formed in
England, underwritten by ironmasters, to work Edison
patents in Norway. England imports over five million
tons of ore per year, but the handiest Norwegian ores are
not touched because they are worth only about 40 per
cent, and are heavy in phosphorus. Edison’s patents, the
result of experiments alleged to have cost over half-a-
million, cover a series of processes for grinding the ore,
magnetically concentrating it to a high grade (65 per
cent.), extracting most of the phosphorus, and briquetting
the iron dust. Cost of quarrying and everything else to
shipboard is put at Bs. per ton of ore. The English
Edison syndicate (which, by the way, includes Mt.
Morgan D’Arcy) takes 141,000 paid shares for the patent
righfe for Norway and Sweden.
Mr. Justice M. H. Stephen (a son of the
lata N.S.W.' Chief Justice Alfred Stephen) be-
comes Acting Chief Justice during Judge Dar-
ley’s absence on leave; at the end of that term
he will retire on pension. Meanwhile Mr. It. D.
Pring gets a permanent Justiceship at the reason-
able and useful age of 48. Mr. Pring, like Mr.
Stephen, is a most excellent lawyer, of the dry-
as-dust order.
“The Breaker’s” verses (including a
photographic reproduction of the last, written by
him in Pietersburg gaol) are included in “ Bush-
man and Buccaneer ” with a memoir of the life of
Harry Morant. Every bushman should have a
copy of these racy and characteristically Australian
verses. Wholesale of Edwards Dunlop and Cos.,
Sydney. Retail of all booksellers; post-free from
Bulletin office, Is. Id.*
From Adelaide :
J. Langdon Parsons, M.L. C., ex-Goverument Resident of
the Northern Territory and S.A. Vice-Consul for Japan, is
booming a land-grant railway scheme for connecting
Oodnad.itta with the Territory. Parsons is backed by all
the chief merchants of Adelaide, who fear that if the
Territory is handed over to the Commonwealth a trans-
continental line would not end at Adelaide but be
diverted from Daly Waters to the east, connecting with
the N.S. W. and Q. railway systems. The Jap. Consul is also
backed by Tories and syndicate-mongers, on general
princip es, and with the sneaking hope that the cheap-
nigger labor idea will get some kind of an indirect lift.
(Parsons himself once favored Jap. labor for the Terri-
tory, and probably still does.) The * ‘ Land Grant Trans-
continental Railway League,” of which most of the
S.A. Tory politicians are members, was formed the
other day, and hasenthusiasticsponsors in the consistently
Tory Register and-the Advertiser— once Radical, but
now, alas, an organ of the Stomach Party. ‘ ‘ The advant-
age of the land-grant system," says Bonython’s paper,
“is the creation of a direct incentive to occupation and
development. . . . Provided fair terms can be arranged,
the chance is one which S.A. should not miss.” Some
years ago Kingston jumped heavily on one Watt, who
came aljng with similar proposals, and the Advertiser,
then Kingston’s obedient servitor, made great fun of the
incident; but times have changed since they threw
S’Langdon Bonython his bauble.
A London paper’s Capetown cur.espondjnt
says things:—
For the past two or three days the people of Capetown
have had the opportunity of seeing what manner of men
the “ Empire saviours ’’ are who left Australasia for South
Africa, at ss. a day. Notwithstanding previous experi-
ences, which should have taught the military authori-
ties wisdom, some 1700 returning troopers were allowed
to spend two or three days in Capetown while waiting for
transports, and during that period they have done just a 3
they pleased. Their pleasure was to show that they were
in ..o way. amenable to discipline, and to gratify their de-
sires, irrespective of public order and decency, and
legarJtess of the rights of property. They paraded the
streets in gangs, frequently in a semi-drunken condition,
interfered with inoffensive passers-by, insulted officers
when the latter were accompanied by ladies). They
s.flashed several bars, and damaged two or three hotels,
Visited the Opera House, and wrecked the box-office,
and attempted to do the same at the circus, where the
performance had to be stopped. Some of these things
were done after dark, but others in the daytime, and the
authorities seemed helpless. In one of the principal
streets, a numerous gang of these “ gentlemen in
kharki ” stretched a rope across the thoroughfare,
an 1 indulged in a jumping competition, and
stopped the tratncar traffic until such time as
tlieir “ sport ” was concluded. Gab-drivers were dragged
from their seats, and their vehicles taken possession of,
the latter being driven “ all round the town,” singing,
shouting, and swearing. Passing carts and waggons, con-
taining fruit and mineral waters—anything eatable or
drinkable -were stopped, and their contents seized,
aid no one was safe from attack. Some of
tie hotelkeepers applied to the military authorities
fur protection, ana were told that none could be
given them, and a number of the military police were
chased by the Australians, and were dragged from their
horses. An officer who was present when the attack was
made upon the Opera House, ventured to remonstrate.
He was knocked down, as a result, and kicked when
lying upon the ground. On the third night all the bars
were closed at dusk to prevent a general riot
These men (who included a large number of Austra-
lian Bushmen, some ,pt the Victorian Contingent, and
also some New Zealanders) were supposed to have
completed 12 months’ service, but they displayed few
signs of it Their uniforms were but little stained or
torn, and they-xhemselves appeared in the pink of con-
dition. Sir. Brodrick recently declared that Lord
Kitchener would not keep useless men in South Africa.
One wonders what use these men have been? Certainly,
if they ivere not more amenable to discipline up country
than in Capetown, the.r room would be preferable to their
company. Soldiers of all sorts—regulars, militia and volun-
teers—have passed through Capetown, and we have seen
men from Canada, Malta, and Ceylon, and have had no
cause to complain of their behaviour ; it has been left for
these particular Australian troopers to show themselves
a rowdy, undisciplined mob. Needless to say, the local
papers have not said, a word. “ Keep it dark” is the
motto. The censorship is very strict. If a question is
asked in the House of Commons, Mr. Brodrick will, of
reply that “he has no information on the sub-
jeofc.*
At Poverty Point.
..ied Plumpton’s memory suffers some very disrespect-
ful treatment in the Orchestral Association Notes, pub-
lished In the May number of London Orchestral Times.
ine editors of the Times don’t hold themselves responsible
tor opinions expressed in these notes, but they yet there
“r the same. -‘Notes" says: “ Another death, which
closely concerned the orchestral profession, was that of
Alfred Plunipton, musical director at the Pa ace
theatre for some years. With orchestral players he was
extremely unpopular, and his unfortunate manner did
not improve as years went by, whilst his method of dis
missing’ his players at the slightest offence incensed a
great number of professional musicians who had the mis-
fortune at various times to play with him. Many dis-
agreeable incidents might be mentioned, but we desire to
iorget them under the circumstances, but would like
other conductors to read a lesson in our remarks. It is
sincerely to be desired that his successor may prove a
more considerate and honorable chief. . . . Why a musi-
cal director should develop into a tyrant we are at a loss
to understand, unless it is the want of a proper edu-
cation. This snort from the bandsmen is mildly cor-
‘"“other part of the paper by a paragraph stating
that The late Mr. Plunipton, for some years conductor
attiio Palace Theatre, was a musician of many accom-
plishments. In Australia he achieved a reputation as a
musical critic.”
“ S.S.” : Went to Amy Castles’ farewell, at Melb. Exhi-
bition, paid my Is., and battled in with the crowd. Never
bought trouble so cheaply in my life before. Seemed as
1 w * 1 6 *"‘ w * s fighting, and tearing, and cursing in its
effort to get in. The management had made excellent
arrangements for about half as many as turned up, the
other half just fought in the outskirts of the big building,
and struggled, and gouged, and swore. I saw five faint-
mg women carried out of the pack during the evening,
and hardly one woman who was not more or less damaged
and utterly disgusted. The most pathetic object of all
was the matron who was prowling round, wofully dang-
ling the mangled fragments of a hat, asking for the
manager, with a view to claiming compensation. Met
her when 1 first entered, and, after the concert, saw her
asking a policeman, and tearfully displaying the frag-
ments. * I’m sure Miss Castles would give me damages
for my hat if she knew I’d come all the way from Geelong
to hear her,” wailtd the victim.
Maggie Moore and her lesser half are returning to
Australia directly, under arrangement with Anderson.
They appear at Melb. Royal in October.
Charles Garry was to leave the Brough Co. at the con-
clusion of its Adelaide season last Saturday and get
back to England, Brough having kindly released him
from the short remaining term of his contract. Garry
did some good work as ihe bearish husband in “The
Liars,” the giddy old colonel in “ Tyranny of Tears,” the
convict in “ VUlage Priest,” and the kindly “toff” who
knew Mrs. Dane the Defenceless in her disrespectable
days. But he is best remembered for his little sketch*
of the Royal duke in “ The Second in Command,
which was a masterpiece of lordly condescending mannah
and stiffened graciousness. The ducal Garry hopes to be
back on the Australian stage anon, he says, and one feels
that in merely suggesting the probability of his return
Garry puts this country under a deep, hollow obligation,
such as it feels when King Edward acknowledges the
receipt of an official grovel by cablegram.
Mrs. G. B. W. Lewis is trying to fix up a performance
of “Ingomar,” to be given at one of the Melbourne
theatres, wLh Miss Uni Russell as Parthenia, a character
exactly suited to that graceful damosel.
Geil, of the “ simultaneous mission,” started his S.A.
campaign in a wool-store at Port Adelaide. And a rain-
storm--the first for months—promptly came and banged
so persistently on the iron roof that he could not be heard
beyond the first half-dozen rows of seats. The meeting
sang hymns desperately to fill up time, but the storm
abated not, and the whole occasion was ruined. What a
stern moral this incident would have been made to point
if it had happened to a Wicked Man—say Ingersoll !
“F.M.”: “R.A.K.” wants to marry De Murska some
more though (like the Samaritan woman), she is already
credited with seven husbands. Her only Melbourne
marriages were to Anderson, R.A.M., who died inside
twelve months, and conductor Hill, who bolted from her
to ’Frisco. Plumpton was never matrimonially in that
swim.
Arthur Crane, the young baritone lately at Rickards’s,
has been enrolled in J. 0. Williamson’s comic opera
forces. Harold Thorley, late of S’Africa, comes along to
fill the place of Brownlow, who is said to be either
returning to hotel management in Westralia, or returning
to the London Savoy Theatre. Brownlow has a talent
for figuring in contradictory paragraphs.
Adelaide Evening Journal published a crafty left-
handed ad. for “Wrong Mr. Wright” in the form of an
article entitled “ What is the Public Taste ? ” The wicked
hireling scribe began by eulogising Brough for his efforts
to “ refine and elevate the tone of the Australian stage,”
and deplored the fact that the public seem to prefer
broad comedy to delicate art. Then, with tears in his
ink, the apostle of culchaw went on to write :—“ Because
theatre-goers like such effects, and, generally speaking,
demand a wild, uproarious time : ‘ The Wrong Mr.
Wright,’which will be presented, &c., &c., is sure to be
popular.” A smart manager nowadays doesn’t ask the
puff direct for a frivolous production. He inspires a
newspaper lament over its predestined success. Broad-
hurst, the strictly commercial author of “Wright,”
“JoneB,”and “Smith” seems to have inaugurated this
system of advertising. He assumes the apologetic atti-
tude of a man who gives the world what it wants, but
wishes that it wanted something better.
“ H.G.”: Why doesn’t some music-hall artist collaborate
with a smart verse-writer, so as to produce up-to-date
topical verses, varying even nightly, according to the
events ot the day ? Such a course should make the
reputation of the singer and incidentally double his
salary, for there is nothing like current news and local-
isms to fetch the public. The songs of entertainer
Rodney, at the Melb. show of The World’s Entertainers,
are about the dullest ever heard on a stage, and, as for
subjects, they might as well have been written by one of
the Argus leader-writers. Yet the audience applaud
Rodney vociferously, because his stodgy verses deal with
things they understand, and please better than would
sparkling ditties about matters beyond their ken.
“Melb.”: Higginbottom, the theatrical wigmaker,
shows me_a remarkably bird-limey photo.. bearing date
1861. It is the front of Melb. Royal, with a group in-
cluding G. V. Brooke, Avonia Jones, and a number of
other theatrical persons of the time, with passers-by.
and some boys and girls, of whom one speculates as to
what 41 years brought them. The bills in front of the
house show plainly “ Farewell Performances of Mr. G. V.
Brooke and Miss Avonia Jones. Benefit of R. W.
Younge, the Hunchback." A curiosity is the high bell-
toppers worn by most of the personages.
The up-to-date bride. The leading lady of the Selbini
acrobatic troupe, just married in London to another-
member of the profesh, announced at the “breakfast”
her temporary retirement from the arena. So far, after
the old-fashioned model. But this modern woman pro-
ceeded confidently to sign a conti act for her reappearance
in six months. Six months after marriage, in a “star”
acrobatic turn !
A recent attraction at the London Hippodrome was a
»blind pi mist named Mendel—Jewish, no doubt—whose
memory is said to be even more wonderful than his skill
on the bo ■; of music. Blind Mendel is note-perfect in a
thousand pieces, ranging' from sonatas lo song-accom-
paniments, and he can repeat from one hearing a scherzo
or whatnot which has just been played to him for the
first time. The music once photographed on his mind
stays there as long as he cares to retain it.
Veteran “ Jimmy ” Oathcart has old age in the family.
His brother Rowley—“ good old Rowley Oathcart ” —is
just now acting in “A Pair of Spectacles” under John
Hare’s management. A son of Rowley was the Oathcart
who came out to Australia as stage-manager for Wilson
Barrett, the first time that noble Roman set us marvel-
ling at the artfulness of his boots.
Dramatist Sardou (of whom a Bulletin correspondent
spoke as having been converted to spiritualism in his old
age) lately wrote to the editor of Le Matin stating that
his 59 years’ study of “ manifestations ” had carried him
“ from incredulity to surprise and from surprise to con-
viction.” The mueh-talked-of sketches which he (Sardou)
drew whilst under some sort of control date back to 1857,
and the dramatist, who has seen a lot of unspiritual life
since ’57, appears to think that the control was, perhaps,
pulling his leg rather than guiding his hand. But, as
regards his faith in materialisations, Sardou wrote “ I am
still waiting for somebody to explain to me by what
psychic force, or by what trickery, an invisible hand has
been able to lay upon my writing table a bunch of white
roses, which I preserved for years, until they crumbled
into dust! ” What strikes the sceptic as an odd circum-
stance in Sardou’s faith is his evident pride in the dura-
bility of those spirit-roses. If the bouquet had disap-
peared up his nostrils the instant he sniffed its perfume
the miracle would have been more satisfactory, although
even then Sardou could not have sworn that an invisible
hand was concerned in the transaction. Nobody can
swear to the action of a hand he never saw. The spirit
might have come in on its hands carrying the flowers
with its feet.
*
Quitk a number of people in Sydney are taking
great care to disavow all financial connection with
the projected new daily-paper project—the Aus-
tralian Times ; and the hopelessness of the
enterprise becomes clearer every day. Mr.
Barton, it is understood, will endeavor to raise
capital in England for the Australian Times,
but the ohances of success in that direction
are meagre. A rich Jewish or American
capitalist (you don’t meet English capitalists in
England) might one day start a paper in Australia
with himself as sole proprietor, if he saw good
dividends and the prospect of grinding some con-
cession axes ahead ; but London investors are not
likely to sink small sums in a far-away and un-
certain industrial enterprise. It is one of the
dangers threatening Australian public life that
some foreign capitalist will one day seethe enormous
opportunity that the timorousness of Australian
public men offers to a newspaper “boss,” and
come here to play, with skill and daring, the
game that some of the daily papers already play
with the saving merit of stupidity. Given that
the people remain as foolish and the politicians
as corruptible as now, a really brainy, enter-
prising millionaire, founding a ring of news-
papers in Australia, could, in a few years,
almost own the continent, and have nothing
to threaten his reign short of a revolution.
As to the Australian Times enterprise, it
is hinted that if the paper does threaten to come
to print it will be met at the outset by a news-
agents’ boycott, which would force it to organise
distributing arrangements of its own—a matter
of much money. A new daily, either in Sydney
or Melbourne (both cities need one), will only
come when a quarter of a million of money is
available ; or when some honest Australian jour-
nalist with a little money, willing to be burned
slowly at the stake for the convictions that are in
him, begins with a “little rag,” and by sheer
courage of purpose and trusty fidelity to the
national ideal makes it gradually great.
»■
The chief difference between the naval or
military tattoo, where misguided individuals
raise blue marks on fleshy spots by whacking each
other with swords, single - sticks, and other
dangerous weapons, and the Tattoo Oil is that the
marks made by the latter cannot be rubbed off.
Nor can they be worn off. This absolutely safe
wool marking or branding oil has made its mark
in every part of the world where Mary hath a
little lamb.*
The woebegone state of Vic. Public
Service, when necessity occurs for getting men to
take on the big jobs, is evident in the failure to
find one competent to act as principal of the
Training College. The Education Department
has between five and six thousand people of
various grades to choose from, but now that the
new order prevails of giving preference to capa-
bility, the Dept, has to look around among outside
educationalists. A similar difficulty cropped up
a week or two ago, when Stuart Murray, the
hard-headed chief engineer for water-supply, was
deputed to devote his next six months to the
Rivers Commission, and Minister Burton, passing
over the merely routine trainers of his own Dept.,
had to enlist the services of a water expert np in
the Mallee, who is to be retained by Govt, as
“understudy” for the big billet of engineer-in-
chief if he snows aptitude during his probation.


“ W.J.” : Warracknabeal (Vic.), cited by
“ H.P.” (B., 24/5/’02) as being fever-stricken and
so on, is certainly not an exception. The tendency
to stagnate and fatten is apparently infectious, as
“ jungle’’-councils vie, in this respect, with
metropolitan, and the alleged health officials shirk
their duty with ease. Gutters are neglected, or
not even constructed, and shuddersome slops and
garbage are allowed to accumulate and form germ-
nurseries. Sanitary appliances remain of the most
laboriously primitive nature, and a cursory inspec-
tion of .the typical out-back pub. yard would reveal
conditions more than horrifying.
The rebate for white-grown sugar set some
Chinese and other colored farmers thinking in
Bundaberg, (Q). They have decided to empioy
white labor to do all the work, and thus benefit
by the Federal bonus. The irony of it!
The Laundry Bill, just passed by the
Lords, at Westminster, is to protect the poor
washerwoman against the religious charities,
which make such enormous sums by getting the
wash done by pauper Magdalenes. Lord Lytton,
the benevolent fugleman of the Bill, showed that
the subscriptions to some institutions are com-
paratively infinitesimal, outside the washing.

“ Kiwi ” from Voleano Land :
What this State needs above all just now is a healthy
national feeling. At present we are an aimless, blundei-
ing, subservient, conceited people, cast about anj liow on
the blood-red seas of carnal Imperialism. This unhappy
circumstance is largely (although by no means solely)
due to Premier Seddon’s remarkable mana. Pnvatelj,
Seddon’s opinion of the whole business would be ex-
pressed in a chuckle. He looks on the people as so many
fools to be used by political schemers, and at the base of
his big head there is something more than an inkling of
contempt for the crowd whose passions he knows so well
how to humor. Seddon will not be with us always,
hiwever : nor will Maoriland always rush frantically into
. very bloody squabble into which it may please England s
financiers’Government to become involved. Meanwhile,
we have no national feeling-. We don’t seem to be proud
because we are Maorilanders, living within reach of
possibilities which would have been Heaven to our
fathers. We yell ourselves hoarse about England, which
many of us never saw ; and we fawn and cower at the
feet of England. We even fancy that we couldn fc build
roads and bridges without the aid of the bandit chiefs of
London Stock Exchange, who bpss England and con-
descend to be our usurers -- on good security. An
“inspired” message by the Jew-owned cable about
“ uneasiness ” concerning our securities is enough to send
us into fits of apologetic crawlsomeness. Politicians and
newspapers, wide asunder otherwise, agree that we are
dependent on the British money-lender, and one looks in
vain for a protest. But surely it will not be ever so
Freedom must germinate and develop under our genial
skies. Even now the intelligence of the country begins to
whisper a protest.

ALONG BACK IN THE BEGINNING OF THE EIGHTIES
Ilerr Rassmussen introduced his now world-famed Alfaline Herbal Remedies to the Australian public at 547 George
Street, Sydney. Australia was then flooded with American importations and proprietary remedies, manufacture i

by straightforward and fair dealings with the public, offering to stake his reputation and future if his remedies

verdict.

Over twenty years have passed away, hundreds of remedies have come and gone—many of them were forced on
the public in numerous designing ways—thousands of pounds spent on advertising same with the assistance of the
world’s greatest experts in laying traps for the unwary; but the public would have none of them, and they have

Heather Bells.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Heather bells, do you remember
When the skies were softly blue,
In a lost and gone November,
Ere the years had trodden you
Underfoot among the bracken,
Where the wind-voice dies and swells,
Can you tell, have you forgotten ?
O you dainty heather bells !
Heather bells, were you not ringing
On a long-dead afternoon,
By a cob-web rope set swinging
To a quaint and dreamy tune ?
Every year your blooms renewing,
As the spring her tale retells;
Doy ou know me for a lover v
Yours of old, sweet heather bells?
Yours of old, for time is flying,
And my steps but feebly turn,
Where my buried years are lying,
’Neath the bracken and the fern.
But the scent is sometimes wafted
From the hollows and the dells,
Listen ! Hear the bees’ low droning,
And the chime of heather bells.
via.
K. WALKER.
From Blackstone’s Commentaries on the
Laws of England :
In a land of liberty it is extremely dangerous to make
a distinct order of the profession of arms. In absolute
monarchies this is necessary for the safety of the prince,
and arises from the main principle of the constitution,
which is that of governing by fear ; but in free States the
profession of a soldier, taken singly and merely as a pro-
fession, is justly an object of jealousy. In these no man
should take up arms, but with a view to defend his coun-
try and its laws ; he puts off the citizen who enters the
camp ; but it is because he is a citizen, and would con-
tinue such, that he makes himself for a while a soldier
The laws, therefore, and constitution of these kingdoms
know no such state as that of a perpetual standing
soldier, bred up to no other profession than that of war •
and it was not till the reign of Henry VII. that the kings’
of England had so much as a guard about their persons:
Nervous sufferers will find ifc worth their
while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advt. on page
23 of this issue *
“M.J.0.” writes to The Bulletin from
South America (near by the spot where 30,000
Chilian insurgents last May massacred their wives
and daughters en masse “ to show their devotion
to the revolutionary cause”), under date, 10/11/’01:
Here, in Patagonia, and presumably in all Argentina,
anyone may take up a town allotment and own it, subject
to the following conditions: —On squatting on the lot
60dols. must be deposited with the authorities; and if
within 12 months the lot is fenced and a house put on it,
the deposit is returned. Their being no timber on the
pampa, the fence is the more expensive, though it may
be of wire or anything else. As for the house, anything
big enough for a door will do. After the 12 months are
up, it is required that within the next four years a fairly
decent house be erected. According to what I see in this
town, a 10 x 12 iron building, lined, floored, and ceilinged,
with one door and one window, is sufficient. Then you
may apply for your deeds, which you will get at a cost
limited only by the Notary’s power of fleecing you. In
Oallegos there is but one notary, and he refuses to make
out the deeds under lOOdols.—which is S. America all
over. In Paraguay the house must be put on the land at
once, of a value greater in proportion to the nearness to
the Plaza (the public square), and moreover the house
must be continually occupied, either personally or by
proxy, as by letting. This is a point where the Para-
guayans, as a people, score, for it keeps down rent, for
one thing,_ and a certain form of speculation for another.
11 Occupation as the primary right ” holds good through-
out Paraguay. A man settled on saleable Government
land cannot be turned off by any buyer, neither
can he be forced to sell out. Bribery does
much; but such is the law, and such is the
usual working-out of it. And, indeed, I begin to agree
with Larry Petrie, who used to say that the “ Paraguayan
pobledore, half-civilised and ignorant in other ways as he
is, has a grip of the land-question ; ” and that, therefore,
the English will never get the grip of the country they
have of the other Republics.
In Chili the estancia lands are not sold; they are
leased for so many years. At the end of the term they
are put up for re-application, and the highest bidder
wins. Ido not know what is done in case of improve-
ments, should the holding change hands.
In Argentina, if a man is hungry he may go to a
station and demand food. If he has money he may be
compelled to pay for it, but, if he has none, he cannot be
refused. Some time ago, a man, being refused food at an
estancia, simply turned round and shot a stud-sheep
before the owner’s eyes, saying he stood within the law.
Apparently he did, as nothing was done to him.
By law a man cannot will all his property away, and
leave his widow and family destitute—he can only dis-
pose of part, a certain proportion of his substance'being
inalienable from his natural heirs.
In Paraguay natural children inherit with the legiti-
mate, and marriage legitimises children born before wed-
lock. Possibly this removal of the ban of illegitimacy
maj r partly account for the looseness of Paraguaj'an
life. When a Paraguayan marries a girl other than the
mother of his children, he sends the other home to hei
mother with the little ones and maintains them there.
There is no secrecy over the matter. Indeed, the mon
children scattered round, the prouder everyone is of the
fact. “Que guapol” is the admiring exclamation.
Sexual relationship within the forbidden limits is dread
fully common, and the unconcern about the matter may
be gathered from the recent remark of a woman o.
position about her 16-year-old daughter’s baby :—“ It is a
pity it is her brother's.” In fact, one grows sick thinking
of Paraguayan “morality,” and is driven to the con-
clusion that a sense of indecency is the beginning of
virtue.
Which brings me back to Patagonia. Tom Hall,
formerly of Bourke, N.S.W., who may not yet be forgotten
in Australia, said, in leaving here, that “ the vices of
Australia were nothing to those of Patagonia,” and that,
as regards the latter country, “he knew not where to
look for a parallel.” Hall had knocked about long enough
in both countries to know what he was talking about. In
short, in South America one-half the population lives on
the vices of the other half, and the child smitten for the
sins of the father is as common a sight as is the dreadful
woman.
A Queensland town council lately bought
a gun for the inspector of nuisances, so that he
might wipe out any vagrant dogs in the district.
The armed inspector opened proceedings by put-
ting a charge of shot into one of the aldermen.
From an Adelaide paper—a curiosity in
obituary verse:
His prayer is answered—and he sleeps his last
Beside the little mother that he blessed ;
The cripple’s crutches he aside has cast,
To take his compensation—dreamless rest,
Life-tired, dream-tired, and longing-tired, midst pains,
He sighed—“ God take me I”—God took brave George
More trouble—a letter from Northern
N.S.W. this time :
A Tweed (N.S.W.) farmer recently got the centre of his
canefield stripped by Hindu labor, the outer rows of
cane being left untouched. With some display he boasted
to neighbours that, in order to get the benefit of the
Federal rebate, he intended doing the trashing him-
self, and, setting to, finished what the Hindus
had designedly left. While these Indians can be
employed without penalty at every farming indus-
try, excepting cane, there will probably be a good
deal of the same trickery carried on in the future. If a
man is officially caught working blacks amongst his cane
he can simply reply that he does not intend to claim the
rebate. If he succeeds in escaping the eyes of the inspector
he can then fill in the formal declaration that none but
white labor had been employed. Perhaps it will be
possible, too, to grease the palm of the superintending
official. There will then be money in it for both parties.

THE AEOLIAN
has become so important a factor
of pleasure in so many homes, and
especially in the homes of those
whose names are synonymous with
culture and refinement, that it is an
object of interest to everyone who
gives any thought to his own or his
family’s pleasure.
Because one man likes a certain article is no proof that another will.
When, however, enthusiasm for the same article steadily increases
from year to year, there is conclusive proof that it must have unusual
value. The man who does not give himself a fair opportunity to
learn what this value is, cuts himself off from the means of judging the
benefit he might derive from it.

St. Hilda Hill.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
On the crest of the hill in Barkly-streefc, St. Kilda,
where you look out over the Red Bluff, there was
Yerrikee’s Ground —70 years ago.
Yerrikee and his band —mostly youngsters had
come out of Carrum Carrum. Too many old men
in Carrum—those doing all the ordering and the
others all the graft, and promotion too long
coming.
Besides there was a holier bond. These young
men wanted wives and good young plump wives—
not the superannuated leavings of Nerridoc and all
the bald and grey of Carrum —and the thing had
all been talked over till thread-bare, in what stood
to them for their club.
Away ofE north on the other side of the hills of
the Everflowing ran a tribe that Yerrikee and his
pals wotted of, and great oorroborees were not
held for nothing in the old days.
So one fine morning young Yerrikee —chief by
might of these dandies of an old time —gathered
his cronies together, and they strolled off from the
conservative camp of the old’uns, and started a
tribe on their own.
Not empty-handed either. There was Yerri-
kee’s father’s spear, for instance —real iron that
had been ripped off the bottom of the boat of some
murdered whalers generations ago, when England
was ruled by a George. Also a cloak of bearskin,
good for the coldest night.
Another’s father possessed a club with a
chain attachment, at the end of which was a ball
of lead spiked with huge iron nails after the style
of the “ Holy-Water-Sprinkler ” in the Town of
London —used in the wars of the Roses. These,
and things like these, ’twould have been a sin to
leave, so, taking them along, they got away from
Carrum country, and eventually assembled on
what is now St. Kilda hill.
Then there was a yabber. Out of shot of the
old ’uns, they showed how they could talk.
Yerrikee was boss of it; he stood up on where
the trunk bulged out at the bottom of a huge gum,
and, reaching up, with a hatchet chopped
some marks into the tree —deep marks—that
would not come out in a hundred years, and,
pointing to them, made a speech, of which the
following is a free translation :
“Gentlemen, —I’ve not brought you here for
nothing. (‘ Hear, hear.’) You know what I want,
and I know what you want, so we won’t waste
any words about that. (Cheers.)
“ I have chopped here, as you see, the name of
my adorable and never-to-be-too-much-longed-for
Kuya Kuya. She is now a maiden —dwelling, I
trust, in the camp of her father (pointing N.E.)
but to-morrow night—to-morrow night, gentle-
men—she shall be (D.Y.) standing at the foot of
this tree—my wife.”
Enthusiastic approbation, and waving in the
air of anything handy, followed this.
The holy-water-sprinkler, in unpractised hands,
accidentally crashed into the head of a too-adja-
cent black. 'lt all happened in a moment, and his
gory remains were for the time hastily clapped
behind a bush, the brother of deceased making a
note of the name of the bearer of the holy-water-
spriiikler for future reference.
The leavings of the defunct being duly divided
up (Yerrikee himself got a pair of boomerangs—
models in their way), the chief resumed: “As I
was about to observe, my friends, at the time of
this most regrettable occurrence, it’s not a ques-
tion of myself alone; there in the camp of the
Meris are others than the chief’s daughter with
soft bosoms and many rows of fat.
“ Are these to be marketed as old men’s slaves ?
Not much ! We’ve laid our plans, and to-morrow
we’ll all meet at this tree again, each with his
lubra, and this shall be Yerrikee’s tribe.”
Wild antics of applause greeted this finish. The
man with the holy-water-sprinkler gave himself
more room this time and no one was much hurt.
Then there was more talk and pointing, some-
times over the sea—that referred to the white man;
he was to give trouble later on—sometimes
towards Carrum, that was farewell to home and
mother —then over to Studley Park way, and that
boded the Meri-ites no good.
Then they took the remains of the accidentally
killed and fixed him up on some poles in the ti-tree
scrub down on the beach.
The brother of the dead man first cut off one of
the hands from the body in fulfilment of a tribal
custom. He would smoke it dry in some native
way until it would be hard and shiny and black
like ebony.
Then it would be a charm to wear round his
neck, and warn him of the approach of an enemy.
The funeral over, they all sallied forth, and by
nightfall were scrambling up and down the steeps,
not far from where you will now find a convent.
With cat-like tread and many a whispered
“Yah, yah” and “jali, jah,” they crept cautiously
through the dark towards where the smoke of
smouldering fires rested in the air. Then they
set a watch, and turned in for an hour or so, to be
fresh on the job just about when the other fellows
were well into their first solid slumber, after the
native bear was eaten.
About an hour before midnight, Yerrikee,
awake and listening, heard a mopoke’s cry thrice
repeated.
He had said before, “We have laid our plans.”
He now said ‘ ‘ Advance ! ” and they advanced.
What could be was done quietly—a creeping
round a miamia—a chop with a hatchet —smash
with a club—dig with a spear—a grunt—a groan—
a gasp—a choking and throat-cutting of the men
and a gagging of the women—by the time the
alarm was fairly raised the thing was done.
After the ball was over, who but Kuya-Kuya
nimbly jumped from an adjacent tree to the side
of Yerrikee, and, looking up blushingly, said —not
in English—“O, I’m so glad you’ve come, dear.
Do you know, I was so afraid they would notice
that funny mopoke business—how did I do it?”
A lot of the women were in the thing before-
hand. It was a put-up job, and this was the
beginning of Yerrikee’s tribe (King Billy of the
whites.)
The raided Meri-ites got up a great revenge ex-
pedition, with friends to help.
Then the old Carrumites chipped in because—
first —there was to be a fight. Second—the new
tribesmen were, afterall, their sons who had reflected
credit on their sires, and they were not going to
bide quiet to see them chopped up by the Yarra
The northern tribes were a big lot and had the
advantage of being counselled by the great white
chief Buckalee, and they came down in hate and
fury, and there was a great to-do down about
where Hawksburn is at this time of day. _
Then Yerrikee, tomahawking, clubbing and
shouting, showed ■"• bat he was made of.
The holy-water-sprinkler sprinkled afresh,
and left lanes of devastation behind. This weapon
had changed hands, it was noticed—the brother or
the man'who had been accidentally killed with it
on St. Kilda beach was now the artist.
In the end the Northerners were half of them
slaughtered, and the astute Buckalee and Ins
friends all but failed to manage a retreat with the
remainder. The reputation of Yerrikee was
made from that moment, and he resumed his in-
dependence on the hill with more promise than
ever. Also his tribe was largely augmented by
Carrumites who flocked to join a rising cause.
When the men went to hunt in the morning
Kuya Kuya would climb Yerrikee’s tree and look
after them, and signal in curious tribal ways miles
off. She did her art-work up this tree. A con-
venient fork in it formed her studio. She’d work
such things as necklaces for Yerrikee’s neck ;
brow-bands adorned with kangaroos’ teeth ; boo-
merang sashes for his waist; or fashion a bone to
go through his nose. The great tree bulged out
all round at the base, forming a natural seat.
From that seat could be seen one day a sloop
coming up the bay, with white people spying out
the land, to have and to own it, and subsequently
Fawkner’s and Batman’s settlement on the Yarra
was formed.
One day Yerrikee and a chosen band were out
as usual on their “ give-us-this-day-our-daily-
bread ” hunt. .
A white man had been murdered ; indignation
was in the air at the settlement; revenge parties
were out, and almost the first thing that happened
was that the young chief was shot through the
heart with a white man’s gun; and that was the
end of Yerrikee.
Kuya Kuya, in her tree, worked a shell-necklace,
and grew anxious watching the hours pass, and
looking Macedon way, Yarra way, Carrum way—
all ways. .
After a while, the six that returned brought
news—news that she was a dead chief’s widow.
The camp was to remove at once. Yerrikee —
the Great Yerrikee —called King Billy by the
whites, was dead, and they were all going to be
united to their fathers again for mutual protection
from the white men. When they started off
they looked round for Kuya but couldn’t find her.
She hid and hung around Yerrikee’s tree after
they had gone. There not many days after she
was spied by a shepherd.
He went to “capture” her, and was surprised
she did not make off but came straight for him as
if a white man was all she was waiting for, and
had closed on him in a sort of wolfish grapple be-
fore he knew what was up. He was a man, and
had his fists to use, but a gun slung round his
shoulders rather hindered him, and the widow,
was impetuous and fought in irregular ways with
her nails and teeth. It was over in no time, and
after laying him out she beat out his brains with
a log of wood.
Then she departed from the neighborhood, and
that was the last of the tribe on St. Kilda Hill.
Natives used to come along afterwards, and at
times they identified Yerrikee’s mark on the tree,
which stands now in a private garden with a look-
out on a fork of it, whence you can see all over the
Oneday in the ’9o’s an old lubrapilgrimaged from
one of the outlying settlements for the protection
of the aborigines, and could have been seen crawl-
ing through Melbourne city and afterwards on
through Albert Park.
She had an old shawl over her shoulder, and
hanging by a string round her neck was a metal
disc, on which was engraved :
KUYA KUYA,
LUBF.A
To Yekrikbe
(Kins Billy of the whites;,
Killed 1837.
Towards evening she was making her way
slowly up Prince’s-street, St. Kilda. At the top
she took a long breath and a look round, spying
the locality. .
She was in front of a large private garden.
Fairy lamps were lighted, and dreamy music
was in the air, and vehicles now and then briskly
drove in at one large gate and out at another.
Outside, the old lubra peered in between the
iron railings, and presently, unnoticed, slid
through one of the open gates and was lost m the
shadows. , ,
It was a pleasant night, and the lawns were
lively with loungers and strollers.
Of these, a couple were contentedly sauntering
to a sort of bower contrived against the trunk of a
huge gum-tree. The woman (for you could not
call such a magnificent creature a “ girl ”) stopped
at the arbor, and read over the entrance !
KING BILLY’S TREE
“ Why King Billy’s Tree! ”
“ Some native chief, I believe—long before your
time or mine—before the white man came.”
“ King Billy’s Tree,” she mused, and they sat
down on the arbor seat arranged out of the bulge
around the base that some large gums have.
“ We’re sitting on a king’s throne, Jack —won-
der if there was a queen ? ”
Hardly were the words out of her mouth when
some bushes parted and the aged lubra stood
gazing at them as they sat together on the old
seat.
Jack struck a match and stood up to have a look
at her. Large coal-black eyes with yellow whites
she had—which she kept fixed on the fair one.
Jack took up the medal that hung from her
neck and read the inscription.
Then he lit another match and he and his wife
read it together.
“Why Jack, you got this up for me.”
“ Good Lord, no ! ”
The old black, making guttural noises, went up
to the tree feeling the trunk with her fingers.
She stood on the seat and reached up as if
looking for something. Jack cast a flickering
light on the scene, holding his box as a permanency
and striking a match as required.
She found the old axe-marks, and said
“ Yerrikee ” excitedly.
“ King Billy’s coat of arms carved on the tree,”
said Jack ; really, the sign for Kuya Kuya that
had been cut deep generations ago by a strong-
young arm, not to come out in a hundred
years
The match went out suddenly, so did a bad
word as Jack put his finger in his mouth.
Then everybody in the place seemed to get
round the spot. The old gin was taken to the
house kitchen, and made much of. Among other
things she had two bottles of English ale, and was
set to recline in a large cane-lounge on a verandah,
and there was a sort of levee around her, in the
midst of whicli she dozed off to sleep.
She was accommodated for the night as a species
of curiosity, but in the morning she was gone. So
was a considerable quantity of liquor. The early
milkman reported having seen the last of the
royalty of Carrum, burdened with eight bottles of
beer, and vanishing in the direction of Gippsland-

AMONGST the citizens of Australia and in all parts of the world, BILE BEANS
FOR BILIOUSNESS have earned for themselves, by their unequalled excellence,
a reputation which places them to-day far in advance of any other similar prepara-
tion. This specific is not a carelessly-prepared nostrum ;on the contrary, it is the
result of long and thoughtful research on the part of a medical man, who, after years of
study, discovered a natural vegetable substance which, after further study, he succeeded in
compounding with other ingredients, so that its effect on the digestive organism of the human
system is identical with that of the natural bile created by the liver. Finding this, then, to
be the most natural catharic obtainable, the public rapidly accepted it in preference to all
others, and consequently the old methods of starving and the use of pepsin and bismuth
sank into insignificance and oblivion. Acting on the bowels in a gentle manner, Bile Beans
do not weaken or in any way distress the patient. This is a point in which they differ
materially from nearly all laxative remedies sold to-day, as the latter usually act in a violent
manner, weakening the patient and in many cases doing more harm than good. Bile Beans
have been aptly termed “a medicine for the people at a popular prioe.” Many wonder how
this remedy can have the great virtue that we claim for it, and still be sold at so reasonable a
price ; yet the solution is simple. Millions upon millions of Bile Beans are consumed an-
nually (a fact that in itself is positive proof that the world’s inhabitants know how to appre-
ciate a really genuine remedy). Owing to this immense annual sale we are enabled to pur-
chase the very best drugs in exceedingly large quantities, and thus get them at a great deal
lower price than if we bought only moderate quantities at a time. Then, again, Bile Beans
are manufactured in possibly the largest and best-equipped laboratories in the world. In
this establishment only the very best of latest improved machinery is used, and this fact also
greatly minimises the cost of production. Thus it will be seen that through the great con-
sumption the proprietors are enabled to sell this valuable remedy at a price that places it
within the reach of all, viz., 13£d per box.

PEARS
The Sisters.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Young Kate was wearing Jenny’s gown
(Because she liked herself in red)
When, hastening homeward from the town,
She met her handsome cousin Fred
And blushed a little as he said—
“ How very cold and proud we’ve grown !”
Kate blushed the newest shade in red.
He turned, but did not take her arm,
He spoke about the season’s gains,
And what was doing at the farm
Away beyond the Beelah plains,
Now soddened by the autumn rains.
Kate’s quiet answers had the charm
That womanhood’s first season gains.
Instead of “ teasing,” as of old,
She merely gave him “Yes” or “No,”
Though always in a tone that told
The warmth her manner did not show.
Their homeward steps were very slow
As Fred, now truculently bold,
Demanded one more “Yes ” or “No.”
She whispered in her lover’s ear,
Then wept a moment on his breast,
For surely, only yester year
He’d seemed to love her sister best.
Fred turned the question with a jest,
And pointing to the homestead near
He drew her closer to his breast.
In Kitty’s gown of sober grey
Her sister, standing at the gate,
Smiled on the man and turned away
To kiss the glowing cheek of Kate.
So sweet she looked, and so sedate.
Love’s heart was sad that autumn day
When Jenny closed the wicket gate.
vio.
EDMUND FISHER.
Hopetoun Splashes.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Hopetoun, Eabl, left many memories in the old
Victorian days. He was a mixed sort of man and
his antics came of mixed blood.
“ Lank an’ supple, swank and thin ;
Fine for gaun against the win’.”
He looked in the bush a good deal like a bush man.
The “gentle Johnston ” strain in him showed out
there. It came when Charles Hope, first Earl,
married in 1699 Henrietta, only daughter of
William Johnstone, of Annandale. How that
religious-paper-yarn about the mercantile ancestor
and the family ledger comes in, neither Burke
nor Debrett explains; but it is 200 years odd since
the grandson of Lawyer Hope of Hopetoun mar-
ried into the Devil’s Dick clan —mosstroopers all.
John Adrian, seventh in descent from that mar-
riage, had more of the desire than the capacity of
the Johnstones. He had lived too much amongst
buttons and lace, gold-sticks and wig-powder.
Still, there was a spark in him, and in the old days,
doubtless, he would probably have ridden home
from a foray with Devil’s Dick himself.
We started out-back one day from Mildura, the
wind coming out of a hot bottle, and the sun like
a live coal set in a red-hot basin. The Earl was
up (on the best horse, of course), scorning the
buggy, and Mac, the overseer, said, with a
chuckle, “Hopetoun ’ll get particular hell the
day.” We all got it hot, and about 4 p.m. Mao
came over to take, by force if necessary, another
man’s place in the buggy—“ Come out of that, one
o’ yer—l’m losing leather ; first time since I came
to the Mallee, five-and-twenty years ago.” The
boy-driver got down, and Mac lumbered up with
many a groan. Evening came, and His Ex., a
little languid, but game like his horse, came
over. “Well, Mac, and where is that particular
hell Hopetoun was to get to-day?” “It’s
here, mon, ” answered Mac, indicating the site of
the “ lost leather,” and giving a ghastly groan as
he rose from his seat.
His Ex. was better in a long ride than after the
hounds. He didn’t understand the pace at which
those who followed the old Starter’s pack had to
ride—at anything. It was quite as hot the day
at Merrylands as over the hurdles at Randwick,
and a four-foot-six post-and-railer just ahead.
“Come on. You are a duffer!” said the 17-
year-old daughter of the master.
“I know I am, but ” There was a bit of
picking-up to do the other side of the fence.
* * * *
“ I never saw him look really angry before, and
I wasn’t surprised,” said the political Jeames of
Melbourne. It was out west (Australia Felix—
not the N.S.W. West), and the Earl desired to
shake hands with Sandy Mac. Sandy was a hard
case; had worked out Glasgow and York before
trying Australia, but had a lot of good yarns. He
was selling a horse. “His Excellency is not
accustomed to wait,” said Jeames at length.
“Tell His Excellency I’d drink with a convicted
felon, but I wouldn’t lose a deal for a duke.” It
was a hard saying to be overheard.
A Victorian Governor always looks his best
when his team swings round from the carriage-
enclosure to the grand-stand at Flemington.
Loch, handsome as an old Assyrian king, used to
tool them round himself. The Hopetoun postil-
lions made a more accurate curve, and sat like
wooden images while the band played and the
hats lifted. “ Ay,” said a looker-on who had no
respect for dignities; “they do it well; but if one
of them girls was in pants she’d put a better head on
it.” The “girls” were the Hopetoun sisters, visit-
ing Australia that year. No man looking at them
could doubt that no little of the gentle Johnstone
blood had come down on the distaff-side. While
his lordship was always more than a little anaemic,
the honorable misses were robust, and free as ever
was Madge or Janet with milk-pail hoisted, kilts
in a byre knot, and Jock hanging round for the
forenicht. A good deal of Toorak was aghast
when one of them shook “Old Jack” (Carbine)by
the forelock and t’other hit him a sounding smack
on the flank, with a “You’re a brick, old man ! ”
The Countess was a little overshadowing. A
good rider and straight shot, she was not much in
line with Toorak, and was pretty fond of a “ lone
hand ” in Australia and elsewhere. “ A Demolin,
you know,” Toorak used to say. And while
Madame Woolpack talked of the blue-black Irish
hair and Irish eyes, and of descent from kings of
old, with a mixture of the blood of Don Diavolo,
wrecked in the homeward scurry of the Great
Armada and entertained of the friendly Celts—
Miss De Bags had other views. Demolin (De
Moleyn) was unquestionably Norman. It savored
of Battle Abbey, Acre, Damascus, Tancred and
Saladim If they had only looked up Burke !
Burke is cruel on De Moleyn. “Ventry, Baron
(Sir Dayrolles Blakeney Eveleigh De Moleyn) ”
that is the title to-day. But the beginning of it
all was Frederick William Mullins, who in 1666
took up> a place in Co. Kerry, and who, doubtless,
in his time was called Bill Mullins. Mullins-De
Moleyns. It’s cleverer than Smith Smythed !
F.M.
That Bandy-Legged Parson.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Jones, the manager, Evans and I were seated on
the homestead verandah, smoking and wondering
when rain was going to fall (we were always won-
dering that), when a horseman appeared over the
range. Nobody spoke until he drew rein close to
the front gate, when Jones broke the silence.
“Look here, you chaps,” he said, “ that makes
the fifth parson this week, and I’m just about
tired of ’em. Don’t mind them so much, only they
always have horses, and more horses mean less
grass, and—well, I’m going to put a stop to it,
that’s all.”
We all went out to welcome (?) the stranger.
He was a typical sky-pilot, with long bandy legs,
and his horse was a typical sky-pilot’s “ crock. ”
They were both lean, lank, and hungry-looking,
and neither seemed capable of much further
effort.
He introduced himself as Mr. Chadwick, and,
with a far-away look in his eyes, begged of us to
see to his er—horse.
I led it away, feeling sorry for both of them,
while Jones took the stranger inside, and showed
him a room.
After tea we again adjourned to the verandah,
and I was considerably surprised at Jones’s cor-
diality toward Mr. Chadwick. The latter told us
tales of slum work in London, and we in return
spun yarns about cattle and brumbies till he began
to look incredulous. Then we went to bed.
. I rose at daylight as usual, and found the parson
sitting on the cap of the stockyard gate, smoking.
“Always liked a pipe before breakfast,” he said.
Breakfast over, we hurried out to the yard to
catch our horses. Mr. Chadwick informed me
that he was going to ride a station horse on to
TJndulla, and leave his old grey “to pick up a
bit.” I was wondering what horse the boss had
lent him, when Evans ran one into a small yard
and put the parson’s saddle on it. To say I was
horrified is to put it mildly, as the horse in
question was the worst buck-jumper on the place
—an outlaw in fact. He was quiet enougli to
saddle, and mount; after that he was a lightning
contortionist.
I said nothing. Silence is golden.
Jones’s face was as devoid of expression as a
Chinaman’s as he said : “I hope your old grey is
fat by the time you come back, Mr. Chadwick.”
The parson hoped so too, and, having led the
horse outside the yard, proceeded to mount.
Evans had lent him my new spurs. They evi-
dently wanted to do the job properly. Mr. Chad-
wick swung himself up behind that big valise
with an easy grace that told of long practice.
Then followed a wondrous gymnastic display.
A clatter of hoofs, and a dark whirling mass in a
cloud of dust was about all we saw. The “ thud”
we expected did not come.
Mr. Chadwick was flogging old “Fireworks,”
as we called him, with his hat, and he stuck the
spurs in hard as he headed him for the gate over
the ridge. I looked at the other two.
“Well, I’m damned ! ” they said in chorus.
• • ■ •
In three weeks he was back. Jones and Evans
saw him coming, and camped-out that night.
Mr. Chadwick seemed disappointed when I told
him that they would not be back for two or three
days.
“Pity,” he said, smiling grimly; “I wanted to
see Mr. Jones particularly.” I did not blame
him.
He went next morning, after having expressed
himself as pleased with the improved condition of
his own. horse. “ Fireworks ” was quite broken-
down from hard riding, and now it is not safe to
mention a parson in Jones’s hearing.
Q.
S. C. O. JESSOP.
Constance Clyde describes a Labor Farm :
Many attempts have been made to provide work for the
workless, and one very good attempt is to be found at
Little Bay (Sydney) in the State Labor Farm. This is a
cheerful building, guarded by a light auburn dog, and
surrounded by a vegetable garden that is a credit to these
droughty times. It is a harbor into which the derelict
working man is cast, and where he can gather up his
courage anew till he.is ready to battle with the sea of life
once more. That resting time is three months, and may
be prolonged; no very strict rule is kept as to the fre-
quency of any man’s visits. The dietary scale is good,
and the State Farm laborer has hot water laid on, so that
he can luxuriate in warm baths with an ease that none of
his brethren in Sydney know of. He also possesses a
library, not too well stocked, and he can have his own
special sky-pilot upon Sunday—if the pilot is available,
but so far no soul-saver of any brand whatever has chosen
to come, probably owing to the fact that the necessarily
small wages earned by the congregation would not per-
mit of much addition to the collection-plate. Some fur-
ther improvements are promised for winter, including
lockers for the convenience of such as have anything to
lock.
The State Labor Farm is so far a boon to the com*
munity that it is a satisfactory answer to such Domain
gentry as perambulate this city with their wardrobe in a
state of disrepair, to polish handles that don’t need polish-
ing and do other light, genteel work that will not exercise
the muscles over much. It is also a satisfactory
reply to the other gentlemen whose energy is expended
in getting their wives washing to do, and who are
apt to lose faith in Providence when the missis is incapa-
citated by rheumatism. Of course, it offers no advantage
to the married workman with a penchant for supporting
his family, because he isn’t allowed to bring his household
into the severely bachelor establishment at Little Bay,
and he naturally does not make enough to support it out-
side. These remarks are not made in disparagement of the
State Labor Farm, but only to express a hope that it is but
thenucleus of somethingbroader and better—a State Labor
Village, growing into a State Labor City, where families
of our workers may go, not, of course, to escape toil, but
to escape the greater drudgery of looking for drudgery.
Such, anyway, seems to be the hope of the large gentle-
man, with ideas to match, whose enthusiasm is responsible
for the creation of this refuge. Before anything further
can be done, however, the community must get rid of the
idea that the moral fibre of a working-man will be
weakened by a system that encourages him to look to the
State “ for leave to toil,” and that such a system would
destroy his power of initiative. The man who has enough
initiative to work from eight to five every day, and more,
for all his adult life, at a wage that wouldn’t give him a
month’s ■ holiday in a year, has all the initiative
that is expected of him. The power of initia-
tive may be demanded too much from our
workers. In some periods of history, they have shown it
by upsetting things in general and by making up long
arrears of working without wages by taking wages with-
out working. The French revolutionists showed some of
this power when they used up the aristocrats’ heads as
adornments for Labor processions, but it is not on record
that this burst of originality was appreciated by the
upper classes.
